The Definition and Permissibility of Agency (Al-Wakalah)
Definition and proof of permissibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of wakalah and identify the Quranic and Sunnah proofs for its permissibility.

Core Idea
Agency (wakalah) is permissible by Quran, Sunnah, and consensus.

This passage opens the Book of Agency by defining wakalah and establishing its legal permissibility. The commentator connects it to the previous book on testimony, then gives linguistic analysis and textual proofs from the Quran and Sunnah to show that appointing an agent is a lawful practice in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of wakalah
Examine the linguistic derivation: wakalah is from tawkil, meaning delegation. Note that al-wakil can mean both the one delegated (passive) and the protector (active).
2
Identify the hukm of agency
The matn states 'jaza al-tawkil' – agency is permissible (ja'iz). This is the default ruling; no obligation or prohibition.
3
Examine the transmitted proofs (dalil naqli)
The author cites the Quranic story of the Companions of the Cave (18:19) where they sent one with silver, which is a form of agency. He also cites the hadith of Hakim ibn Hizam whom the Prophet delegated to buy a sacrificial animal.
4
Understand the shar' man qablana principle
The law of those before us is binding if related without criticism and not abrogated. The Quranic story is accepted as proof because it was narrated without rejection and no abrogation is known.
5
Confirm consensus (ijma')
The passage mentions that consensus has been formed on the permissibility of agency, reinforcing the textual evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم
كتاب الوكالة
جاز التوكيل
كتاب الوكالة
(جاز التوكيل ""
[٢]
(1)
[١] قوله : كتاب الوكالة ؛ لما فرغ المصنف الله له عن بيان أحكام الشهادة بأنواعها وما يتبعها من الرجوع عنها ، شرع في بيان أحكام الوكالة ؛ لمناسبة أنَّ كلاً من الشاهد والوكيل ساع في تحصيل مرادِ غيره الموكل والمدعي معتمداً على كل منهما، والوكالة : بفتح الواو وكسرها اسم من التوكيل كما في «الصحاح»، وغيره. يقال : وكلتُ الأمر إليه وكلا من باب وَعَدَ ووُكولا : فوَّضتُ إليه واكتفيتُ به ، والوكيل : فعيل بمعنى مفعول ؛ لأنه موكول إليه، ويكون بمعنى فاعل، إذا كان بمعني الحافظ، ومنه : حَسْبُنَا اللَّهُ وَنِعْمَ الْوَكِيلُ ) ، والجمع وكلاء، ووكلته توكيلاً فتوكل، وتوكل على الله : أي اعتمد عليه ووثق به ووكل عليه في أمره كذلك ، والاسم التكلان. كذا في «المصباح» (۳). [٢] قوله : جاز التوكيل ؛ وجوازه ثابت بالنقل والعقل : أما النقلُ : فالكتاب

In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. The Book of Agency. (Agency) is permissible. The Book of Agency. (Agency is permissible) [2] (1) [1] His statement: 'The Book of Agency'; when the author - may Allah have mercy on him - finished explaining the rulings of testimony, its types, and what follows from it regarding retraction, he began explaining the rulings of agency, due to the appropriateness that each of the witness and the agent are engaged in achieving the desire of another - the principal and the claimant - relying on each of them. And 'al-wakalah' - with fath or kasr of the waw - is a noun derived from 'al-tawkil' as in 'al-Sihah' and others. It is said: Wakaltu al-amra ilayhi (I delegated the matter to him) as a verb from the pattern of wa'ada and wukulan: I entrusted it to him and sufficed myself with him. And 'al-wakil' is on the pattern of fa'il meaning maf'ul, because he is the one to whom the matter is delegated, and it can also mean fa'il when meaning 'protector', as in: 'Sufficient for us is Allah, and [He is] the best Disposer of affairs' (Quran 3:173), and the plural is wukala'. And wakkaltuhu tawkilan so he tawakkala, and tawakkala 'ala Allah: he relied upon Him and trusted in Him and delegated his affairs to Him similarly. And the noun is al-tuklan. This is as in 'al-Misbah' (3). [2] His statement: 'Agency is permissible'; its permissibility is established by transmitted evidence and reason. As for transmitted evidence: the Book and the Sunnah. As for the Book: His statement - exalted - narrating about the Companions of the Cave: 'So send one of you with this silver coin of yours to the city' (Quran 18:19), and their sending was through the means of agency. And the law of those before us is a law for us when Allah and His Messenger relate it without disapproval, and no abrogation is apparent. As for the Sunnah: what Abu Dawud narrated in his 'Sunan' in the 'Book of Sales': 'He (the Prophet) gave a dinar to Hakim ibn Hizam to buy a sacrificial animal for him. He bought it for a dinar and sold it for two dinars, then returned and bought a sacrificial animal for a dinar. The Messenger of Allah then prayed for him that his trade be blessed.' And this has been mentioned previously, and consensus has been formed upon it.
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Agency (Kitab al-Wakalah) from the commentary of 'Umdat al-Riayah on Sharh al-Wiqayah. The commentator first explains why the author placed the Book of Agency after the Book of Testimony: both the witness and the agent act on behalf of another person, making them conceptually connected. He then provides a linguistic definition of wakalah, noting that it can be pronounced with a fatha or kasra on the waw, and that it derives from tawkil, meaning delegation. The active participle wakil can mean the one delegated (passive sense) or the protector (active sense), as in the Quranic verse 'Hasbuna Allahu wa ni'ma al-wakil.' The commentator then addresses the matn's statement 'jaza al-tawkil' (agency is permissible) by establishing its permissibility through both transmitted (naqli) and rational ('aqli) evidence. The transmitted evidence is drawn from the Quran (the story of the Companions of the Cave sending one of them to the city with money, which constitutes agency) and from the Sunnah (the hadith of Hakim ibn Hizam where the Prophet delegated him to buy a sacrificial animal). The commentator notes that the Quranic story is sharia of those before us (shar' man qablana), which is binding upon us as long as it is not abrogated. He also mentions that consensus (ijma') has been established on the permissibility of agency.

Key Terms
الوكالة (al-wakalah): A noun derived from tawkil, meaning delegation of one's affairs to another and being sufficed by him.
التوكيل (al-tawkil): The act of appointing someone as an agent (wakil).
الوكيل (al-wakil): The person who is delegated to act on behalf of another; can also mean protector or guardian.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which Quranic story is cited as evidence for the permissibility of agency (wakalah)?
A) The story of Prophet Yusuf (Joseph)
B) The story of the Companions of the Cave
C) The story of Prophet Musa (Moses) and Khidr
D) The story of the Battle of Badr

Key Takeaway
Agency is a permissible legal institution in the Hanafi school, proven by Quran, Sunnah, and consensus.

Permissibility of Agency (Wakalah) with Proofs
Opening of Wakalah with proofs from Quran, Sunnah, Ijma

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on agency and identify the Quranic and Sunnah proofs that establish its permissibility.

Core Idea
Agency is permissible in Hanafi fiqh, proven by Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma.

This lesson opens the Book of Agency (Wakalah) in the commentary. The author establishes the permissibility of agency using transmitted proofs from the Quran and Sunnah, as well as rational reasoning. Understanding these proofs is essential for grasping the foundation of representation in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal ruling
What is the ruling (hukm) on agency according to the text? Determine the hukm level (e.g., mubah, wajib).
The text explicitly says 'جاز التوكيل' – agency is permissible (ja'iz).
2
Classify the proofs
The author mentions two categories: naqli (transmitted) and 'aqli (rational). List the naqli proofs.
Naqli proofs are Quran and Sunnah; Ijma is also cited.
3
Examine the Quranic proof
Identify the verse and explain how it implies agency.
Surah al-Kahf 18:19: 'So send one of you with this silver coin to the city.' The command to send implies delegation.
4
Examine the Sunnah proof
Identify the hadith and its relevance.
Hadith of Hakim ibn Hizam: The Prophet gave him a dinar to buy a sacrificial animal, demonstrating agency.
5
Note the principle of shar' man qablana
Why is the story of previous prophets a valid proof?
Hanafis accept laws before us when related without criticism and no abrogation is known.

In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. The Book of Agency. Agency is permissible. [2] (1) [1] His saying: 'The Book of Agency' – when the author, may Allah have mercy on him, finished explaining the rulings of testimony with its types and what follows it of retraction, he began explaining the rulings of agency due to the similarity that both the witness and the agent strive to achieve the desire of another (the principal and the claimant), relying on each of them. Al-Wakalah (with opening of the waw and kasra) is the noun form of al-tawkil as in al-Sihah and others. It is said: wakaltu al-amra ilayhi waklan (from the pattern of wa'ada and wukulan), meaning I delegated it to him and sufficed with him. Al-Wakil is fa'il in the sense of maf'ul because he is delegated to; it also comes in the sense of fa'il when meaning the protector, as in 'Sufficient for us is Allah and He is the best Wakil' (Quran 3:173). The plural is wukala'. Wakaltuhu tawkilan so he tawakkala. Tawakkala 'ala Allah means he relied and trusted in Him, and delegated his affair to Him. The noun is al-tuklan. Thus in al-Misbah (3). [2] His saying: 'Agency is permissible' – its permissibility is established by revelation and reason. As for revelation: it is the Book and the Sunnah. As for the Book: the statement of Allah, the Exalted, narrating about the companions of the cave: 'So send one of you with this silver coin of yours to the city' (Quran 18:19). Their sending was by way of agency, and the laws of those before us are a law for us when Allah and His Messenger relate it without objection, and no abrogation is apparent. As for the Sunnah: what Abu Dawud narrated in his Sunan in the Book of Sales: 'He gave a dinar to Hakim ibn Hizam to buy a sacrificial animal for him. He bought one for a dinar and sold it for two dinars, then returned and bought a sacrificial animal for a dinar and came back to the Messenger of Allah, so he prayed for his trade to be blessed.' It has been mentioned before, and consensus has been formed upon it.
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Agency (Wakalah) in the commentary. The author explains why the chapter on agency follows testimony, noting that both the witness and the agent act on behalf of another. He then provides a linguistic definition of agency, quoting classical lexicons: 'wakalah' is a noun from 'tawkil', meaning delegation. The term 'wakil' is used in the Quran for Allah as the Protector. The core legal ruling is that agency is permissible (ja'iz). The proof is twofold: transmitted (naqli) and rational ('aqli). The transmitted proof comes from the Quran (the story of the People of the Cave sending one with silver to town, which was an act of agency) and the Sunnah (the hadith of Hakim ibn Hizam, where the Prophet delegated him to buy a sacrificial animal). The author states that the laws of previous prophets are applicable to us when narrated without rejection and no abrogation is found. Additionally, consensus (ijma') is cited. This establishes the permissibility of agency definitively.

Key Terms
الوكالة (al-wakalah): The noun form of tawkil, meaning delegation of an affair to another and being content with him.
التوكيل (al-tawkil): The act of delegating, making someone an agent.
الوكيل (al-wakil): The agent, one to whom the task is delegated; can also mean protector as in Quran.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The story of the People of the Cave sending one with silver to the city
C) The hadith of Hakim ibn Hizam buying a sacrificial animal
D) The consensus of the companions

Key Takeaway
Agency is permissible in Hanafi fiqh, supported by Quranic story of the People of the Cave, the Sunnah of the Prophet, and consensus.

Definition of Agency and its Condition: An Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Defines agency and explains its condition with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of agency (wakalah) and identify the two different conditions for its validity according to Abu Hanifa and the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad).

Core Idea
Agency is delegation of a permissible act, but Hanafis disagree whether the principal must own the act or the agent must be able to own it.

This lesson explores the definition of agency (wakalah) as given by the Hanafi jurists and the crucial condition for its validity. The source text explains that agency is the delegation of disposing to another, but an intra-Hanafi dispute arises: does the principal need to own the right to the act, or is it sufficient that the agent can own it? We will examine both positions and their practical consequences, especially in cross-faith transactions.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi definition of agency
Identify the technical definition from the text: 'It is the delegation of disposing (tafwid al-tasrif) to someone else.' Note that it excludes wasiyyah (will) because agency is effective during the principal's lifetime.
The Arabic phrase 'تفويض التصرف' is the core definition.
2
Identify the condition for validity
The text states a condition: 'that the principal owns it' (an yamlikahu al-muwakkil). Understand that this refers to the right to perform the act being delegated. But the pronoun 'it' (al-tasrif) is ambiguous: does it mean absolute disposing or the specific disposed act?
This ambiguity leads to the ikhtilaf.
3
Recognize the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
According to the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the condition is that the principal must own the specific act delegated. According to Abu Hanifa, the condition is that the agent must be able to own the act (i.e., have legal capacity for it), even if the principal does not own it.
The example of a Muslim appointing a dhimmī to sell wine illustrates the difference.
4
Apply the ikhtilaf to a concrete scenario
If a Muslim principal appoints a dhimmī agent to sell wine: Abu Hanifa rules valid because the agent can own the act of selling (his capacity is recognized regardless of the item's legality). The two disciples rule invalid because the principal does not own the right to sell wine (as it is unlawful for him).
This shows the practical outcome of the theoretical difference.
5
Understand the rational basis for agency
The text provides a rational (aqlī) justification: people may be unable to act personally due to various reasons (lack of expertise, busyness, illness, old age, dignity). Hence agency is permitted as a necessity.
This is a maslahah-based argument, not a textual dalil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية
وهو تفويض التصرُّف إلى غيره، وشرطه أن يملكه الموكَّلُ وهو تفويض التصرف" إلى غيره ، وشرطه ) أن يملكه الموكل)، الضمير المنصوب يرجعُ إلى التّصرُّف، والظاهرُ أنَّ المراد مطلق التَّصرُّف ، فإن عبارة «الهداية» هكذا : ومن شرط الوكالة أن يكونَ الموكَّلُ من يملِكُ التَّصْرُّف بأن يكونَ حُرِّاً بالغاً ، أو مأذوناً). وإن أُريد بالتَّصرُّف التَّصرُّف الذي وُكِّلَ به لا مطلق التصرُّف يكونُ قولهما لا قول أبي حنيفة لله ، فإنَّ المسلم إذا وَكُلَ الدَّمي ببيع الخمر، يجوز عنده
لا عندهما
وأما العقل : فإنَّ الإنسان قد يعجز عن المباشرة بنفسه لقلة هدايته في التصرفات ، أو لكثرة اشتغاله ، أو لمرضه، أو لضعفه، أو لكونه شيخاً فانياً، أو لكونه ذا وجاهة لا يتولى الأمور بنفسه، فاقتضى ذلك جواز الوكالة.
[1] قوله : وهو تفويض التصرف ... الخ ؛ أي إقامة غيره مقام نفسه في التصرُّف الذي يمكن أن يباشره بنفسه، وخرج بهذا القيد الإيصاء، فإنّه أيضاً تفويض التصرف إلى غيره، لكن إتمامه إنما هو بموت الموصي.
وحينئذ لا يمكنه أن يباشر التصرُّف بنفسه، لكن يشكل هذا بتوكيل المسلم الذمي ببيع الخمر، والجواب أنَّ المراد إمكانُ المباشرة، وهو مطلق الإمكان، سواء كان على وجه الشرع أو لا، والمراد بالتصرُّف التصرُّف المعلوم، حتى لو لم يكن معلوماً بأن قال : وكلتك بمالى، ثبت به، أو في تصرُّفاتِ الوكيل وهو الحفظ فقط ؛ ولهذا أورد التصرُّف معرفاً. كذا في «شرح النُّقاية» للبرْجَنْدِي، وغيرِه. [٢] قوله : وشرطه ...الخ ؛ أي شرط تفويض التصرُّف أن يملك الموكل التصرُّفَ الذي فوَّضَ إلى الوكيل، إذ هو تملك ذلك من الموكل، فلا بُدَّ أن يكون مالكاً له ؛ ليصح التمليك منه ، ، قيل : هذا عندهما ، وعند أبي حنيفة له فالشرط أن يكون التوكيلُ حاصلاً بما يملكه الوكيل، سواء كان الموكل مالكاً له أو لا ، حتى يجوز عنده توكيلُ المسلم الذمي بشراء الخمر. فعلى هذا يكون الضمير في يملكه راجعاً إلى مطلق التصرف، فيخرج عن ذلك المجنون والصبي الذي لا يعقل ، وقيل : هذا على قول الكل، فإنَّ المراد هو أن يكون
The essence of the final for the support of care: It is the delegation of disposing to someone else, and its condition is that the principal owns it. It is the delegation of disposing to someone else, and its condition: that the principal owns it. The object pronoun refers to the disposing, and apparently what is meant is absolute disposing. So the expression of al-Hidayah is: 'And from the conditions of agency is that the principal be one who owns the disposing, i.e., free, adult, or permitted.' But if by disposing is meant the specific act delegated, not absolute disposing, then it is their view (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), not the view of Abu Hanifa. For if a Muslim appoints a dhimmī agent to sell wine, it is valid according to him, not according to them. As for reason: a person may be unable to act directly due to lack of guidance in transactions, or too much occupation, or illness, or weakness, or being an elderly man, or having dignity such that he does not handle affairs himself, so that necessitates the permissibility of agency. [1] His saying: 'It is the delegation of disposing...' – i.e., putting someone else in his place regarding the disposing that he can perform himself. By this restriction, wasiyyah (will) is excluded, because it is also a delegation of disposing to someone else, but its completion is only by the death of the testator. Then he cannot perform the disposing himself. But this is problematic regarding a Muslim appointing a dhimmī as agent to sell wine. The answer is that what is meant is the possibility of direct performance, which is absolute possibility, whether legally or not. And what is meant by disposing is known (ma'lum) disposing, so even if it is unknown, like saying 'I appoint you as agent over my wealth,' it is established thereby, or concerning the agent's dispositions which are only preservation; for this reason disposing is stated as definite. Thus in Sharh al-Niqaya of al-Barjandi and others. [2] His saying: 'And its condition...' – i.e., the condition of delegating disposing is that the principal owns the disposing that he delegated to the agent, because it is a transfer of ownership from the principal, so he must be owner of it for the transfer to be valid. It is said: this is according to them (the two disciples), and according to Abu Hanifa, the condition is that the agency occurs regarding what the agent can own, whether the principal owns it or not, so that according to him it is valid for a Muslim to appoint a dhimmī to buy wine. Accordingly, the pronoun in 'yamlikuhu' refers to absolute disposing, thus excluding the insane and a child who does not understand. And it is said: this is according to everyone, for the meaning is that it is...
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage defines agency (wakalah) as 'delegation of disposing (tasrif) to someone else.' The condition is stated: the principal must own the right to the disposing he delegates. The commentator explains the definition by noting that it excludes wasiyyah (will), which also involves delegation but only becomes effective after death. He then addresses an objection: if a Muslim appoints a dhimmī to sell wine, according to Abu Hanifa it is valid because the agent has the capacity to own the act (even though the principal may not legally own wine), while the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider it invalid because the principal must own the right to sell wine (which he does not, as it is unlawful). This is a key intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. The passage also gives a rational proof for the permissibility of agency in general, based on human need. The condition is further elaborated: the principal must be someone who can legally perform the act himself or has permission (adult, free, sane). The commentator resolves the pronoun reference in the condition: if 'yamlikuhu' refers to absolute disposing, it excludes the insane and child; if it refers to the specific act, it leads to the ikhtilaf mentioned.

Key Terms
تفويض التصرف (tafwid al-tasrif): Delegation of disposing: putting another in one's place regarding a legal act that one could perform personally.
الموكل (al-muwakkil): The principal; the one who delegates the act to the agent.
الذمي (al-dhimmī): A non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state, whose legal capacity is recognized in certain transactions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), what is the condition for a valid agency (wakalah)?
A) The agent must be able to own the act delegated.
B) The principal must be able to perform the act himself at the time of delegation.
C) The principal must own the right to the act being delegated.
D) The act must be permissible (mubah) in itself.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of agency is delegation of a permissible act, but the condition for validity differs between Abu Hanifa (agent's capacity) and the two disciples (principal's ownership), affecting cross-faith transactions.

Definition and Conditions of Agency (Wakalah)
Defines agency and states its conditions, with an intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of agency, identify its conditions, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the principal's ownership of the disposal.

Core Idea
Agency is delegation of disposal; the principal must own the disposal, and the agent must understand and intend the act.

Agency (wakalah) is a fundamental contract in Islamic law allowing delegation. This passage defines agency and lays out its essential conditions, including an important disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples over whether the principal must own the specific delegated act. We'll explore the definition, conditions, and the reasoning behind the relied-upon view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
The passage defines agency as 'delegation of disposal to another'. Note that it is distinct from bequest (wasiyyah), which only becomes effective after death.
The definition excludes cases where the principal cannot personally perform the act.
2
Determine the conditions for validity
List the conditions: the principal must be legally capable (free, adult, or authorized), the agent must be sane and intentional, and the delegated act must be known and permissible.
The agent must understand the nature of the contract (e.g., sale transfers ownership) and intend it.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Compare Abu Hanifa's view (the principal need not own the specific disposal; possibility of direct action suffices) with the Sahibayn's view (the principal must own the specific disposal).
The difference affects whether a Muslim can appoint a dhimmi to sell wine.
4
Understand the wisdom (hikmah)
Agency is permitted because people may be unable to act directly due to illness, busyness, or lack of expertise.
This is the basis for the permissibility (mubah) of agency.
5
Synthesize the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon view is that of the Sahibayn, i.e., the principal must own the specific delegated disposal. This is the condition adopted in fatwa.
Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah supports this view.

It is delegation of disposal to another. Its condition is that the principal should own that disposal. The pronoun refers to disposal, and apparently what is meant is absolute disposal. The wording of al-Hidayah is: Among the conditions of agency is that the principal be one who owns the disposal, i.e., free and adult or authorized. If what is meant by disposal is the specific disposal delegated, not absolute disposal, then it would be the opinion of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), not Abu Hanifa. For if a Muslim appoints a dhimmi to sell wine, it is valid according to him, not according to them. As for reason: a person may be incapable of direct action due to lack of guidance in transactions, or due to many engagements, illness, weakness, being an old man, or being a person of status who does not handle matters himself, so this necessitates the permissibility of agency. [1] His statement: 'It is delegation of disposal etc.' means appointing another in his place in a disposal that he could personally undertake. This excludes bequest, for it is also delegation of disposal to another, but its completion is only upon the death of the testator. Then he cannot personally undertake the disposal. But this is problematic regarding a Muslim appointing a dhimmi to sell wine. The answer is that what is meant is the possibility of direct action, absolute possibility, whether lawful or not. What is meant by disposal is the known disposal, such that if it is not known, e.g., saying 'I appoint you as my agent over my property,' it is established. Or it refers to the agent's disposals, which is only preservation. So disposal is defined. [2] His statement: 'And its condition etc.' means the condition of delegating disposal is that the principal owns the disposal that he delegated to the agent, for it is ownership of that from the principal, so he must be its owner for the transfer to be valid. It is said: This is according to them; according to Abu Hanifa, the condition is that the agency occurs through what the agent owns, whether the principal owns it or not, such that he permits the agent to buy wine. Thus, the pronoun in 'owns' refers to absolute disposal, excluding the insane and the minor who does not understand. It is said: This is according to all, for what is meant is that he be... And that the agent should understand it and intend it: i.e., he understands that sale transfers ownership, purchase brings ownership, and he knows slight from excessive fraud, and intends the contract, such that if he acts jokingly, it does not occur on command. So agency of a free, adult, or authorized person is valid, who owns that disposal, considering the essence of disposal, even if prevented by an obstacle. Sale of wine is valid from a Muslim in essence, but is prevented due to the prohibition. [1] His statement: 'i.e., he understands etc.' means that among the conditions of agency is that the agent should know that disposal, e.g., he knows in sale and purchase that sale transfers ownership, purchase brings ownership, and he knows slight from excessive fraud, and intends that disposal, i.e., not jokingly, but intends by performing the cause the establishment of the ruling. So if the agent is a minor who does not understand, or insane, or joking, it does not occur on command and becomes void. The secret is that the agent stands in place of the principal in expression, so he must be capable of expression, which is through rationality. [2] His statement: 'So valid etc.' the reason for validity is that the principal owns the disposal and the agent is capable of expression. It is necessary to restrict 'free' to 'sane' to exclude the insane. It says: Agency of an insane and a minor who does not understand is absolutely invalid. A discerning minor is invalid for harmful disposals like divorce, manumission, gift, charity, but his agency for beneficial acts is valid without permission.
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage is a definition and condition exposition from a Hanafi fiqh work on agency (وكالة). The author begins by defining agency as 'delegation of disposal to another' and states its condition: the principal must own that disposal. The commentary clarifies that 'owning the disposal' means the principal must be legally capable of performing the act himself. The opinion of Imam al-Hidayah is cited: the principal must be free, adult, or authorized (مأذون). A key intra-Hanafi difference is discussed: if the disposal is specific (e.g., selling wine), Abu Hanifa holds that a Muslim can appoint a dhimmi to sell wine, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree. The reason is that for Abu Hanifa, the condition refers to the agent's ownership, not the principal's. The passage then gives the wisdom behind agency – human incapacity due to various reasons. The agent must also be sane and intentional: he must understand the nature of the contract (e.g., that sale transfers ownership) and intend the contract. A minor who does not understand, an insane person, or someone joking cannot be valid agents. The validity of agency requires the principal to own the disposal and the agent to be capable of expression. A discerning minor can be appointed for beneficial acts but not harmful ones like divorce or gift.

Key Terms
وكالة (wakālah): Delegation of disposal to another, i.e., appointing someone to act on one's behalf.
تفويض التصرف (tafwīḍ al-taṣarruf): Granting authority to another to perform a legal act on one's behalf.
الموكل (al-muwakkil): The principal who delegates authority to the agent.
الوكيل (al-wakīl): The agent who is appointed to act on behalf of the principal.
شرط (sharṭ): A condition that must be present for the validity of agency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The principal must be Muslim.
B) The principal must own the specific disposal legally.
C) The principal must be free, adult, or authorized.
D) The principal must appoint only a Muslim agent.

Key Takeaway
Agency requires a capable principal and a sane, intentional agent, with the relied-upon view that the principal must own the specific delegated disposal.

Conditions of the Agent and Valid Appointment
Commentary on conditions of agent and permissibility of appointing various persons

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the essential conditions for a valid agent (wakil) in Hanafi law and identify which persons may be appointed as agents.

Core Idea
A valid agent must understand the transaction and intend it; even a discerning minor or slave can be appointed for beneficial acts.

This passage from a commentary on agency (wakalah) lays out the fundamental conditions that an agent must satisfy for the appointment to be valid. It also clarifies who can serve as an agent—free adults, but also minors and slaves who have been permitted to engage in trade. Understanding these rules is essential for correctly structuring agency relationships in Islamic finance, commerce, and daily transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two essential conditions of the agent
Read the opening line: 'And the agent understands it and intends it'. Extract the two conditions: the agent must have understanding of the transaction (e.g., know that sale transfers ownership) and must intend the contract.
The text emphasizes 'understanding' (al-'aql) and 'intention' (al-qasd).
2
Distinguish intention as a condition for agency vs. for the agent's sale
Note the commentator's remark from al-Munyah: intention is a condition for the validity of the agent's sale (to avoid jesting), but not a condition for the validity of the agency contract itself. This distinction is critical.
The agency contract can be valid even if the agent later acts without intention; the condition applies when the agent executes the transaction.
3
Recognize who may be appointed as agent
The text states that it is valid to appoint a free adult or a 'ma'dhun' (permitted minor/slave) as agent. A 'ma'dhun' is a discerning minor or slave who has received permission from guardian or master to engage in trade.
The passage also discusses that a non-discerning minor, insane person, or restricted minor/slave cannot be appointed.
4
Analyze the phrasing issue: 'like them' vs. 'each of them'
The author notes that the phrase 'like them' (مثلهما) only covers appointing a free adult by a free adult and a ma'dhun by a ma'dhun. Saying 'each of them' would include all four combinations (free adult appointing ma'dhun, etc.), which is also valid.
This highlights the precision expected in legal drafting.
5
Understand the underlying rationale
The agent stands in place of the principal in expression (qiyas al-na'ib 'an al-asl). Therefore, the agent must have the capacity of expression ('ahliyyat al-'ibarah), which requires sanity and understanding—not necessarily full legal capacity like majority or freedom.
This rational explains why a discerning minor or slave can be an agent for beneficial acts.

أصل الحريَّة في ذلك ؛ لأنَّ صحة العبارة بكونه آدمياً. لا يقال : إنَّ الصبي العاقل المحجور والعبد المحجور لـو كـانـا مـن أهـل التصرف فينبغي أن يصح منهما التزام العهدة، وليس كذلك ؛ لأنـا نـقـول : التزام العهدة مـن الصبي لا يجوز ؛ لأنَّ أهليَّته قاصرة ؛ لعدم بلوغه، ومن العبد بحق سيده ؛ لئلا يلزم الضرر به، ولا تعدَّر التزامُ الضّرر بهما، تعلّق بأقرب الناس إليهما، وهو الموكل الذي انتفع بهذا التصرف.
And the agent understands it and intends it, so it is valid to appoint a free adult or a permitted one like them. (And the agent understands it and intends it): i.e., he understands that sale removes ownership and purchase brings it, and he knows slight fraud from excessive, and he intends the contract such that if he acts jestingly, it does not occur on behalf of the principal. So it is valid to appoint a free adult or a permitted one like them, possessing that disposition, with regard to the origin of the disposition, even if it is prevented by an impediment, like the sale of wine is permissible from a Muslim in origin, but is only prevented by the impediment of prohibition. Thus in al-Kafi and al-Mudmarat. He said: 'i.e., he understands... etc.' means that among the conditions of agency is that the agent knows that disposition, e.g., he knows in sale and purchase that sale removes ownership and purchase brings it, and he knows slight fraud from excessive and excessive from slight, and he intends that disposition by not being a jester, rather he intends by directly performing the cause the establishment of the ruling. So if the agent is a minor who does not understand, or insane, or a jester, it does not occur on behalf of the principal and becomes void. The secret in it is that the agent stands in the place of the principal in expression, so he must be among the people of expression to be capable of disposition, and that is through reason. He said in al-Munyah: 'As for their interpretation of intention to avoid the sale of the jester and the coerced, it is outside the purpose because the discussion now is about the validity of the agency, not about the validity of the agent's sale; hence he omitted it in al-Kanz and we omitted it in al-Mukhtasar as well.' End. He said: 'So it is valid... etc.' The face of validity is that the principal owns the disposition and the agent is among the people of expression, and it is appropriate to restrict 'free' to 'sane' to avoid the insane. He said in al-Munyah: 'It is not valid to appoint an insane person and a minor who does not understand absolutely, and a discerning minor for harmful dispositions like divorce, manumission, gift, charity etc., and it is valid to appoint him for what benefits him without permission, and a discerning minor and a restricted slave in disposition.' And if he had said 'each of them' it would be more comprehensive because it includes appointing a free adult like him and the permitted, and appointing the permitted like him and the free adult. And what is meant by 'the permitted' is the discerning minor whom the guardian has permitted and the slave whom the master has permitted. And a discerning minor and a restricted slave in disposition for his guardian, like accepting a gift; and it is valid to appoint him for what is between harm and benefit, and lease if the minor principal is permitted in trade, otherwise it is contingent on the guardian's permission; if he permits it is valid, otherwise not, as if he performed it himself. End abridged. He said: 'And if he had said... etc.' Its elaboration: His saying 'like them' indicates that appointing a free adult a free adult and appointing a permitted a permitted is valid, and it does not indicate the validity of appointing a free adult a permitted and appointing a permitted a free adult, while in fact it is valid for each of them to appoint the other; so if he had said 'each of them' it would be more comprehensive because it includes the four categories. He said: 'And what is meant... etc.' means that the term 'permitted' includes the permitted slave and the discerning permitted minor, for it is valid for a discerning permitted person to appoint another as his other dispositions, unlike when the minor is restricted where he cannot appoint another. He said: 'And a discerning minor... etc.' is conjoined to his saying 'like them', i.e., and it is valid to appoint a free adult and the permitted a minor... etc. The secret in it is that the minor is among the people of worship, hence his disposition is effective with his guardian's permission, and the slave is among the people of disposition over himself, owning it; hence if he confesses to property it is binding after freedom, and his confession to hudud and qisas is valid, as they state. He does not own disposition regarding the master only to prevent harm, and appointment is not a disposition regarding the master because the validity of appointment is connected to his expression and capacity, and the slave is maintained on the original freedom in that because the validity of expression is due to his being human. It cannot be said that the discerning restricted minor and the restricted slave, if they are among the people of disposition, then it should be valid for them to undertake liability, but it is not so; because we say: undertaking liability from the minor is not valid because his capacity is deficient due to his not reaching puberty, and from the slave is due to the right of his master lest harm be incurred upon him. And the undertaking of harm is not prevented from them; it attaches to the nearest people to them, which is the principal who benefited from this disposition.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on the Book of Agency discusses the essential conditions for a valid agent (wakil) and who may be appointed as agent according to the Hanafi school. The text begins by stating that the agent must both understand the nature of the transaction (such as knowing that sale transfers ownership and purchase acquires it) and intend the contract. If he acts in jest, his action does not bind the principal. The underlying reason is that the agent stands in place of the principal in expressing the contract, so he must have the capacity to express legal statements, which requires sanity. The commentary then addresses the validity of appointing a free adult or a 'ma'dhun' (a minor or slave permitted by guardian or master). It clarifies that the validity of the agency depends on the principal's ownership of the disposition and the agent's capacity of expression. The text also discusses who can be appointed: a free adult, a discerning minor (for beneficial acts), a permitted slave, etc. A subtle point is made about the phrasing 'like them' (مثلهما) versus 'each of them' (كلا منهما); the latter would be more comprehensive as it covers all four combinations of free adult and ma'dhun appointing each other. The commentary further explains that a restricted minor or slave cannot appoint an agent because they lack full capacity, but they can be appointed as agents because agency does not impose liability on them—liability falls on the principal who benefits. The passage concludes with a rebuttal of an objection: even though a restricted minor or slave can be agents, they cannot undertake liability themselves; liability attaches to the principal.

Key Terms
الوكيل (al-wakil): The agent who acts on behalf of the principal in a legal transaction.
المأذون (al-ma'dhun): A minor or slave who has been permitted by the guardian or master to engage in trade or specific transactions.
الهازل (al-hazil): One who speaks or acts in jest without intention to create legal effects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is a condition for a valid agent (wakil) in a contract of sale?
A) The agent must be a free adult Muslim.
B) The agent must understand the transaction and intend it.
C) The agent must be a male.
D) The agent must be a legal scholar (faqih).

Key Takeaway
For a valid agency in Hanafi law, the agent must understand the transaction and intend it; the agent need not be a free adult—a discerning minor or permitted slave may also serve, as long as the act is beneficial.

Validity of Appointing a Minor or Slave as Agent
Explains the ruling on appointing a discerning minor or restricted slave as agent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on appointing a minor or slave as agent, identify the conditions for validity, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the buyer's option to rescind.

Core Idea
A discerning minor or restricted slave can be an agent because agency affects the principal, not the agent's capacity.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah expands on the ruling that a discerning minor (sabi 'aqil) and a restricted slave (abd mahjoor) can be validly appointed as agents. It explains the reasoning, the role of permission from guardians, and a dispute within the Hanafi school about the buyer's option.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling and its conditions
The passage states that appointing a discerning minor or restricted slave is valid, with rights reverting to the principal.
Note that the minor must be discerning (ya'qilu) and the principal must be a free adult or authorized person.
2
Examine the reasoning behind the ruling
The author provides rational arguments: the minor has capacity for worship, the slave has capacity for speech, and agency does not harm the master's rights.
This is a dalil aqli (rational proof) based on legal capacity (ahliyyah).
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf allows rescission for an unaware buyer; the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) do not.
The thamarah al-khilaf is practical: whether the buyer can annul the sale.
4
Apply the ruling to exceptions
The passage notes that certain contracts like loan (qard) cannot be delegated by the borrower; agency in litigation is valid for any right.
Understand the difference between agency in contracts and agency in litigation.

The book of agency: its definition and rulings. And a discerning minor, and a restricted slave in transaction. And if he said 'each of them', it would be more comprehensive, covering the appointment of a free adult like him and an authorized person, and the appointment of an authorized person like him and a free adult. And by 'authorized' is meant the discerning minor whom the guardian has given permission, and the slave whom the master has given permission. And a discerning minor, and a restricted slave in their transaction, like accepting a gift. And his agency is valid in what involves both harm and benefit, and leasing if the minor principal is authorized in trade; otherwise it depends on the permission of his guardian; if he permits it, it is valid, otherwise not, just as if he did it himself. End of abridgement. His saying: 'And if he said...' etc.; its explanation: His saying 'like them' indicates that the appointment of a free adult by a free adult and the appointment of an authorized person by an authorized person are permissible, but does not indicate the permissibility of a free adult appointing an authorized person, nor an authorized person appointing a free adult, while in fact each may appoint the other. So if he had said 'each of them', it would be more comprehensive, covering the four cases. His saying: 'And the meaning...' etc.; means that the term 'authorized' includes the authorized slave and the authorized discerning minor, for the appointment of a discerning authorized person as an agent for someone else is permissible, like his other transactions, unlike when the minor is restricted, where he cannot appoint someone else as agent. His saying: 'And a discerning minor...' etc.; is conjoined to his saying 'like them', i.e., it is valid for a free adult and an authorized person to appoint a minor... etc. The reasoning therein: The minor is among those capable of worship; hence his transaction is valid with the permission of his guardian. The slave is among those capable of transaction regarding himself; he owns himself; hence if he confesses to a debt, it becomes binding upon him after emancipation, and his confession affecting hudud and qisas is valid, as they have stated. He does not possess the right of transaction concerning the rights of the master, to prevent harm. And agency is not a transaction concerning the rights of the master, because the validity of agency depends on his speech and capacity, and the slave remains on the original basis of freedom in that, because the validity of speech is due to his being human. It cannot be said: 'If the restricted discerning minor and the restricted slave are capable of transactions, then they should be capable of undertaking liability (dhaman), but they are not.' Because we say: Undertaking liability by the minor is not valid, because his capacity is deficient due to his not having attained maturity; and by the slave, it is due to the right of his master, to avoid harm to him. And since the undertaking of harm does not apply to them, it attaches to the nearest relative to them, which is the principal who benefited from this transaction. 'And his rights revert to their principal, not to them': i.e., when a free adult or an authorized person appoints a restricted minor or a restricted slave, the rights of the contract revert to their principal, not to them. 'Regarding everything he contracts by himself' is connected to his statement 'so his agency is valid...' up to the end. 'And regarding litigation (khusoomah)'... His saying: 'And his rights revert' means the rights of the contract carried out by the restricted minor and restricted slave revert to their principal, not to them. But liability (uhdah) binds the restricted slave after emancipation, because the obstacle (the master's right) is removed, unlike the minor, because the obstacle is his own right, and his right does not cease upon attaining maturity. And from Abu Yusuf: The buyer, if he did not know the condition of the agent-seller, and then learns that he was a minor or restricted, has the option to rescind, as in al-Kafi. His saying: 'Regarding everything he contracts by himself' means that agency is valid for every contract that the principal may contract for himself. So it is not objected that the agent possesses the right of disposition in what he is appointed for, while he does not possess the right to appoint someone else, because the agent possesses disposition for another, not for himself. Similarly, it is not objected that a Muslim may not sell wine, but he may appoint a dhimmi to sell it, and a dhimmi may not appoint a Muslim to sell his wine while he may do it himself, because it is the reverse, and objection (naqd) only applies in a direct analogy (tard). Yes, there remains a point: The borrower may himself undertake the loan for himself, and he is not permitted to appoint someone else to do so; even if he appoints an agent, and the agent borrows, it is for the agent, not for the principal, because the consideration in the matter of lending does not become a debt in the borrower's liability by the contract; rather it becomes due by possession, and the order to take possession is not valid, because it is the property of another, as al-Zayla'i and others have stated. Except that it is said in al-Khaniyyah: If he appoints for borrowing and the agent attributes the borrowing to the principal, it is for the principal; otherwise it is for the agent. End. Hence al-Quhistani said: There is a difference of opinion. And Allah knows best what is correct. His saying: 'And regarding litigation...' etc. means that agency is valid for litigation regarding every right, whether...
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the ruling regarding the validity of appointing a minor or a slave as an agent (wakil) in Hanafi law. The author explains that a discerning minor (sabi 'aqil) and a restricted slave (abd mahjoor) can be validly appointed as agents by a free adult or by another authorized person (ma'dhun), subject to certain conditions. The key condition is that if the minor or slave is themselves authorized (ma'dhun) by their guardian or master to engage in trade, their appointment of someone else as agent is valid. Conversely, if they are restricted (mahjoor), their appointment is suspended upon the permission of the guardian. The reasoning provided is that the minor or slave possesses basic legal capacity (ahliyyah) for speech (ibarah), and the act of appointing an agent does not cause direct harm to the guardian or master's rights. The rights and liabilities arising from the contract revert to the principal (muwakkil), not to the minor or slave agent. A notable intra-Hanafi dispute is mentioned: According to Abu Yusuf, if a buyer in a sale conducted through such an agent was unaware that the agent was a minor or slave, the buyer has the option to rescind the contract upon discovery. The passage also clarifies that agency is valid for all contracts that the principal can perform himself, with exceptions such as loan (qard), where the borrower cannot appoint an agent because the liability arises only upon possession. The text further discusses that agency in litigation (khusoomah) is valid for any right.

Key Terms
المأذون (al-ma'dhun): A discerning minor who has been given permission by his guardian, or a slave who has been given permission by his master, to engage in trade or financial transactions.
المحجور (al-mahjoor): A minor who is restricted from financial transactions due to lack of maturity, or a slave restricted due to the rights of the master.
الوكالة (al-wakalah): A contract of agency where one person (al-muwakkil) appoints another (al-wakil) to perform a legal act on his behalf.
الخصومة (al-khusoomah): Litigation or legal dispute; agency in litigation means authorizing someone to represent in court or in claims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position explained in this passage, what condition is required for a minor (sabi) to be validly appointed as an agent (wakil)?
A) The minor must be at least 15 years old.
B) The minor must be discerning (ya'qilu) and the principal must be a free adult or authorized person.
C) The minor must have reached puberty (baligh).
D) The minor must be given explicit permission by the judge.

Key Takeaway
A discerning minor or restricted slave can validly be an agent because agency transfers rights and liabilities to the principal, not the agent.

intermediate

Rights of Contracts Return to the Principal
Explains principal's rights versus agent's liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that contract rights return to the principal and distinguish the cases where the agent bears liability.

Core Idea
A contract's rights always revert to the principal — the agent is merely a conduit.

This lesson unpacks a foundational principle of Hanafi agency law: when an agent (wakil) conducts a contract, the resulting rights and obligations belong to the principal (muwakkil), not the agent. The passage clarifies that this holds even when the agent is a minor or slave under interdiction, while highlighting special cases such as the slave's post-emancipation liability and the unique rules of loan agency. Understanding this principle prevents confusion about who is bound by the contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling
Locate the core statement: 'ويرجع حقوقه إلى موكلهما دونهما' – rights of the contract return to the principal, not the agent. Note that this applies even when the agent is a minor or slave under interdiction.
The ruling is based on the nature of agency: the agent acts for another's benefit.
2
Analyze the liability distinction
Compare the fate of liability (ʿuhdah) for the slave agent versus the minor agent. For the slave, liability becomes binding after emancipation (zawal al-maniʿ). For the minor, liability never attaches because the impediment is his own right (lack of capacity), which does not disappear with puberty.
This distinction shows how the school weighs different types of impediments: the master's right vs. the minor's own protection.
3
Examine Abu Yusuf's added option
Note that Abu Yusuf grants the buyer the option to rescind (khiyar al-faskh) if he was unaware of the agent's incapacity at the time of contract. This is an intra-Hanafi nuance that protects the innocent party.
This illustrates how later imams can supplement the founding principle to achieve fairness.
4
Understand the exception of loan (qard)
Read carefully the exception: a borrower cannot appoint an agent to borrow because the loan contract becomes binding only upon possession (qabd), and the principal cannot authorize possession of something not yet his. However, if the agent expressly attributes the borrowing to the principal, it belongs to the principal — a disputed position.
This exception is a rare departure from the general rule, justified by the specific nature of loan as a possessory contract.

كتاب الوكالة تعريفها وأحكامها ويرجع حقوقه إلى موكلهما دونهما ، بكل ما يعقده بنفسه ، وبالخصومة ويرجع حقوقه" إلى موكلهما دونَهما) : أي إذا وَكَّلَ الحرُّ البالغ ، أو المأذونُ صبياً محجورا ، أو عبداً محجورا يرجع حقوق العقدِ إلى مؤكلهما، ولا يرجع إليهما. بكل ما يعقده بنفسه ) ، يتعلّق بقولهِ فصح توكيل الحر إلى آخره، (وبالخصومة (۱۱۳) [١] قوله : ويرجع حقوقه ؛ أي حقوق العقد الذي باشره الصبي المحجور والعبد المحجور إلى موكلهما لا إليهما، لكنَّ العهدة تلزم العبد المحجور بعد العتق لزوال المانع وهو حق المولى بخلاف الصبي، فإنَّ المانع حقه، وحقه لا يزول بالبلوغ، وعن أبي يوسف : إنَّ المشتري إذ لم يعلم بحال الوكيل البائع ، ثمَّ عَلِمَ أنه صبي أو محجورٌ جاز له الفسخ كذا في «الكافي». ٢١) قوله : بكل ما يعقده بنفسه ؛ يعني إنَّ التوكيل يصح بكل عقد يعقده الموكل بنفسه لنفسه، فلا يرد أنَّ الوكيل يملك التصرُّف فيما وكل فيه مع أنه لا يملك التوكيل ؛ لأنَّ الوكيل يملك التصرُّفَ لغيره لا لنفسه، وكذا لا يردُ أنَّ المسلم لا يملك بيع الخمر، ويملك توكيل الذمي به، ولا يملك الذمي توكيل مسلم بيع خمره وهو يملكه ؛ لأنه عكس، والنقض لا يكون إلا في الطرد. نعم ؛ بقي هاهنا شيء وهو أنَّ المستقرض له أن يباشره بنفسه لنفسه، ولا يجوز أن یوگل به غيره، حتى لو وكل به فاستقرض الوكيل كان له لا للموكل ؛ لأنَّ البدل في باب القرض لا يجب ديناً في ذمَّةِ المستقرض بالعقد، وإنمـا يجـب بالقبض، والأمرُ بالقبض لا يصح؛ لأنه ملك الغير، كما صرح به الزَّيْلَعِيُّ (٢)، وغيره. (۲) إلا أنه قال في «الخانية»: إن وكل بالاستقراض إن أضاف الوكيل الاستقراض إلى الموكل كان للموكل، وإلا كان للوكيل. انتهى ؛ ولذا قال القُهستاني: إنه مختلف فيه ، والله أعلم بالصواب
Book of Agency: Its definition and rulings. "And its rights return to their principal, not to them" — meaning when a free adult or authorized person appoints a minor under interdiction or a slave under interdiction as agent, the rights of the contract return to their principal, and do not return to them. "With everything he contracts himself" — relates to his statement that the agency of the free person is valid, etc. "And with litigation" — commentary: "His statement: 'and its rights return' — i.e., the rights of the contract performed by the minor under interdiction and the slave under interdiction return to their principal, not to them. However, liability binds the slave under interdiction after emancipation due to the removal of the impediment, which is the right of the master, unlike the minor, for the impediment is his right, and his right does not cease with puberty. And from Abu Yusuf: If the buyer did not know the state of the selling agent, then later knew he was a minor or under interdiction, he has the option of rescission, as in al-Kafi. His statement: 'With everything he contracts himself' — means agency is valid for every contract the principal can contract for himself. Thus it is not objected that the agent has the power to dispose in what he is appointed for while he does not have the power to appoint another, because the agent disposes for another, not for himself. Similarly, it is not objected that a Muslim cannot sell wine but can appoint a dhimmi for it, while a dhimmi cannot appoint a Muslim to sell his wine though he can do it himself, because it is the reverse, and objection is only in direct cases. Yes, there remains something here: the borrower can perform the borrowing himself, but may not appoint another to do so; even if he appoints an agent and the agent borrows, it belongs to the agent, not the principal, because the consideration in loan does not become a debt in the borrower's liability by the contract, but only by possession, and an order to take possession is invalid because it is the property of another, as stated by al-Zayla'i and others. However, he said in al-Khaniya: if he appoints for borrowing and the agent attributes the borrowing to the principal, it belongs to the principal; otherwise to the agent. End. Hence al-Quhistani said: it is disputed. And Allah knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage from the sharh of al-Wiqayah explains the fundamental principle of agency: the rights and obligations arising from a contract performed by an agent (wakil) revert to the principal (muwakkil), not to the agent personally. The author begins by clarifying that this applies when the agent is a minor under interdiction or a slave under interdiction. The principle is that the agent is merely a representative. However, there is a nuance: in the case of a slave, after emancipation, the liability (ʿuhdah) that was suspended due to the master's right becomes binding on the former slave, because the impediment is removed. For a minor, the impediment (lack of legal capacity) is inherent and does not disappear with puberty—rather, the minor's disability is for his own protection, so he never becomes liable. Abu Yusuf adds that if the buyer was unaware of the agent's incapacity, he may rescind. The text then affirms that agency is valid for any contract that the principal can perform himself, and addresses potential objections: the agent can delegate (though he cannot appoint a sub-agent without permission), and a Muslim can appoint a dhimmi to sell wine because the contract is attributed to the Muslim principal. The exception of loan (qard) is highlighted: a borrower cannot appoint an agent to borrow on his behalf because the loan contract becomes binding only upon taking possession, and the principal cannot authorize taking possession of something that is not yet his property; however, if the agent expressly acts for the principal, the loan belongs to the principal according to al-Khaniya. The passage ends by noting this is a disputed issue.

Key Terms
وكالة (wakalah): A contract by which one person appoints another to act on his behalf in a legal matter.
موكل (muwakkil): The person who appoints the agent; the principal.
وكيل (wakil): The person appointed to act on behalf of another; the agent.
عهدة (ʿuhdah): Liability or responsibility for a contract, especially regarding defects or debts.
محجور (mahjur): A person under legal interdiction due to minority or slavery.
قرض (qard): A loan contract; in Hanafi fiqh, it is a possessory contract that becomes binding only upon taking possession (qabd).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens to the rights of a contract when a minor under interdiction acts as an agent?
A) The rights belong to the minor agent.
B) The rights return to the principal.
C) The rights are suspended until the minor reaches puberty.
D) The contract is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The rights and obligations arising from an agent's contract always belong to the principal, not the agent, with limited exceptions for liability after impediment removal and the special case of loan agency.

The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Opponent's Consent in Litigation Agency
Intra-Hanafi dispute on condition of opponent's consent in litigation agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute on opponent's consent in agency for lawsuits and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Agency in lawsuits requires the opponent's consent per Abu Hanifa; Sahibayn disagree; later scholars balance both.

In Hanafi fiqh, appointing an agent to handle a lawsuit (wakalah bil khusumah) is generally valid. However, a significant intra-madhhab dispute arises over whether the opponent's consent is a condition for the agency's enforceability. This lesson explores the arguments of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and traces how later scholars resolved the matter into a practical, balanced fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Understand the legal context
Recognize that agency in lawsuits is needed because not everyone is skilled in legal procedures. The passage affirms that such agency is valid for all types of claims, including hudud and qisas, for both plaintiff and defendant.
The basis is necessity and the principle that what one can do personally, one can delegate.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa holds that agency in lawsuits requires the opponent's consent; without it, the agency is either invalid or non-binding. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold it valid and binding without consent.
Some scholars frame the dispute as being about enforceability (luzum) rather than validity (sihha).
3
Examine the proofs for each view
Abu Hanifa's evidence includes the hadith of Ali appointing Aqil as agent, which implies consent from the opponent? (text is ambiguous). Sahibayn rely on general permissibility of agency and the necessity argument.
The text cites al-Bayhaqi's narration: 'Ali appointed Aqil as his agent in lawsuits.' The exact implication is debated.
4
Trace the evolution of the tarjih
Early scholars like al-Halwani gave the mufti a choice. Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi preferred the Sahibayn's view for noble persons. Later scholars (mutakkhirin) adopted a middle ground: the judge examines the situation. If the opponent shows aversion, agency is not forced; if the principal intends harm, consent is needed.
This balanced view is attributed to Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi and becomes the mufta bihi.
5
Apply the mufta bihi in practice
A judge today would assess the circumstances: if the opponent clearly objects or the principal is trying to harm, the agency is not accepted without the opponent's consent. Otherwise, agency is allowed.
This preserves the right of the opponent while recognizing the necessity of agency.

قوله : وبالخصومة...الخ ؛ أي وصح التوكيلُ بالخصومة في كل حق، سواء كان في كل حق، ولا يلزم بلا رضا خصمه قال بعض المشايخ : التوكيلُ بالخصومة بلا رضا الخصم باطل عند أبي حنيفة ، صحيح عندهما، وقال البعض : الاختلافُ في اللزوم لا في الصحة ، وفي «الهداية» اختار هذا حداً أو قصاصاً أو غيرهما، وسواء كان من جانب المدعي أو المدعى عليه ؛ لأنَّ الخصومة يشملها. نص عليه في «الإيضاح»، ووجه الصحة أنَّ كلّ أحد لا يهتدي إلى وجوه الخصومات فيحتاج إلى التوكيل بالضرورة ؛ ولأنَّ الموكل يملك مباشرتها بنفسه، فكذا يملك التوكيل بها. وأخرجَ البَيْهَقِيُّ : «إنَّ عليّاً له وكل في الخصومات عقيلاً ) ، لكن لا بُدَّ له (1) من رضاء الخصم عند أبي حنيفة له ، وعندهما يجوز من غير رضاء الخصم. وفي «الخلاصة»: قال شمس الأئمة الحَلْوَانِيُّ : المفتي مخير في هذه المسألة، إن شاء أفتى بقول أبي حنيفة ، وإن شاء أفتى بقولهما، والفقيه أبو الليث له كان يفتي بقولهما في الشريف. وفي «الخزانة»: إنَّ الفقيه أبا الليث الا الله اختار قولهما للفتوى ، الشريف والوضيع في ذلك سواء. وفي «الكافي»: المتأخّرون اختاروا الفتوى أنَّ القاضي إذا علم من الخصم ا في إباء التوكيل لا يمكنه من ذلك ، ويقبلُ التوكيل من الموكل، وإن علم من الموكل القصد إلى إضرار الخصم بالتوكيل لا يقبل منه التوكيل إلا برضا الخصم، وهو اختيار شمس الأئمة السرخسي . كذا في شرح البرْجَندِي» و«كمال الدراية». والخصومة في اللغة: بيكار، والخصم المخاصم، والجمع خصوم، وقد يكون : للجمع والاثنين ،والمؤنّث، وفسّرها في «الجوهرة» (۳) : بالدعوى الصحيحة أو الجواب الصريح. [1]قوله : قال بعض...الخ ؛ يعني أنه لا شك في أنَّ رضاء الخصم يشترط في
His statement: 'And with litigation' — i.e., agency in lawsuits is valid for every right, whether it is a prescribed punishment or retaliation or other, and whether the agent is from the plaintiff's side or the defendant's, because litigation includes all. It is stated in al-Idah. The basis of validity is that not everyone knows the ways of litigation, so there is a necessity for agency; and because the principal can directly handle it, so he can appoint an agent. Al-Bayhaqi narrated that Ali appointed Aqil as his agent in lawsuits. However, the opponent's consent is necessary according to Abu Hanifa, while according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is valid without the opponent's consent. In al-Khulasa: Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani said: The mufti has a choice in this matter; if he wishes, he may give fatwa according to Abu Hanifa's view, and if he wishes, according to theirs. Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi used to give fatwa according to their view for the noble. In al-Khazana: Abu al-Layth chose their view for fatwa, and the noble and the common are equal in that. In al-Kafi: The later scholars chose the fatwa that if the judge knows from the opponent an aversion to agency, he does not allow it, but accepts agency from the principal; and if he knows from the principal an intent to harm the opponent through agency, he does not accept it without the opponent's consent. This is the choice of Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi. So in Sharh al-Barjandi and Kamal al-Diraya. Al-khusumah linguistically: a contest; al-khasm: the opponent, plural khussum; it can be used for plural and dual and feminine. In al-Jawhara it is interpreted as a valid claim or a clear response. [1] His statement: 'Some said' — meaning there is no doubt that the opponent's consent is required in
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage from the sharh addresses the ruling on appointing an agent for lawsuits (wakalah bil khusumah). It begins by affirming the validity of such agency for all types of legal claims, including hudud and qisas, and for both plaintiffs and defendants. The reason is that not everyone is skilled in legal procedures, so agency is a necessity. Also, since the principal can handle the lawsuit personally, he can delegate it. A hadith from al-Bayhaqi is cited: Caliph Ali appointed his brother Aqil as his agent in lawsuits. However, a dispute arises: Abu Hanifa holds that agency in lawsuits is only valid with the opponent's consent; without it, the agency is invalid (or not binding, as some say). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold it is valid without the opponent's consent. The commentary notes that some scholars say the dispute is about enforceability (luzum), not validity. Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani said the mufti may choose either view. Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi preferred the Sahibayn's view for fatwa, especially for noble persons. Later scholars opted for a balanced approach: if the opponent shows aversion, the judge should not force him to accept the agency; but if the principal's intent is to harm the opponent, agency is not allowed without consent. This is the choice of al-Sarakhsi. The passage ends by noting the linguistic meaning of khusumah.

Key Terms
خصومة (khusumah): Litigation or lawsuit; a dispute between two parties in court.
رضا الخصم (rida al-khasm): The consent of the opposing party in a lawsuit.
وكيل بالخصومة (wakil bil khusumah): An agent appointed to represent someone in a legal case.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later scholars' preferred fatwa in the Hanafi school, when can a judge reject an agency in a lawsuit?
A) Only if the agent is a minor.
B) If the opponent clearly refuses or the principal intends harm.
C) Always, because agency in lawsuits is invalid.
D) Only if the lawsuit involves hudud.

Key Takeaway
Agency in lawsuits is valid in Hanafi fiqh, but the opponent's consent is a point of dispute; the later preferred fatwa balances the necessity of agency with protection from harm.

Opponent’s Consent in Litigation Agency: Intra-Hanafi Dispute and Resolution
Dispute on whether opponent's consent is needed for valid agency in litigation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on opponent's consent in litigation agency, explain their evidence, and apply the later mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa requires opponent's consent; the Sahibayn do not; later scholars condition it on harm or obstruction.

This passage from ‘Umdat al-Ri‘āyah presents a key intra-Hanafi dispute: is the opponent’s consent required for a valid agency in litigation? The disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two companions leads to different practical consequences. The passage then shows how later scholars resolved the tension, offering a nuanced fatwa that balances the principal’s right to appoint an agent with the opponent’s right to avoid harm.

Guided Path
1
State the Ruling and the Dispute
Identify the act (litigation agency) and the two opposed rulings: Abu Hanifa requires opponent's consent; Sahibayn do not. Note that the dispute may be about validity (sihhah) or bindingness (luzūm).
The passage records both views: some say validity, others say bindingness.
2
Examine the Proofs
Review the evidence for each side: Sahibayn cite necessity, analogy to agency for debt collection, and the hadith of ‘Ali appointing ‘Aqil. Abu Hanifa cites potential harm to the opponent and analogy to the jointly-owned slave's kitābah.
The hadith is from Bayhaqi; its transmission is zanni.
3
Analyze the Dispute Point
Understand the core illa: Sahibayn say agency is a disposal in the principal’s exclusive right; Abu Hanifa says the right of response is shared and harm may result.
Abu Hanifa also argues that the opponent's right to respond is not exclusive to the principal.
4
Apply the Later Tarjih
Learn the mufta bihi from al-Sarakhsī and later scholars: agency is valid without consent unless the principal intends to harm the opponent. If the opponent is obstructive, the judge can force acceptance.
This middle ground preserves both the right to appoint an agent and the protection from harm.
5
Note the Exceptions
Both sides agree that consent is not required when the principal is sick, absent (travel distance), or intending to travel, because the opponent's right to a direct response is not absolute.
These exceptions are based on necessity and reduced obligation.
