⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في كل حق، ولا يلزم بلا رضا خصمه) ، قال بعض المشايخ : التوكيلُ بالخصومة بلا رضا الخصم باطل عند أبي حنيفة ، صحيح عندهما، وقال البعض : الاختلافُ في اللزوم لا في الصحة ، وفي «الهداية» اختار هذا حداً أو قصاصاً أو غيرهما، وسواء كان من جانب المدعي أو المدعى عليه ; لأنَّ الخصومة يشملها. نص عليه في «الإيضاح»، ووجه الصحة أنَّ كلّ أحد لا يهتدي إلى وجوه الخصومات فيحتاج إلى التوكيل بالضرورة ; ولأنَّ الموكل يملك مباشرتها بنفسه، فكذا يملك التوكيل بها. وأخرجَ البَيْهَقِيُّ : «إنَّ عليّاً له وكل في الخصومات عقيلاً ) ، لكن لا بُدَّ له من رضاء الخصم عند أبي حنيفة له ، وعندهما يجوز من غير رضاء الخصم. وفي «الخلاصة»: قال شمس الأئمة الحَلْوَانِيُّ : المفتي مخير في هذه المسألة، إن شاء أفتى بقول أبي حنيفة ، وإن شاء أفتى بقولهما، والفقيه أبو الليث له كان يفتي بقولهما في الشريف. وفي «الخزانة»: إنَّ الفقيه أبا الليث الا الله اختار قولهما للفتوى ، الشريف والوضيع في ذلك سواء. وفي «الكافي»: المتأخّرون اختاروا الفتوى أنَّ القاضي إذا علم من الخصم ا في إباء التوكيل لا يمكنه من ذلك ، ويقبلُ التوكيل من الموكل، وإن علم من الموكل القصد إلى إضرار الخصم بالتوكيل لا يقبل منه التوكيل إلا برضا الخصم، وهو اختيار شمس الأئمة السرخسي . كذا في شرح البرْجَندِي» و«كمال الدراية». والخصومة في اللغة: بيكار، والخصم المخاصم، والجمع خصوم، وقد يكون للجمع والاثنين ،والمؤنّث، وفسّرها في «الجوهرة» : بالدعوى الصحيحة أو الجواب الصريح. [1]قوله : قال بعض...الخ ; يعني أنه لا شك في أنَّ رضاء الخصم يشترط في التوكيل بالخصومة عند الإمام إلا أن يكون الموكل مريضاً لا يمكنه حضور مجلس الحاكم، أو غائباً مسيرة سفر ، أو مريداً للسفر كما سيذكره المصنف ، ولا يشترط عندهما، وهو قول الشافعي له أيضاً. فقال بعض مشايخنا : هذا الخلاف في صحة التوكيل، فلا يصح التوكيل بدونِ رضاء الخصم عند الإمام، ويصح عندهما ، وقال البعض: إنَّ الخلاف في اللزوم. ومعناه : إنَّ الوكالة هل ترتد برد الخصم أم لا ؟ فعند الإمام غير لازمة، حتى لا يلزم الخصم الحضور، والجواب بخصومة الوكيل وعندهما يلزم ذلك ; لأنَّ التوكيل تصرُّف في خالص حق الموكل ، فإنّه وكل الوكيل بالجواب أو بالخصومة، وكلاهما حق الموكل، والظاهر أن التصرُّف في خالص حقه لا يتوقف على رضاء غـيـره ، كما أنَّ التوكيل بقبض الديون لا يتوقف على رضاء المديون. وللإمام أن كون التوكيل تصرُّفاً في خالص حقه ممنوع ; لأنَّ الجواب حق للمدعي على المدعى عليه ; ولهذا يستحضر المدعي الخصم في مجلس القاضي، والمستحق للغير لا يكون خالصاً له. وإن سلمنا أنه تصرف في خالص حقه، فنقول : تصرُّف الإنسان في خالص حقه لا يصح إلا إذا لم يتضرر به غيره، وهاهنا ليس كذلك ; لأنَّ الناس يتفاوتون في الخصومة، فإنَّ الإنسان قد يصوِّرُ الباطل بصورة الحق، وقد لا يمكنه تمشية الحق على وجهه، ويمكن أن يكون للوكيل حذق في الخصومات، فيتضرر بذلك الخصم ، فيتوقف على رضاه. كما أنَّ العبد المشترك إذا كاتبه أحد الشريكين يتخير الآخر، فإنَّ الكتابة تتوقف على رضاء الآخر، وإن كان تصرُّفاً في خالص حقه لإمكان ضرر شريكه، فيتخيَّرُ بين الإمضاء والفسخ، بخلاف التوكيل بالقبض والإيفاء ; لأنَّ الحق معلوم بقبضه، فلا يحصلُ بالتوكيل بهما ضرر الآخر، وبخلاف المريض والمسافر، فإنَّ الجواب غير واجب عليهما، وهو ظاهر.
In every right, and it is not binding without the consent of his opponent. Some scholars said: Agency for litigation without the opponent's consent is invalid according to Abu Hanifa, valid according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Others said: The disagreement is about bindingness, not validity. In al-Hidayah, he chose this for hudud or qisas or other matters, whether from the plaintiff or defendant; because litigation includes it. Textually stated in al-Idah. The face of validity is that everyone is not guided to the aspects of litigations, so he needs agency by necessity; and because the principal can undertake it himself, so he can also appoint an agent for it. Al-Bayhaqi narrated that Ali appointed Aqil as his agent in litigations. But there must be consent of the opponent according to Abu Hanifa, while according to the two it is permissible without the opponent's consent. In al-Khulasa: Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani said: The mufti is given a choice in this matter; if he wishes, he gives fatwa according to Abu Hanifa, and if he wishes, he gives fatwa according to the two. And the faqih Abu al-Layth used to give fatwa according to the two for the noble. In al-Khazana: The faqih Abu al-Layth chose the opinion of the two for fatwa, the noble and the common are equal in that. In al-Kafi: The later scholars chose the fatwa that if the judge knows that the opponent refuses agency, he prevents him from that and accepts the agency from the principal; but if he knows that the principal intends to harm the opponent by agency, he does not accept the agency without the opponent's consent. This is the choice of Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi. Similarly in Sharh al-Birjandi and Kamal al-Diraya. Litigation in language: (bikär meaning work), and al-khasm is the adversary, plural khosum, and it can be used for plural and dual and feminine. It is interpreted in al-Jawhara as the correct claim or explicit answer. He said: Some said... meaning there is no doubt that the opponent's consent is a condition in agency for litigation according to the Imam, except if the principal is sick and cannot attend the court, or absent at a travel distance, or intending to travel as the author will mention, and it is not a condition according to the two, and it is also the opinion of al-Shafi'i. So some of our scholars said: This disagreement is about the validity of agency; agency is not valid without the opponent's consent according to the Imam, and valid according to the two. Others said: The disagreement is about bindingness. Its meaning is: Does the agency become void by the opponent's rejection or not? According to the Imam it is not binding, so the opponent is not obliged to attend and respond to the agent's litigation; according to the two it is binding. Because agency is a disposal in the principal's exclusive right, for he appointed the agent for response or litigation, both of which are the principal's right. It is apparent that disposal in one's exclusive right does not depend on another's consent, just as agency for receiving debts does not depend on the debtor's consent. The Imam's response: That agency is a disposal in his exclusive right is disputed; because the right of response belongs to the plaintiff against the defendant; for this reason the plaintiff brings the opponent to the court, and what is due to another is not exclusive to him. And if we concede that it is a disposal in his exclusive right, we say: A person's disposal in his exclusive right is only valid if no harm comes to another thereby, but here it is not so; because people vary in litigation; a person may present falsehood in the form of truth, and may not be able to conduct the truth properly, and the agent may be skilled in litigation, thus harming the opponent, so it depends on his consent. Just as a jointly owned slave, if one of the partners enters into a kitabah (contract of manumission) with him, the other partner has the option; for kitabah depends on the other's consent, even though it is a disposal in his exclusive right, due to the possibility of harm to his partner, so he has the choice between ratification and annulment. This differs from agency for receipt and payment, because the right is known when received, so agency for them does not cause harm to the other. And this differs from the sick and the traveler, because response is not obligatory upon them, and this is apparent.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage from ‘Umdat al-Ri‘āyah is a commentary on the topic of agency (wakālah) in litigation. The author begins by presenting a dispute among the early masters regarding the requirement of the opponent’s consent (riḍā al-khaṣm) for the validity or bindingness of appointing an agent to litigate. The passage is an expansion probe into the ruling. 

The first opinion attributed to Abū Ḥanīfa is that agency for litigation without the opponent’s consent is invalid (bāṭil); the two companions (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad) hold it valid. A second group among the scholars clarifies that the disagreement is about bindingness (luzūm) not validity: according to Abū Ḥanīfa, the opponent is not bound to appear and respond to the agent; according to the two, he is bound. The text then provides the rationale for validity: necessity and analogy to the principal’s own right to litigate. A ḥadīth from al-Bayhaqī about ‘Alī appointing ‘Aqīl is cited as a precedent, but Abū Ḥanīfa maintains the condition of consent.

Later scholars offer a compromise. Shams al-A’imma al-Ḥalwānī says the mufti is free to choose either opinion. Abū al-Layth al-Samarqandī favored the two companions’ opinion. The later scholars (al-muta’akhkhirūn) refined the position: if the opponent is obstructive, the judge can prevent him and accept the agency from the principal; but if the principal intends to harm the opponent, the judge does not accept the agency unless the opponent consents. This is the choice of Shams al-A’imma al-Sarakhsī.

The passage then defines “khuṣūmah” and “khaṣm” linguistically and technically. It continues with the proof of the two companions: agency is a disposal in the principal’s exclusive right, so it should not depend on another’s consent, analogous to agency for debt collection. Abū Ḥanīfa counters: the right of response is actually the plaintiff’s right, not exclusively the defendant’s; moreover, harm may result due to varying skill levels in litigation, so consent is needed. He draws an analogy to the case of a jointly owned slave’s kitābah, where the partner’s consent is required due to potential harm. Exceptions are made for the sick, absent, or traveler, where consent is not needed because response is not obligatory on them.

This passage illustrates the Hanafī method of reasoning: a combination of qiyās, istiḥsān, and consideration of ḍarar (harm), as well as the internal hierarchy of authority from Abū Ḥanīfa, the two companions, and the later tarjīḥ (preference).

Key Terms
التوكيل بالخصومة (al-tawkeel bi al-khusoomah): Appointing someone to represent oneself in litigation (plaintiff or defendant).
الخصم (al-khasm): The opposing party in a dispute (litigant).
رضا الخصم (rida al-khasm): The consent of the opposing party to the appointment of an agent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later preferred fatwa in this passage, when can the judge accept the agency without the opponent's consent?
A) Only if the principal is sick or absent
B) Only if the opponent is obstructive in refusing the agency
C) Only if the agent is a skilled litigator
D) Only if the principal intends to harm the opponent

Key Takeaway
The operative Hanafi position on opponent's consent in litigation agency is conditional: consent is not required unless the principal intends to harm the opponent.

Exceptions to Opponent's Consent for Agency in Litigation
Explains exceptions and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on agency binding without consent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four Hanafi exceptions where an agent may be appointed without the opponent's consent, explain the reasoning behind each, and apply the ruling to determine whether agency is binding in new scenarios.

Core Idea
Sickness, travel, intention to travel, and seclusion excuse a principal from court attendance, allowing agency without opponent's consent to prevent hardship.

In litigation, normally an agent can only be appointed with the opponent's consent. However, Hanafi fiqh recognizes exceptions for principals who are sick, absent at a travel distance, intending to travel, or a secluded woman. This lesson examines the conditions for these exceptions, the evidence based on necessity (darurah), and an important intra-madhhab difference regarding whether the agency is immediately binding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule and the exceptions
The default rule: an agent cannot be appointed without the opponent's consent. The text lists four exceptions: sick unable to attend, absent at travel distance, intending to travel, and secluded woman.
These exceptions are based on the principal's inability or extreme difficulty to appear in court.
2
Analyze the reasoning (illa) for each exception
For the sick: inability due to illness. For the traveler: physical absence. For one intending to travel: potential harm from delaying travel preparations. For the secluded woman: modesty and custom.
The common thread is preventing hardship (jarh) to the principal.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some scholars (e.g., al-Shumnunni) hold that the agency is immediately binding without consent. The view in al-Muhit (likely Sahibayn) says the opponent may wait or consent, making it binding only upon consent.
The commentator notes the most correct view (al-ashah) that even if the sick can ride, the exception applies.
4
Apply the ruling to borderline cases
What if the sick can ride? What if intention to travel is claimed without evidence? The text provides answers: riding does not negate the exception; intention must be proven by preparation or oath.
The judge investigates and may administer an oath.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
Al-Tahtawi in his Hashiyah on al-Durr al-Mukhtar favors the waiting/consent view, making it the relied-upon position for fatwa.
This balances the rights of both litigants.

Cream of the End for the Support of Care
Except with a principal who is sick, unable to attend the court session, or absent at a journey's distance, or intending to travel.
(Except with a sick principal unable to attend the judge's court, or absent at a journey's distance, or intending to travel.)
As for the sick, it is due to his inability due to illness; as for the traveler, because he is absent. So the opponent has no right to demand their presence. Thus, in their appointment of an agent, there is no forfeiture of the right due upon them. Al-Shumnunni and al-Ayni and others mentioned it.
[1] His saying: 'Except with a sick principal...etc.' means if the principal is sick and unable to attend the judge's court in person, or absent at a journey's distance, or intending to travel, then the appointment of an agent for litigation is binding without the opponent's consent. And if the principal is sick and cannot attend himself but can ride an animal or be carried on people's hands, the appointment of an agent is binding without the opponent's consent, even if riding does not increase his illness, in the most correct view.
And if the claimant or the defendant says: 'I intend to travel,' the appointment of an agent becomes binding without his opponent's consent, because he would suffer harm by being cut off from his affairs. But his statement regarding the intention to travel is not accepted; rather, the judge investigates his matter, because the condition of one who travels is not hidden. This was explicitly stated by Al-Shumnunni and others.
[2] His saying: 'or absent at a journey's distance'; our teacher al-Tahtawi said in his Hashiyah on al-Durr al-Mukhtar: It should be that this is when he does not wait; therefore it is said in al-Muhit: If the principal is sick or a traveler, their appointment of an agent is not binding without the opponent's consent. Rather, it is said to the plaintiff: If you want a response from your opponent, wait until the excuse is removed, and if you do not wait, your way is to consent to the agency. When he consents, it becomes binding upon him by his consent in the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah). End. End of what is in Hashiyat al-Tahtawi.

Or a secluded woman who is not accustomed to going out. And with his fulfilling and receiving.
[2]
That is, he is engaged in preparing the equipment for travel, or a secluded woman not accustomed to going out. And with his fulfilling and receiving.
But it has not appeared if the defendant is sick or a traveler, and he qualified it by saying 'a journey's distance' because the absent person less than that is like one present.
1. His saying: 'That is...' He said in al-Bahr: The intention to travel is an internal matter, so proof is necessary. It is either the opponent's confirmation of it or an external indication. And his statement 'I intend to travel' is not accepted, but the judge looks into his condition and his preparation, for the preparation of one who travels is not hidden. End. And in al-Bazzaziyyah: If he says 'I am leaving with such and such caravan,' he is asked about them. And in Khizanat al-Muftiyyin: If he says 'I intend to travel,' agency becomes binding whether he is the claimant or defendant, but the defendant gives surety so that the claimant can claim his debt. If the opponent denies it, the judge makes him swear: 'By Allah, you intend to travel.' End.
[2] His saying: 'preparing equipment for travel'; it is said: a'dadtuhu i'dadan meaning I prepared it and brought it. Al-'Udda (with damm) means preparation and readiness, and al-'iddah is what one has prepared of money, weapons, or other things. The plural is 'udad like ghurfa and ghuraf. This is in al-Misbah al-Munir. And he said in Muntaha al-Arab: 'Udda means preparation and readiness. It is said: 'be on the 'udda,' meaning prepare. End.
[3] His saying: 'or a secluded woman...'; al-mukhaddara is from al-khidr (with fatha on kha): confining the girl to the khidr (with kasr on kha), which is a curtain extended for the girl in a corner of the house. This is in Hashiyat al-Tahtawi. And he said in al-Misbah: al-khidr is the curtain, and the plural is khudur. Khidr is used for the house if it contains a woman, otherwise not. Akhdarat al-jariyah means she kept to the khidr, and akhdarah ahluhu is transitive and intransitive, and khaddaruha with tashdid is also used. And al-Sarqasti said: akhdarat...
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn from the chapter of agency (wakalah). It details the exceptions to the general rule that an agent cannot be appointed without the opponent's consent in litigation. The general rule is that both plaintiff and defendant must be present or consent to agency; however, when the principal (muwakkil) is sick and unable to attend court, absent at a travel distance (masirat safar), intending to travel, or a secluded woman (mukhaddarah), the agent can be appointed without the opponent's consent. The reasoning is that requiring their presence would cause hardship (jarh). The commentator first explains the exception: for the sick, due to inability; for the traveler, due to absence. Then he clarifies that even if the sick can ride or be carried, agency is still binding. For those intending to travel, their statement alone is not accepted; the judge looks for external evidence like preparation (iddah). Al-Tahtawi's note from al-Muhit indicates that according to Zahir al-Riwayah, the agency is not immediately binding; rather the opponent is asked to wait or consent. Later, the text defines terms: 'iddah as preparation of travel supplies, and mukhaddarah as a woman who stays in her house behind a curtain. Various opinions are quoted about proving intention to travel, including the judge swearing the party if necessary. The passage thus provides a nuanced ruling balancing the rights of the litigants and the excuses of the principal.

Key Terms
موكل (muwakkil): The principal who appoints an agent; in this context, the person on whose behalf the agent acts in litigation.
مسيرة سفر (masirat safar): The distance of a journey that is considered travel; typically three days and nights (approx. 80 km) in Hanafi fiqh, enabling concessions like qasr salah.
مخدرة (mukhaddarah): A woman who is secluded in her home, not accustomed to going out in public; she is considered a purdah-observing woman.
عدة السفر (iddat al-safar): Preparation or equipment for travel; includes gathering supplies, money, etc., indicating genuine intention to travel.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which of the following situations may an agent be appointed without the opponent's consent according to the Hanafi school?
A) When the principal is healthy but busy.
B) When the principal is sick and unable to attend court, absent on a journey, intending to travel, or a secluded woman.
C) Only when the principal is a traveler intending to go on Hajj.
D) When the principal is a minor.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school allows agency without opponent's consent for sick, traveling, intending-to-travel, and secluded principals, with the opponent having the option to wait or consent per the relied-upon view.

Ruling on Appointing an Agent for Travel or Woman's Seclusion
Commentary on conditions necessitating appointment of an agent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a Hanafi must appoint an agent in legal claims and apply the ruling to a secluded woman's case.

Core Idea
A traveler preparing for departure or a secluded woman must appoint an agent if unable to appear in court.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri‘ayah explains when it becomes necessary (wajib) to appoint an agent in litigation. The key situations are active travel preparation and female seclusion (mukhadara). We will examine the evidence requirements for travel intention and the definition of a secluded woman, concluding with the mufta bihi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two required categories
Recognize that the text specifies a traveler engaged in preparing provisions and a secluded woman (mukhadara) not accustomed to going out as persons who must appoint an agent.
2
Understand evidence for travel intention
Mere claim of intending to travel is insufficient; the judge must see physical preparation. The text cites multiple sources confirming this.
The preparation ('uddat al-safar) is the key indicator.
3
Define 'mukhadara' and its conditions
A mukhadara is a woman who stays at home, does not frequent markets, and does not mix with men. Occasional outings for necessity do not break her seclusion.
The attribute 'not accustomed to going out' clarifies the term.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
Ibn Abi Layla restricted the ruling to virgins, but the majority (including later scholars) applies to all women who cannot speak due to shyness.
The fatwa is on the general position.
5
Conclude with mufta bihi
The relied-upon position makes it wajib for any woman who is unable to effectively present her case, regardless of marital status.
Al-Kifaya states 'upon it is the fatwa'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو مخدرة لا تعتاد الخروج. وبإيفائه، واستيفائه
وهو أن يكون مشتغلا بإعدادِ عُدَّةِ السَّفر ، أو مخدرة لا تعتاد الخروج.
وبإيفائه، واستيفائه
لكن لم يظهر ما إذا كان المدعى عليه مريضاً أو مسافراً ، وإنما قيده بقوله : مسيرة سفر ؛ لأن الغائب ما دونها كالحاضر. قوله : وهو أن يكون...الخ ؛ قال في «البحر»: إرادة السفر أمر باطني، فلا بُدَّ من دليلها، وهو إما تصديق الخصم بها أو القرينةُ الظاهرة، ولا يقبل قوله : إني أريدُ السفر، لكن ينظر القاضي في حاله وفي عدّته، فإنّه لا يخفى عدة من يسافر. انتهى. وفي «البزازية»: وإن قال : اخرجُ بالقافلة الفلانية، سألهم عنه، وفي خزانة المفتين»: لو قال: إنّي أريد السفر يلزم منه التوكيل طالباً كان أو مطلوباً، لكن يكفل المطلوب ؛ ليتمكن الطالب من استيفاء دينه ، وإن كذبه الخصم يحلفه القاضي : بالله إنك تريد السفر. انتهى. قوله : بإعداد عُدة السفر ؛ يقال : أعددته إعداداً : هيأته وأحضرته، والعُدة بالضم : الاستعداد والتأهب والعدّة ما أعددته من مال أو سلاح أو غير ذلك، والجمع عُدَد، مثل غرفةٍ وغرف . كذا في «المصباح المنير»، وقال في «منتهى الأرب»: عدة بالضم ساز و ساخت يقال: كونوا على عدة ، يعني : برا ما دلي ياشيد. انتهى. قوله : أو مخدّرة ... الخ ؛ المخدّرة من الخدر بفتح الخاء : إلزام البنت الخدر بكسر الخاء، وهو ستريمدُّ للجارية في ناحية البيت. كذا في حاشية الطحطاوي) وقال في «المصباح» : الخدر : هو الستر ، والجمع : خُدور، ويطلق الخدر على البيت إن كان فيه امرأة، وإلا فلا، وأخدَرَتِ الجارية لزمت الخدر، وأخدرها أهلها يتعدى ولا يتعدى ، وخدَّروها بالتثقيل أيضاً وبالتخفيف، وقال السَّرْقُسطي : أخدرتِ الجارية: لزمت خدرها، وأخدرتها : إذ يستعمل لازماً ومتعدّياً، والمعنى: ستروها وصانوها عن الامتهان والخروج لقضاء حوائجها. انتهى.
فالحاصل : إنَّ المخدرة هي التي لا تخرج عن بيتها إلى الأسواق غالباً، ولم تخالط مع الرجال، فإنَّ الخروج للحاجة لا يقدح في تخديرها ما لم يكثر، بأن تخرج لغير حاجة، صرح به في «البَزَّازيّة»، فقوله : لا تعتاد الخروج صفة كاشفة لقوله : مخدرة. قال في «الهداية» : لو كانت المرأة مخدرة لم تجر عادتها بالبروز وحضور مجلس الحاكم، قال الرازي له : يلزم التوكيل ؛ لأنها لو حضرت لا يمكنها أن تنطق بحقها لحيائها، فيلزم توكيلها ، قال له : وهذا شيء استحسنه المتأخرون. انتهى.
وقال في «الكفاية» : وعليه الفتوى. انتهى. وقال في «الفتح» : أما على ظاهرِ إطلاق الأصل» وغيره عن أبي حنيفة له ، فلا فرق بين البكر والنيب والمخدرة والبرزة، والفتوى على ما اختاروه من ذلك. انتهى. وفي «العناية : قال ابن أبي ليلى ه : تقبل من البكر دون الثيب. انتهى.
Or a secluded woman who is not accustomed to going out. And with his payment and his collection. That is, he is occupied with preparing travel provisions, or a secluded woman not accustomed to going out. And with his payment and his collection. But it is not clear if the defendant is sick or traveling, and he only restricted it by his saying: a journey's distance; because one absent less than that is like a present. His saying: 'That is, he is...' etc.; said in 'Al-Bahr': Intention to travel is an internal matter, so there must be evidence for it, either the opponent's affirmation or a clear indication, and his saying 'I intend to travel' is not accepted, rather the judge looks at his condition and his preparation, for the preparation of one who travels is not hidden. End. And in 'Al-Bazzaziyya': If he says: 'I am leaving with such-and-such caravan', he asks them about him. And in 'Khizanat al-Muftiyyin': If he says: 'I intend to travel', it necessitates appointment of an agent whether he is claimant or defendant, but the defendant gives a guarantee so that the claimant can collect his debt, and if the opponent denies, the judge makes him swear by Allah that he intends to travel. End. His saying: 'by preparing travel provisions'; it is said: 'a'dadtahu i'dadan': I prepared and readied it, and 'al-'udda' (with damma): preparation and readiness, and 'al-'idda' is what you prepare of money, weapons, or other things, its plural is 'udud' like 'ghurfa' and 'ghuraf'. So in 'Al-Misbah al-Munir'. And said in 'Muntaha al-Arab': 'Udda bil-damm' means equipment and preparation; it is said: 'be on readiness', meaning: prepare for what you need. End. His saying: 'or a secluded woman...' etc.; 'al-mukhadara' from 'al-khidr' (with fath al-kha'): confining the girl to the 'khidr' (with kasr al-kha'), which is a curtain extended for the girl in a corner of the house. So in Hashiyat al-Tahatawi. And said in 'Al-Misbah': 'al-khidr' is the curtain, plural 'khudur', and 'khidr' is used for the house if there is a woman in it, otherwise not. 'Akhadarat al-jariya': she kept to her curtain, and 'akhdaraha ahliha' is transitive and intransitive, and 'khaddaruha' with tashdid also and with takhfif. And Al-Sarqasti said: 'Akhadarat al-jariya': she kept to her curtain, and 'akhdaraha' is used as both transitive and intransitive, meaning: they veiled her and protected her from being demeaned and from going out for her needs. End. So the conclusion: The secluded woman is one who does not go out of her house to the markets often and does not mix with men. Going out for a need does not harm her seclusion as long as it is not excessive, such as going out without need, as stated in 'Al-Bazzaziyya'. So his saying 'not accustomed to going out' is a descriptive attribute for 'secluded woman'. Said in 'Al-Hidaya': If a woman is secluded, not accustomed to appearing and attending the judge's court, Al-Razi said: She must appoint an agent; because if she attends, she cannot speak her right due to her shyness, so she must appoint an agent. He said: This is something approved by later scholars. End. And said in 'Al-Kifaya': And upon it is the fatwa. End. And said in 'Al-Fath': As for the apparent meaning of 'Al-Asl' and others from Abu Hanifa, may he have mercy, there is no difference between a virgin, a non-virgin, a secluded woman, and one who appears, and the fatwa is according to what they chose from that. End. And in 'Al-Inaya': Ibn Abi Layla said: It is accepted from a virgin but not from a non-virgin. End.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the conditions that necessitate the appointment of an agent in judicial proceedings. The original text states that a person who is preparing for travel (mashghul bi i'dad 'uddat al-safar) or a secluded woman (mukhadara) who is not accustomed to going out must appoint an agent. The commentator explains that the intention to travel is internal and must be evidenced by either the opponent's admission or clear indications such as preparing provisions. The judge does not accept a mere claim of intention to travel but investigates the person's readiness. Similarly, for a woman who is secluded (mukhadara), the commentary defines the term and notes that if she is shy to appear in court, she must appoint an agent. The fatwa is that this applies to all women regardless of marital status, though Ibn Abi Layla restricted it to virgins. The Hanafi position is that the agent can both pay and receive payments on behalf of the principal.

Key Terms
مخدرة (mukhadara): A woman who stays in her home, does not go out to markets, and does not mix with men; her seclusion is not harmed by occasional outings for necessity.
عُدَّة السفر (‘uddat al-safar): Preparation and equipment for travel, such as food, weapons, and supplies.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) She must personally attend court as the default rule.
B) She must appoint an agent if she cannot speak due to shyness.
C) She may choose to appear or appoint an agent.
D) Only a virgin woman must appoint an agent.

Key Takeaway
A person actively preparing for travel or a secluded woman who cannot speak in court must appoint an agent; the fatwa includes all such women.

Rights of the Agent in Contracts: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Compares Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on rights from contracts executed by an agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the allocation of contractual rights in agency and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position, identifying the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
When an agent contracts in his own name, he holds all rights of the contract; the principal cannot assert them during the agent's lifetime.

Agency (wakalah) involves a principal appointing an agent to act on his behalf. A critical question arises: when the agent executes a contract attributing it to himself, who owns the resulting rights and obligations? The Hanafi and Shafi'i schools diverge sharply. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling, contrasts it with the Shafi'i view, and explores the practical legal consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and parties
The agent executes a contract (sale, lease, or settlement) and attributes it to himself (e.g., 'I sold'). Determine who is the direct contractual party according to the Hanafi school.
The agent does not mention the principal; the contract is in his own name.
2
List the specific rights mentioned
The passage lists rights: receiving the price, litigating defects, claiming pre-emption, and suing for the price. According to Hanafi, who holds these rights?
These rights belong to the agent as long as he holds the property.
3
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that these rights revert to the principal immediately. What is the basis of this opposing view?
The Shafi'i view sees the agent as a mere representative; the principal is the true owner.
4
Analyse the Hanafi justification
The Hanafi ruling is built on the principle that the agent, by attributing the contract to himself, acts as an original party (asil) in rights of the contract. How does this logic yield the ruling?
This is a core difference in the nature of agency: agent as direct party vs agent as intermediary.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference in real-world application? For example, who can sue for a defect or claim the price while the agent is alive?
Under Hanafi, only the agent can litigate; under Shafi'i, the principal can act directly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحقوق عقد يضيفه الوكيل إلى نفسه كبيع وإجارة وصلح عن إقرار يتعلق به فيسلم المبيع ويقبضه. وثمن مبيعه. ويطالب بثمن مشريّه ، ويخاصم في عيبه ، وشفعة ما باع، وهو في يده ، فإن سلمه إلى آمره فلا يُرَدُّ بالعيب، إلا بإذنه، ويرجع بثمن مشريه مستحقا) : هذا كله عندنا، وعند الشَّافِعِي يرجعُ الحقوق إلى الموكل
And rights of a contract that the agent attributes to himself, like sale, lease, and settlement regarding an admission that pertains to it, so he hands over the sold item and takes possession of it. And the price of what he sold. And he demands the price of what he purchased, and he litigates regarding its defect, and the pre-emption of what he sold while it is in his hand. Then if he hands it over to his principal, it cannot be returned due to defect without his permission. And he claims the price of what he purchased as being entitled to it. This is all according to us (Hanafi), and according to al-Shafi'i the rights revert to the principal.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on agency (wakalah) presents the Hanafi ruling that when an agent executes a contract (e.g., sale, lease, or settlement), he attributes it to himself (e.g., saying 'I sold') without needing to mention the principal. As a result, all rights and obligations arising from the contract—such as receiving the price, litigating defects, claiming pre-emption, and suing for the price—belong to the agent as long as he holds the property. If he delivers the property to the principal, he cannot return it for a defect without the principal's permission, and his authority ends. The text then contrasts this with the Shafi'i position, where these rights revert to the principal immediately. The practical consequence: under Hanafi law, the principal cannot directly claim the price or sue until the agent hands over the rights; the agent is the proper party in legal proceedings. This difference stems from the Hanafi view that the agent, by attributing the contract to himself, becomes the direct party, while the Shafi'i view considers the principal as the true party.

Key Terms
وكيل (wakīl): An agent appointed to act on behalf of another (the principal) in legal transactions.
حقوق عقد (ḥuqūq ʿaqd): The legal rights and obligations arising from a contract, such as price, possession, and litigation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The principal immediately
B) The agent during his lifetime
C) The buyer
D) It is shared between agent and principal

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that an agent who contracts in his own name retains all contractual rights during his lifetime, differing from the Shafi'i school which gives these rights to the principal.

First Type of Agency: Agent Cannot Be Compelled
States the first type of agency actions and the ruling on compulsion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for the first type of agency actions and explain why the agent cannot be compelled.

Core Idea
The agent is a volunteer in the first type of agency; he cannot be forced to act.

This passage introduces the first category of actions in agency (wakalah) where the agent has authority but no compulsion. Understanding this distinction is crucial for knowing the rights and limitations of an agent. The Hanafi school considers the agent a volunteer (mutabarri‘), so if he refuses, the principal must appoint another agent rather than compel him.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actions in the first type
List the four actions mentioned: receiving the sold item, demanding the price from the buyer, disputing a defect, and seeking restitution for an appropriated item.
These are non-essential actions where the agent's role is facilitative.
2
Note the agent's authority and limitation
The agent has authority (wilayah) over these actions but is not compelled; he is a volunteer (mutabarri‘).
The key point: authority does not imply compulsion.
3
Understand the reason for no compulsion
The agent is mutabarri‘ — he undertook the agency voluntarily without legal obligation. Hence he cannot be forced.
Compare with obligatory acts where compulsion may apply.
4
Know the remedy if the agent refuses
The principal appoints another agent. He cannot perform the actions himself without a new delegation.
This contrasts with the second type of agency (not covered here).
5
Recognize the Shafi'i disagreement
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the principal retains authority to perform these actions directly without delegation from the agent or his heirs.
This difference affects the principal's options.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالأَوَّلُ : كقبض المبيع، والمطالبة بثمن المشتري، والمخاصمة في العيب، والرجوع بثمن المستحق، ففي هذا النوع للوكيل ولاية هذه الأمور، لكن لا يُحِبُّ عليه، فإن امتنع لا يُجيرُهُ الموكل على هذه الأفعال ؛ لأنه متبرع في العمل، بل يوكل الموكل لهذه الأفعال، وسيأتي في كتاب المضاربة» بعض هذا، وهو قوله : وكذا سائر الوكلاء. وإن مات الوكيل فولاية هذه الأفعال لورثته، فإن امتنعوا، وَكُلُوا موكل مورثهم، وعند الشَّافِعِي الله للموكل ولاية هذه الأفعال بلا توكيل من الوكيل، أو وارثه.
The first type: like receiving the sold item, demanding the price from the buyer, disputing a defect, and seeking restitution for an appropriated item. In this type, the agent has authority over these matters, but he is not compelled. If he refuses, the principal cannot force him to perform these actions because the agent is a volunteer in the work. Rather, the principal appoints another agent for these actions. And it will come in the book of Mudarabah some of this, which is his statement: 'and likewise all agents.' If the agent dies, then the authority over these actions goes to his heirs. If they refuse, they appoint an agent for the principal's heir. According to al-Shafi'i, the principal has authority over these actions without needing a delegation from the agent or his heir.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the chapter on agency (wakalah). It categorizes the actions an agent may be authorized to perform into two types. The first type includes actions where the agent has authority (wilayah) but cannot be compelled; he is a volunteer (mutabarri'). If he refuses, the principal must appoint another agent. If the agent dies, his heirs inherit this authority; if they refuse, they appoint another agent for the principal's heir. The passage notes that Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, holding that the principal retains the authority to perform these actions directly without needing a new appointment from the agent or his heir. This is a brief mention of the inter-madhhab difference, without the full proof here.

Key Terms
وكيل (wakīl): An agent appointed by the principal to perform a specific action on his behalf.
موكل (muwakkil): The principal who appoints the agent.
ولاية (wilāyah): Legal authority or power to act.
متبرع (mutabarri‘): One who acts voluntarily without legal obligation or compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first type of agency actions, if the agent refuses to act, what should the principal do according to the Hanafi school?
A) Compel the agent by court order
B) Appoint another agent
C) Perform the action himself without permission
D) Cancel the agency contract

Key Takeaway
The first type of agency actions gives the agent authority but no compulsion because he is a volunteer; the principal must appoint a new agent if needed.

Second Type of Agency Actions: Agent Can Be Compelled as Defendant
Explains the second type where the agent is a defendant and can be compelled, with Hanafi proof and response to objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that the agent can be compelled in the second type of agency actions and evaluate the opposing argument.

Core Idea
In the second type, the agent is defendant; as principal in rights, he can be compelled despite being a volunteer.

In the previous lesson, we learned about the first type of agency where the agent is the plaintiff and cannot be compelled. Now we examine the second type where the agent is the defendant. The Hanafi school distinguishes this type, arguing that the agent, by his speech, effects the contract and becomes the principal in rights, thus can be compelled. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning and the response to objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of agency actions
Recall that in the first type the agent is the plaintiff (claimant), while in the second type the agent is the defendant. This distinction determines whether compulsion is possible.
This is based on who is being sued – the agent or the principal.
2
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that in the second type, the agent can be compelled to perform obligations like delivery or payment because he is the defendant. The text says 'الوكيل مدعى عليه'.
Compulsion here means the court can force the agent to act.
3
Analyze the opposing argument
Opponents (possibly Shafi‘i) argue that rights follow ownership, which belongs to the principal. Thus, the agent should not be liable. They compare the agent to a mere messenger.
Their proof: the contract's legal effect (ownership) attaches to the principal.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: the contract is effected by the agent's speech, so he is the principal (asīl) in rights. The principal is only a deputy (nā’ib) in the ruling. Therefore, the agent is directly liable. The distinction between first and second types is based on whether the agent is plaintiff or defendant.
This response is built on the idea that the agent's speech gives him a real stake in the contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي النوع الآخر : الوكيلُ مدَّعى عليه، فللمدعي أن يُجبر الوكيل على تسليم المبيع، وتسليم الثَّمن وأخواتهما. السمني ) ، ودليلهم أنَّ الحقوق تابعة لحكم التصرف، والحكم في هذه التصرفات ، وهو الملك يتعلق بالموكل فكذا ،تابعه، وصار الوكيل هاهنا كمن قال له آخر : كن رسولي في بيع عبدي مثلاً، وكالوكيل في النكاح. ولنا : إنَّ العقد يحصل بالكلام الصادر من الوكيل وقضية هذا أن يكون الحاصل بالتصرف واقعاً له، إلا أنَّ الموكل لما استنابه في تحصيل حكم ذلك التصرف جعلناه نائباً في حق الحكم فقط كيلا يبطل مقصوده فيبقى الوكيل أصيلاً في حق الحقوق، ثم هو يستغني عن إضافة العقد إلى الموكل ، ولو لم يكن أصيلاً في العقد، وكان كالرسول والوكيل في النكاح لما استغنى عن ذلك، وإن كان أصيلاً في حق الحقوق جاز تثبيت توكيله فيها، ولا يجوز توكيلُ الموكل فيها ؛ لأنه أجنبي عنها. [1]قوله : وفي النوع الآخر...الخ ؛ ويظهرُ منه الجواب عمّا أورده بعضُهم من أنَّ الوكيل متبرع في التوكيل مطلقاً، فما وجه الفرق في النوع الأول والثاني حتى لا يجبره الموكل في الأوّل، ويجبره في الثاني. وتصوير الجواب : أنا سلّمنا أنَّ إلتزام الوكالة تبرُّعٌ من الوكيل، لكنَّه بالنسبة إلى الموكل، ومعناه أنه لو لم يلتزمها لم يجبر عليها ، أما إذا التزمها فأعماله لا تخلو : إما أن (۱) في «كمال الدراية»(ق٥٠٨).
And in the second type: the agent is the defendant, so the plaintiff can compel the agent to deliver the sold item, deliver the price, and similar matters. (The Samani?) and their proof is that rights are subordinate to the ruling of the transaction, and the ruling in these transactions—which is ownership—is attached to the principal, so likewise its subordinate. And the agent here becomes like one to whom another said: 'Be my messenger in selling my slave, for example,' and like the agent in marriage. And for us: The contract is effected by the speech emanating from the agent, and the implication of this is that what is attained by the transaction belongs to him, except that since the principal appointed him to obtain the ruling of that transaction, we made him a deputy only with respect to the ruling so that his purpose is not invalidated. Thus, the agent remains the principal in terms of rights. Then he is independent of attributing the contract to the principal. If he were not the principal in the contract, and were like a messenger and the agent in marriage, he would not be independent of that. And since he is principal in terms of rights, it is permissible to affirm his agency therein, and it is not permissible for the principal to be an agent therein because he is a stranger to them. [Commentary]: His statement: 'And in the second type... etc.' and from it appears the answer to what some raised: that the agent is a volunteer in agency absolutely, so what is the difference between the first and second types such that the principal cannot compel him in the first but can compel him in the second? The answer: we grant that undertaking wakalah is a volunteered act by the agent, but it is relative to the principal. Its meaning is that if he had not undertaken it, he would not be compelled to do so. However, once he undertakes it, his actions are not free from: either they make him a defendant or not.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and an inter-madhhab dispute. It addresses the second type of agency actions where the agent is the defendant (mudda‘a ‘alayh). The text first presents the opposing view (possibly Shafi‘i) that the agent can be compelled because rights follow ownership, which is for the principal. The Hanafi response (ولنا) argues that the contract is attributed to the agent's speech; he is the principal (asīl) in rights, while the principal is only a deputy (nā’ib) in the legal ruling. Therefore, the agent can be compelled directly. The commentary resolves the objection: if the agent is a volunteer, why is he compelled here but not in the first type? The answer distinguishes based on whether the agent is a defendant or a plaintiff. In this type, the agent is the defendant, so compulsion is justified. This distinction is key in Hanafi agency law.

Key Terms
مدعى عليه (mudda‘ā ‘alayh): The defendant in a legal claim; the one against whom a claim is made.
أصيل (aṣīl): The principal party in a transaction, as opposed to a deputy or agent.
نائب (nā’ib): Deputy or representative who acts on behalf of another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the key factor that allows the agent to be compelled in the second type of agency?
A) The agent owns the property
B) The agent is the defendant
C) The principal is absent
D) The agent is a paid agent

Key Takeaway
In the second type of agency, the agent is considered the principal in rights due to his speech effecting the contract, and thus he can be compelled as defendant.

Ownership in Agency: Direct Vesting or Transfer?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ownership in agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on where ownership vests when an agent purchases goods, and apply the relied-upon view to determine the consequences if the agent buys his own relative.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, ownership of purchased goods vests directly in the principal, not the agent.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: when an agent (wakil) purchases an item on behalf of his principal (muwakkil), does ownership vest initially in the agent and then transfer, or does it vest directly in the principal? The relied-upon view has practical implications, especially regarding the freeing of slaves and validity of marriages. Let's dissect the arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing views
Al-Karkhi holds ownership vests in the agent first; Abu Tahir al-Dabbas holds it vests directly in the principal. The text favours the latter as 'al-asahh'.
2
Analyse the proof (dalil) for each view
Al-Karkhi's proof: the cause of ownership (the contract) is from the agent. Abu Tahir's proof: practical consequences—if agent owned, buying his relative would free slaves/invalidate marriage, but no scholar says that.
This is a talil (reasoning by consequence) argument.
3
Understand the analogy with the slave
The agent is like a slave who acquires property: ownership vests in the master directly, not the slave. Similarly, the principal replaces the agent (khilafah) in ownership.
4
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If an agent buys his own father (a slave), according to al-Karkhi the father would be freed (since agent owns him), but according to the relied-upon view he is not freed because ownership is with the principal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويثبت الملك للموكل ابتداءً، فلا يُعْتَقَ قريب وكيل شراه : أي إذا اشترى الوكيل، فالأصح أن يثبت الملك للموكل ابتداء، وعند بعض المشايخ له يثبت الملك أولاً للوكيل ، ثم يَنْتَقِلُ منه إلى موكله بسبب عقد يجري بينهما، وإن لم يكن ملفوظاً، بل مقتضى للتوكيل السابق [ ١ ] قوله : ويثبت الملك...الخ ؛ يعني يثبت الملك للموكل خلافة ، وبدلاً عن الوكيل، باعتبار التوكيل السابق ، لا أن يثبت للوكيل فينتقل إلى الموكل كما زعم الشافعي ، فالحاصل أن الوكيل أصيل في حق الكل، لكن في حق الملك يخلفه الموكل، فيقع له من غير أن يكون أصيلاً فيه، كما أنَّ العبد يقبلُ الهبة ويصطاد، والملك يثبت للمولى ابتداءً خلافة عن العبد، والمولى يقوم مقامه بذلك السبب. [٢] قوله : فالأصح ...الخ ؛ توضيحه : إنَّ العلماء اختلفوا في هذه المسألة، فقال الكَرْخي : يثبتُ الملك للوكيل لتحققِ السبب من جهته، ثم ينتقل إلى الموكل بسبب عقد يجري بينهما، وإن لم يكن ملفوظا بل مقتضى التوكيل السابق. وقال أبو طاهر الدباس : يثبت الملك ابتداء للموكل، وهذا هو الأصح ؛ لأنَّ المشترى إذا كان منكوحة الوكيل أو قريبه لا يفسد النكاح، ولا يعتق عليه، ولو ملك المشتري فلا بد من أن يعتق عليه، ويفسد النكاح، ولم يقل به أحد.
And ownership is established for the principal initially, so the relative of an agent who purchased [a slave] is not freed. Meaning: When the agent purchases, the correct view is that ownership is established for the principal from the beginning. According to some mashayikh, ownership is established first for the agent, then it transfers to his principal by a contract that occurs between them, even if not verbal, but rather by the implication of the previous appointment. [1] His statement: 'And ownership is established... etc.' means that ownership is established for the principal as a substitution (khalafan) and instead of the agent, by virtue of the previous appointment, not that it is established for the agent then transfers to the principal as al-Shafi‘i claimed. The conclusion: the agent is the principal (asīl) in all respects, but with regard to ownership, the principal replaces him (yakhlufuhu), so it falls to him without the agent being a principal in it, just as a slave accepts a gift and hunts, and ownership is established for the master from the beginning as a substitution for the slave, and the master stands in his place by that reason. [2] His statement: 'the correct view... etc.' its clarification: The scholars differed on this issue. Al-Karkhi said: Ownership is established for the agent because the cause is realized from him, then it transfers to the principal by a contract between them, even if not verbal, but rather by implication of the previous appointment. Abu Tahir al-Dabbas said: Ownership is established for the principal directly, and this is the correct view because if the purchased item is the wife or relative of the agent, it does not invalidate the marriage nor does it make the agent a freedman. If the agent had owned it, then it would necessarily cause [the agent's] freedom and invalidate the marriage, and no one has held that.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the ownership of property purchased by an agent. The matn states that ownership vests in the principal directly (ibtida'an), and the commentary explains that this is the correct view (al-asahh). Two positions are presented: that of al-Karkhi, who holds that ownership vests in the agent first and then transfers to the principal automatically by virtue of the appointment, and that of Abu Tahir al-Dabbas, who holds that ownership vests in the principal directly. The reliance on Abu Tahir's view is supported by a practical consequence: if the agent buys his own close relative, such as his wife or a mahram, if ownership had vested in the agent, that relative would be freed (if a slave) or the marriage would be invalidated (if a wife). Since none of the scholars say that such effects occur, it proves that ownership does not vest in the agent. This is a standard Hanafi argument using the absence of a necessary consequence (an 'adam al-mukharrij). The commentary also compares the agent to a slave: the slave's acquisition vests in the master directly.

Key Terms
ملك (milk): Legal ownership of property.
خلافة (khilāfah): Substitution; one person standing in the place of another in legal ownership.
أصيل (aṣīl): The principal party in a transaction; here the agent is considered aṣīl in rights but not in ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The father becomes free because the agent owned him
B) The father does not become free because ownership vests in the principal directly
C) The purchase is invalid
D) The agent must free his father

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, ownership of goods purchased by an agent vests directly in the principal, not the agent, based on the relied-upon view of Abu Tahir al-Dabbas.

Ownership in Agency: The Principal Owns Directly
Explaining the ruling that ownership vests in the principal, not the agent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on who owns an item purchased by an agent and explain the proof using the analogy with a slave.

Core Idea
In Hanafi agency, the principal owns directly — the agent is merely a substitute in ownership acquisition.

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya' clarifies the fundamental rule of ownership in agency (wakala). When an agent purchases an item, the ownership vests directly in the principal (muwakkil) from the moment of contract. The agent never becomes the owner. This ruling is established by analogy with a slave's acquisitions and is reinforced by the unacceptable consequence of agent-ownership (automatic emancipation of relatives). Let's examine the proofs and the internal Hanafi debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The matn states: 'ownership is established for the principal initially' (يثبت الملك للموكل ابتداءً). This is the foundational ruling of the chapter.
Note the phrase 'ابتداءً' (initially) — it emphasizes that the principal owns from the outset, not after a transfer.
2
Trace the proof
The commentator presents the proof via analogy (qiyas): Just as a slave (a property) who accepts a gift or hunts acquires ownership for his master directly, the agent (acting for the principal) acquires ownership for the principal directly. The agent is a substitute (khalifah) in the act of acquisition.
Key phrase: 'كما أن العبد يقبل الهبة ويصطاد، والملك يثبت للمولى ابتداءً'.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Al-Karkhi argued that ownership first vests in the agent because the cause (sabab) — the contract — originates from him, then transfers to the principal via an implicit contract. Abu Tahir al-Dabbas held the direct view. The commentator declares Abu Tahir's view as 'al-ashah' (most correct).
This is an ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school, not between madhhabs.
4
Apply the consequence test
If the agent bought his own relative (e.g., his father who is a slave), would the relative be emancipated? Ownership of a relative triggers automatic emancipation. Both views agree the relative is not freed. For Abu Tahir, because the agent never owned; for al-Karkhi, because the agent's ownership was unstable (غير متقرر) and did not cause emancipation. This practical agreement supports Abu Tahir's view as more coherent.
The consequence test shows that even al-Karkhi's view must deny emancipation, suggesting his position is convoluted.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The commentator explicitly says 'وهذا هو الأصح' (this is the most correct). Therefore, the operative ruling (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is that ownership vests directly in the principal.
This ruling is from the zahir al-riwayah and is relied upon in fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوكالة تعريفها وأحكامها
ويثبت الملك للموكل ابتداءً، فلا يُعْتَقَ قريب وكيل شراه
( ويثبت الملك للموكل ابتداءً، فلا يُعْتَقَ قريب وكيل شراه) : أي إذا اشترى الوكيل، فالأصح أن يثبت الملك للموكل ابتداء، وعند بعض المشايخ له يثبت الملك أولاً للوكيل ، ثم يَنْتَقِلُ منه إلى موكله بسبب عقد يجري بينهما، وإن لم يكن ملفوظاً، بل مقتضى للتوكيل السابق
يوجب كونه مدعى عليه، أو لا.
فإن كان ؛ يوجبُ، فيستلزم الجبر عليه لتسليم المبيع، إذا كان وكيلاً بالبيع، وتسليم الثمن إذا كان وكيلاً بالثمن، فإنّهما يجبران عليه لا محالة بالعقد الذي التزم ولايته باختياره لتيسرها، وهذا قضية كونه مدعى عليه، فإنَّ المدعى عليه الذي يجبر عليه، وإن لم يرد الخصومة. وإن كان ؛ لا يوجب، فلا يستلزم الجبر عليه، كقبض المبيع إذا كان وكيلاً بالشراء، ومطالبة الثمن إذا كان وكيلاً بالبيع ؛ لأنَّ الوكيل في هذه الصورة يكون مدعياً، والمدعي لا يجبر عليه، فلا يجبرُ عليه الموكل ؛ لأنه متبرع في حقه فلا يستحق كونه مجبوراً ولا غيره ؛ لأنَّ المدعي هو الذي إذا ترك ترك فلا يؤخذ منه. [ ١ ] قوله : ويثبت الملك...الخ ؛ يعني يثبت الملك للموكل خلافة ، وبدلاً عن الوكيل، باعتبار التوكيل السابق ، لا أن يثبت للوكيل فينتقل إلى الموكل كما زعم الشافعي ، فالحاصل أن الوكيل أصيل في حق الكل، لكن في حق الملك يخلفه الموكل، فيقع له من غير أن يكون أصيلاً فيه، كما أنَّ العبد يقبلُ الهبة ويصطاد، والملك يثبت للمولى ابتداءً خلافة عن العبد، والمولى يقوم مقامه بذلك السبب. [٢] قوله : فالأصح ...الخ ؛ توضيحه : إنَّ العلماء اختلفوا في هذه المسألة، فقال الكَرْخي : يثبتُ الملك للوكيل لتحققِ السبب من جهته، ثم ينتقل إلى الموكل بسبب عقد يجري بينهما، وإن لم يكن ملفوظا بل مقتضى التوكيل السابق.
وقال أبو طاهر الدباس : يثبت الملك ابتداء للموكل، وهذا هو الأصح ؛ لأنَّ المشترى إذا كان منكوحة الوكيل أو قريبه لا يفسد النكاح، ولا يعتق عليه، ولو ملك المشتري فلا بد من أن يعتق عليه، ويفسد النكاح، ولم يقل به أحد.
فعلى التخريج الأول : إذا وَكَّلَ أحداً أن يشتري قريبه من مالكه ، فاشتراه لا يعتقُ على الوكيل ؛ لأنه لا يملكه، وعلى التّخريج الثَّاني : لا يعتقُ أَيضاً ؛ لأَنَّهُ يَثْبُتُ للوكيل ملك غير متقرِّرِ فلا يعتق. [1]قوله : فعلى التخريج الأول...الخ يعني إذا وكل أحدٌ أحداً أن يشتري قريبه من مالکه فاشتراه لا يعتق على ما ذهب إليه أبو طاهر الله له ، واختاره في «المتن» ؛ لأنَّ الوكيل لم يملكه بل ثبت ملكه للموكل ابتداء.
وعلى ما ذهب إليه الكرخي الله ينبغي أن يعتق ؛ لأن الوكيل ملكه أولاً ثم انتقل ملكه إلى الموكل ، لكن لا يعتق ؛ لأنه وإن ثبت للوكيل ملكه، لكنه غير متقرّر ، والموجب للعتق . هو الملك المتقرّر، وهكذا أجاب الكرخي الله فلم يظهر لهذا الاختلاف ثمرة ؛ لكون عدم العتق متفقاً عليه، فالأولى أن يفرّع عليه ما يظهر فيه أثراً لخلاف.
فتدبر.
Book of Agency: Its definition and rulings. And ownership is established for the principal initially, so the relative of an agent who purchases (him) is not emancipated. (And ownership is established for the principal initially, so the relative of an agent who purchases him is not emancipated): Meaning, when the agent buys, the most correct (view) is that ownership is established for the principal initially, and according to some scholars ownership is first established for the agent, then transfers from him to his principal due to a contract that takes place between them, even if not uttered, but rather by implication of the prior agency. It necessitates whether he is a defendant or not. If it necessitates, then it entails compulsion upon him to deliver the sold item if he is an agent for sale, and payment of the price if he is an agent for purchase; they are both compelled without fail by the contract which he undertook by his choice due to its feasibility. This is the case of being a defendant, for the defendant is compelled even if he does not want litigation. But if it does not necessitate, then it does not entail compulsion, like taking possession of the sold item if he is an agent for purchase, and demanding the price if he is an agent for sale; because the agent in this case is a plaintiff, and the plaintiff is not compelled, so the principal is not compelled because he is a volunteer on his behalf, so he does not deserve to be compelled or otherwise; because the plaintiff is one who if he abandons (the claim) he is left alone and nothing is taken from him. [1] His saying: 'And ownership is established...' etc.; meaning ownership is established for the principal as a substitution and in place of the agent, by virtue of the prior agency, not that it is established for the agent and then transfers to the principal as al-Shafi'i claimed. The conclusion is that the agent is an original party in all rights, but in the matter of ownership the principal substitutes him, so it occurs for him without him being an original party in it, just as a slave accepts a gift and hunts, and ownership is established for the master initially as a substitution from the slave, and the master stands in his place by that cause. [2] His saying: 'The most correct (view)...' etc.; its clarification: The scholars differed in this issue. Al-Karkhi said: Ownership is established for the agent due to the cause being realized from his side, then it transfers to the principal due to a contract that takes place between them, even if not uttered, but rather by implication of the prior agency. Abu Tahir al-Dabbas said: Ownership is established initially for the principal, and this is the most correct; because if the purchased item were the agent's wife or relative, the marriage would not be invalidated and he would not be emancipated (by owning him), but if the purchaser owned (him), then necessarily he would be emancipated and the marriage would be invalidated, and no one has said that. According to the first derivation: If someone appoints an agent to buy his relative from his owner, and he buys him, it does not count as emancipation for the agent because he does not own him. According to the second derivation: It also does not count as emancipation because the ownership established for the agent is not stable. [1] His saying: 'According to the first derivation...' etc.; means if someone appoints another to buy his relative from his owner, and he buys him, it does not count as emancipation according to what Abu Tahir held, and it is chosen in the text; because the agent does not own him, rather his ownership is established for the principal initially. And according to what al-Karkhi held, it should count as emancipation because the agent first owned him then his ownership transferred to the principal, but it does not count as emancipation because although ownership is established for the agent, it is not stable, and the cause for emancipation is stable ownership. Thus al-Karkhi answered, so this difference does not produce a practical consequence because the non-emancipation is agreed upon. It is more appropriate to apply it to something where the effect of the difference appears. So ponder.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of al-Wiqayah dealing with the ruling on ownership in agency. The matn states that when an agent purchases an item, ownership is established for the principal (muwakkil) directly, and therefore if the agent buys his own relative, that relative does not become free (since the agent never owned him). The commentator then explains the different views within the Hanafi school. The most correct view (al-ashah) is that of Abu Tahir al-Dabbas: ownership vests in the principal from the start. Al-Karkhi held that ownership first vests in the agent and then transfers to the principal via an implicit contract. The commentator provides proof for Abu Tahir's view by reasoning: if the agent owned the item, then buying his own relative would automatically free that relative (since a person cannot own his own relative), but no jurist holds that, so the agent cannot be the owner. He also draws an analogy with a slave who accepts a gift or hunts: the ownership goes directly to the master, not the slave. The passage also discusses the practical consequence: when the agent buys a relative of the principal, both views agree that the relative is not freed (because either the agent didn't own him, or his ownership was unstable). Thus the difference has no practical effect in this specific case, but the commentator notes that in other cases the difference may matter. The preferred position is Abu Tahir's, and this is the relied-upon view in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
الوكيل (al-wakil): The agent who acts on behalf of another (the principal) in a legal transaction.
الموكل (al-muwakkil): The principal who appoints an agent.
الملك (al-milk): Ownership of the purchased item; the right of possession and disposition.
العتق (al-'itq): Emancipation; freeing a slave.
التخريج (al-takhrij): The legal derivation or application of a principle; here it refers to the two views (Karkhi and Abu Tahir).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The agent (wakil)
B) The principal (muwakkil)
C) Both jointly
D) It depends on the intention

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi agency, ownership vests directly in the principal, not the agent, based on the analogy with a slave's acquisitions and the unacceptable consequence of agent-ownership.

Contracts Requiring Attribution to the Principal
Listing contracts requiring attribution to principal in agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which contracts an agent must attribute to the principal and explain the exception for divorce and manumission as message.

Core Idea
Certain contracts demand explicit attribution to the principal; divorce and manumission allow a messenger mode.

This passage from the Hanafi fiqh text lists specific contracts that an agent cannot validly perform unless he clearly attributes them to his principal. Knowing this list and the exception for divorce/manumission is essential for correctly executing agency transactions. We will examine the list, the requirement of idafah, and the nuance of risalah.

Guided Path
1
List the contracts requiring attribution
Identify each contract mentioned in the passage: marriage, khul', settlement upon denial, intentional blood-money, manumission for consideration, kitabah, hibah, sadaqah, i'arah, wadi'ah, rahn. Memorize this list as a core category.
These are contracts where the agent's own dhamma cannot substitute for the principal's.
2
Distinguish contracts that allow message mode
Note the exception: divorce and manumission can be performed if the agent acts as a messenger (mursal) saying 'So-and-so ordered me to divorce/free'. This is effective on the principal.
The difference lies in ownership: the principal owns the wife/slave, so attribution is inherent.
3
Understand the rationale for marriage exception
Marriage cannot be done via message because the agent's dhamma can bear the mahr; thus the agent must attribute the contract to himself as agent, not merely convey a message.
Contrast with divorce where 'iddah is on the principal in any case.
4
Note the theoretical reconciliation by Abu Zayd
Abu Zayd proposes that the agent is a deputy in ruling (hukm) but original in rights (huquq), reconciling al-Karkhi and Abu Tahir. This explains why some contracts need attribution while others allow message.
This is a theoretical underpinning, not a different ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحقوق عقد يضيفه إلى موكله كنكاح، وخلع ، وصلح عن إنكار، أو دَم عَمْد، وعتق على مال ، وكتابة وهبة، وتصدق، وإعارة، وإيداع، ورهن 
(وحقوق عقدِ يضيفه إلى موكله : كنكاح، وخلع، وصلح عن إنكار، أو دَم عَمْد وعتق على مال وكتابة وهبة، وتصدق، وإعارة، وإيداع، ورهن وهاهنا مسلك آخر ، وهو ما قال أبو زيد الله من أن الوكيل نائب في حق الحكم ، أصيل في الحقوق، فوافق الكرخي له في الحقوق، وأبا طاهر في الحكم، ذكره البَزَّازي وحسنه.
[٢]قوله : يضيفه...الخ ؛ يعني لا يستغني عن الإضافة فيه إلى موكله حتـى لـو أضافه إلى موكله لا يصح، كما ذكروا ، لكن قال في «البزازية»: الوكيل بالطلاقِ والعناق إذا أخرج الكلام مخرج الرسالة، بأن قال : إنَّ فلاناً أمرني أن أطلق أو أعتق ينفذ على الموكل ؛ لأنَّ عدتهما على الموكل على كل حال، ولو أخرج الكلام في النكاح والطلاق مخرج الوكالة بأن أضافه إلى نفسه صح إلا في النكاح.
والفرق أنه في الطلاق إضافة إلى الموكل معنى ؛ لأنه بناء على ملك الرقبة، وهي للموكل في الطلاق والعتاق ، فأما في النكاح فذمَّة الوكيل قابلة للمهر حتى لو كان
And rights of a contract that he attributes to his principal: like marriage, khul', settlement upon denial, or intentional blood-money, manumission for a consideration, kitabah, hibah, sadaqah, i'arah, wadi'ah, and rahn. (And rights of a contract that he attributes to his principal: marriage, khul', settlement upon denial, or intentional blood-money, manumission for a consideration, kitabah, hibah, sadaqah, i'arah, wadi'ah, and rahn) and here there is another approach, which is what Abu Zayd said: that the agent is a deputy in the realm of the ruling, and an original party in rights. So he agreed with al-Karkhi regarding rights and with Abu Tahir regarding the ruling. al-Bazzazi mentioned it and considered it good. [2] His saying: 'He attributes it...' etc.; meaning he cannot dispense with attributing it to his principal, so much so that if he does not attribute it to his principal, it is not valid, as they mentioned. But al-Bazzaziyyah said: The agent for divorce and manumission, if he expresses the words as a message, by saying: 'So-and-so ordered me to divorce or free,' it is effective upon the principal because their 'iddah is upon the principal in any case. And if he expresses the words in marriage and divorce as an agency by attributing it to himself, it is valid except in marriage. The difference is that in divorce, attribution to the principal is effective in meaning because it is based on ownership of the slave, which belongs to the principal in divorce and manumission. As for marriage, the dhimma of the agent is capable of bearing the dower, even if...
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage lists the contracts in which an agent must explicitly attribute the contract to his principal for the contract to be valid. These include marriage (nikah), khul', settlement (sulh) in cases of denial or intentional homicide, manumission for a consideration ('itq bi mal), kitabah (contract of manumission), hibah (gift), sadaqah (charity), i'arah (loan of an object), wadi'ah (deposit), and rahn (pawn). The commentator explains that the agent cannot dispense with this attribution; if he does not attribute it to the principal, the contract is invalid. However, there is an exception in the case of divorce and manumission: if the agent acts as a mere messenger (mursal) and says, 'So-and-so ordered me to divorce or free,' the divorce or manumission is effective on the principal because the waiting period ('iddah) is on the principal in any case. In marriage, however, the agent cannot act as a messenger; he must always attribute the contract to himself (as agent) because the agent's own dhamma (legal capacity) can bear the mahr (dower), so merely conveying a message is insufficient. The passage also mentions a third position by Abu Zayd, who reconciles the views of al-Karkhi and Abu Tahir: the agent is a deputy in the ruling (hukm) but an original party in the rights (huquq).

Key Terms
الإضافة (al-idafah): Attribution of the contract to the principal; performing the contract in the name of the muwakkil.
الرسالة (al-risalah): A message; when the agent acts merely as a messenger conveying the principal's words.
النكاح (al-nikah): Marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which of the following contracts can an agent act merely as a messenger (mursal) without attributing the action to himself?
A) Nikah (marriage)
B) Talaq (divorce)
C) Sale
D) Ijarah

Key Takeaway
For agency, memorize the list of contracts requiring attribution to the principal; remember the exception for divorce and manumission as message.

Contracts Requiring Attribution to the Principal and the Dispute on Buying a Relative
Lists contracts requiring attribution and discusses dispute on buying a relative

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the contracts that require attribution to the principal in Hanafi agency law and explain why an agent does not cause manumission when buying his relative, citing the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
In agency, contracts of relinquishment require attribution to the principal; buying a relative does not free the slave because ownership is either absent or non-established.

The Hanafi school distinguishes between contracts where the agent acts as a messenger (safir) and must attribute the contract to his principal, and those where he can act in his own name. This lesson identifies the former category and examines a specific intra-Hanafi dispute regarding an agent who purchases his relative. Understanding this distinction is crucial for valid agency and property transfers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contracts that require idafah
List the contracts mentioned: nikah, khul', sulh 'an inkar, dam 'amd, 'itiq 'ala mal, kitabah, hibah, sadaqah, 'ariyah, wadi'ah, rahn, and iqrad. Note that these are contracts of relinquishment (isqatat).
The list is not exhaustive; these are examples from the matn.
2
Understand the reasoning for idafah
The agent in these contracts is a safir (messenger). The ruling (e.g., transfer of ownership, termination of rights) cannot be separated from its cause; therefore the contract must be attributed to the principal.
The passage states: 'al-hukm fi hadhihi al-'uqud la yaqbal al-fasl 'an sababihi'.
3
Analyze the dispute on buying a relative
When an agent is told to buy his relative (e.g., father) from the owner, does the slave become free? Abu Tahir: agent never owns it. Al-Karakhi: agent owns briefly but ownership is non-established (ghayr mutaqarrir). Both agree no manumission.
The ikhtilaf has no practical effect here; the author suggests applying it elsewhere.
4
Grasp the concept of milk mutaqarrir
Manumission requires established ownership (milk mutaqarrir). Fleeting or non-established ownership does not trigger freedom. This is why al-Karakhi's view also denies manumission.
This concept appears in other chapters, e.g., inheritance after purchase.
5
Recognize the practical outcome
The mufta bihi is that the slave is not freed. The intra-Hanafi debate illustrates how different legal reasoning can lead to the same result, and it may matter in other scenarios.
The commentary notes: 'so this difference does not yield a practical effect'.
