By marriage from her side, if he words it as agency, he does not become contrary because of attribution to the woman in meaning, as if he said: 'I give you in marriage the private part of my principal.' End. And al-Bahr said after quoting: On this basis, the meaning of attribution to the principal differs. In the evidence of marriage, it is from the husband's side on condition; in other cases, there is permissibility, so its absence is permissible. End. It appears from this that attribution is only necessary in marriage, which contradicts what they stated. Al-Mujtaba said: If he authorized him to pawn a slave of so-and-so for his debt, or to borrow it for him, or to borrow a thousand for him, he attributes the contract to his principal, not to himself, saying: 'Zayd borrows from you such-and-such, or pawns from your slave, or borrows from you.' If he said: 'Give me as a gift, or lend me, or give me a loan, or give me charity,' it is for the agent. End. And the detail is in the detailed works. [1] His saying: 'is related to the principal, not to him' because the agent in these contracts is a mere messenger, and the messenger is one who reports the words of another, and the reporter is not bound by the rulings of the words of another. Do you not see that one who reports the slander of another is not a slanderer? That the agent in these contracts is a mere messenger is apparent; for this reason he cannot dispense with attributing the contract to the principal. Even if he attributes it to himself in marriage, the marriage occurs for him, so he becomes like a messenger. It cannot be imagined that the cause issues from a person as an agent and the ruling occurs for another, so we consider him a messenger. The secret is that the ruling in these contracts cannot be separated from its cause because they are of the type of relinquishments (isqatat). As for other than marriage, it is apparent. As for marriage, because it nullifies the ownership of the woman, and because the original principle regarding private parts is prohibition, so marriage is a removal of prohibition with respect to the original, and ownership is established only by necessity to enable...
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary (sharh) on the chapter of wakalah (agency) from Al-Wiqayah. It addresses a fundamental classification: certain contracts must be explicitly attributed by the agent (wakil) to his principal (muwakkil). The author lists these: marriage (nikah), khul', settlement for denial (sulh 'an inkar), settlement for intentional homicide (dam 'amd), manumission for payment ('itiq 'ala mal), kitabah (contract of manumission), gift (hibah), charity (sadaqah), lending ('ariyah), deposit (wadi'ah), and pledge (rahn). The list also includes lending (iqrad) later. The reason is that the agent in these acts is a mere messenger (safir) — he conveys the principal's action, and the legal consequences attach directly to the principal. The commentary then introduces a specific intra-Hanafi dispute regarding an agent instructed to buy his relative (a slave who is the agent's relative, e.g., father or brother). According to Abu Tahir al-Dabbas, the agent never owns the slave; ownership goes directly to the principal from the seller, so the agent cannot free him through his own ownership. According to al-Karakhi, the agent does own briefly, but that ownership is 'non-established' (ghayr mutaqarrir) because it is immediately transferred; such ownership does not trigger manumission (which requires established ownership, milk mutaqarrir). Both scholars agree the slave is not freed, so the ikhtilaf has no practical consequence in this case. The author suggests applying it elsewhere. Then the commentary delves into the meaning of 'attribution' (idafah). In marriage, if the agent attributes the contract to himself, the marriage is for him, not the principal. In divorce and manumission, attribution to the principal is in meaning because these acts are based on ownership of the slave, which belongs to the principal. The agent's liability for the dower in marriage is discussed. The passage concludes with a ruling from Al-Mujtaba: if the agent uses first-person language (e.g., 'give me a loan'), it is for himself, not the principal. The underlying principle is that in contracts of relinquishment (isqatat), the ruling cannot be separated from its cause.

Key Terms
سفير (safir): A messenger who merely conveys the words of another; the legal effects attach to the principal, not the agent.
ملك متقرر (milk mutaqarrir): Established, stable ownership that is not immediately transferred; it is a necessary condition for manumission by purchase of a relative.
تخريج (takhrij): A derived opinion or legal possibility based on the principles of the madhhab, not a directly transmitted ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the slave is already free by the purchase itself.
D) Because the principal did not authorize manumission.

Key Takeaway
Contracts of relinquishment require attribution to the principal; buying a relative does not free the slave because ownership is either absent or non-established.

The Agent as Safir: Attribution Required in Marriage and Similar Contracts
Defining the agent's role as safir in marriage and qard contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school requires an agent to attribute certain contracts to the principal, and distinguish the position of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq from the majority.

Core Idea
In contracts of removal (isqatat), the agent is a safir and must attribute the contract to the principal; otherwise it belongs to the agent.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah tackles a key distinction in Hanafi agency law: when must an agent attribute the contract to the principal? The author presents the general rule that in certain contracts—marriage, loan, pawn, etc.—the agent is a pure intermediary (safir) who merely conveys the words of another. However, a difference of opinion emerges from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq regarding whether such attribution is mandatory only in marriage or across all these contracts. By examining the talil (legal reasoning) behind this rule, you will understand the nature of 'removal' contracts and why they differ from sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contracts where the agent is considered a safir
List the contracts mentioned: marriage, qard, rahn, i'arah, hibah, sadaqah, wadi'ah, kitabah, 'itq 'ala mal, shirkah, mudarabah. These are isqatat or contracts where ownership transfers by possession (qabd).
These are contrasted with sale (tamlikat).
2
Understand the meaning of attribution (idafah) to the principal
Attribution means the agent phrases the contract to show it is for the principal, e.g., 'My principal Zayd lends you' not 'I lend you'.
Without idafah, the contract is considered for the agent personally.
3
Follow the intra-Hanafi dispute: majority vs. al-Bahr
The majority (from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, al-Mujtaba) require idafah in all safir contracts. Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq only requires it in marriage, allowing the agent to act as original in others.
The passage states that al-Bahr's view contradicts what earlier scholars explicitly stated.
4
Analyze the rationale (talil): why rulings of isqatat cannot be separated from their cause
In marriage, the ruling removes prohibition. Once removed, it cannot be removed again. Therefore it cannot be attributed to the agent first and then to the principal; it must be directly attributed to the principal. In sale, the ruling (ownership) can transfer, so the agent can act as original.
The passage gives the 'secret' (al-sirr) behind the rule.
5
Apply the rule: consequences of misattribution
If the agent says 'I give', 'I lend', 'I marry', the contract is for the agent personally. If he says 'My principal gives', it is for the principal.
Exception: In sale, the agent may act as original even without attribution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوكالة تعريفها وأحكامها
وإقراض يتعلق بالموكل لا به فلا يطالب وكيل زوج بالمهر، ولا وكيل عرس بتسليمها، وببدل الخلع بال[؟] النكاح من جانبها وأخرج يخرج الوكالة لا يصير مخالفاً؛ لإضافته إلى المرأة معنى، فكأنه قال : ملكتك بضع موكلتي. انتهى.
وقال في «البحر» بعد نقل هذا المنقول : فعلى هذا معنى الإضافة إلى الموكل مختلف، ففي دليل النكاح من قبيل الزوج على وجه الشرط، وفيما عداه وجه الجواز فيجوز عدمه. انتهى. وظهر منه أنّ الإضافة لا تلزم إلا في النكاح، وهو مخالف لما صرحوا به.
قال في «المجتبى»: وكله أن يرتهنّ عبد فلان بدينه أو يستعيره له أو يستقرض له ألفاً، فإنّه يضيف العقد إلى موكله دون نفسه، فيقول: إنَّ زيداً يستقرض منك كذا أو يسترهن من عبدك أو يستعير منك، ولو قال : هب لي أو أعرني أو أقرضني أو تصدق علي فهو للوكيل. انتهى. والتفصيل في المبسوطات [؟] قوله : يتعلق بالموكل لا به ؛ لأنَّ الوكيل في هذه العقود سفير محض، والسفير من يكون حاكياً قول الغير، والحاكي لا يلزمه أحكام قول الغير. ألا ترى أن من حكى قذف الغير لا يكون قاذفاً ، أما كون الوكيل في هذه العقود سفير محضاً فظاهر ؛ ولهذا لا يستغني عن إضافة العقد إلى الموكل حتى لو أضافه إلى نفسه في النكاح وقع النكاح له، فصار كالرسول فلا يتصور أن يكون السبب صادراً من شخص على سبيل الأصالة، ويقعُ الحكم لغيره، فجعلناه سفيراً. والسر فيه : أنَّ الحكم في هذه العقود لا يقبلُ الفصل عن سببه ؛ لأنها من قبيل الإسقاطات، أما غير النكاح فظاهر. وأما النكاح ؛ فلأنه يسقط مالكيّة المرأة ؛ ولأنَّ الأصل في الأبضاع الحرمة، فكان النكاح إسقاطاً للحرمة نظراً إلى الأصل، وإنّما يثبت الملك ضرورة ؛ ليتمكن من الوطء ؛ ولهذا لا يظهر في حق الفسخ والتمليك من الغير، وفيما وراء الوطء فهو إسقاط جرياً على الأصل، إذ الحرمة تنافي الملك والساقط متلاش ومضمحل فلا يجوز أن يسقط في حق الوكيل ، ثم يسقط ثانياً في حق الموكل بالانتقال ؛ لأنَّ الساقط لا يعود إلا لسبب جديد، ولم يوجد. فكان حكم النكاح ثابتاً كمن أضيف إليه ابتداءً وهو الموكل بخلاف البيع، فإنّ حكمه يقبلُ الفصل عن السبب، كما أنه في البيع بشرط الخيار، فجاز أن يصدر السببُ عن شخص أصالة، ويقع الحكم لغيره خلافةٌ. فإنَّ المحل فيه خلق مباحاً وقابلاً للتملك بطريق الأصالة، وذلك الحكم مما يقبلُ ملك إلى ملك، فجاز أن يملكه شخص ثم ينتقل عنه إلى شخص، وجاز أن يصدر السبب من شخص ويقع الحكم لغيره. ومن أخوات هذا النوع العتق على مال والكتابة والهبة، والتصدق والإعارة، والإيداع والإقراض، والرهن ،والشركة، والمضاربة، فإنَّ الحكم فيها يثبت بالقبض ، وإنه يلاقي محلها مملوكاً للغير، فلا يجعل أصيلاً فيه بل سفيراً ومعبراً، وزيادة التفصيل في شروح «الكنز» وحواشي «الهداية».
Book of Agency: its definition and rulings. And lending relates to the principal not to the agent, so the agent of a husband is not asked for the dowry, nor the agent of a bride for handing her over, nor for the substitute of khul' with [؟] marriage from her side, and if he acts the agency [؟] he does not become contrary; because of his attribution to the woman in meaning, as if he said: I gave you in marriage the private part of my principal. End. And he said in al-Bahr after quoting this: On this basis, the meaning of attribution to the principal differs; in the evidence of marriage it is on the condition of the husband, and in other than it it is on the basis of permissibility, so its absence is permissible. End. And it appears from this that attribution is necessary only in marriage, and this contradicts what they explicitly stated. He said in al-Mujtaba: If someone appoints an agent to pawn [؟] the slave of so-and-so for his debt, or to borrow him for him, or to borrow a thousand for him, then he adds the contract to his principal, not to himself, saying: Zayd borrows from you such and such, or pawns from your slave, or borrows from you. If he said: Give me as a gift, or lend me, or give me as a loan, or give me charity, then it is for the agent. End. And the detail is in the expansive works [؟] His saying: It relates to the principal not to him; because the agent in these contracts is a pure intermediary, and the intermediary is one who reports the speech of another, and the reporter is not bound by the rulings of the speech of another. Do you not see that one who reports the slander of another is not a slanderer. As for the agent being a pure intermediary in these contracts, it is apparent; for this reason he cannot do without attributing the contract to the principal, so that even if he attributes it to himself in marriage, the marriage falls for him, and he becomes like a messenger. So it is inconceivable that the cause proceeds from a person as an original and the ruling occurs for another, so we make him an intermediary. The secret of it is that the ruling in these contracts does not accept separation from its cause; because they are of the category of removals. As for other than marriage, it is apparent. As for marriage, because it removes the woman's ownership; and because the original principle in private parts is prohibition, so marriage is a removal of prohibition in view of the original, and ownership is only established by necessity to enable intercourse; for this reason it does not appear with regard to annulment and transfer of ownership to another, and beyond intercourse it is removal according to the original, since prohibition contradicts ownership. And what has been removed is null and void, so it cannot be removed in favor of the agent, then removed again in favor of the principal by transfer; because what has been removed does not return except for a new cause, and that is not found. So the ruling of marriage is as if it were attributed initially to the principal. Unlike sale, for its ruling accepts separation from the cause, as in sale with the right of option, so it is permissible for the cause to proceed from a person as an original and the ruling to occur for another in substitution. Indeed, the subject matter in it is created free and capable of being owned by original right, and that ruling is of the type that transfers from ownership to ownership, so it is permissible for one person to own it then transfer it to another, and it is permissible for the cause to proceed from one person and the ruling to occur for another. And from the siblings of this type are manumission for compensation, kitabah, gift, charity, loan for use, deposit, lending, pawn, partnership, and mudarabah, for the ruling therein is established by taking possession, and it encounters its subject matter as owned by another, so he is not made the original therein, but rather an intermediary and a conveyer. And an increase of detail is in the commentaries of al-Kanz and the glosses of al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage discusses the role of the agent (wakil) in certain contracts, specifically those that involve moving ownership or removing rights. The author begins by stating the general rule: in contracts such as qard (loan), the contract relates to the principal (muwakkil), not the agent. Therefore, an agent appointed by a husband to give a dowry cannot be sued for it, nor can an agent appointed by a bride to hand her over. This is because the agent is a pure intermediary (safir), merely reporting the words of another. The agent does not bear the legal consequences of the contract.

The text then cites a report from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, which presents a difference of opinion: the requirement of attribution (idafah) to the principal differs. In the case of marriage, the contract must be attributed to the principal; in other cases, it may be permissible to omit attribution. The author notes that this contradicts what earlier scholars explicitly stated.

Al-Mujtaba is then quoted to clarify the practice: when an agent is appointed to pawn, borrow, or take a loan, he must attribute the contract to the principal (e.g., saying 'Zayd borrows from you'). If the agent instead says 'give me' or 'loan me', the contract is for the agent personally.

The passage then delves into the rationale: the agent in these transactions is a pure intermediate, and the rulings of such contracts do not admit separation from their cause because they are essentially removals (isqatat). For marriage, the underlying principle is the prohibition of private parts, so marriage removes that prohibition. Since the removed prohibition cannot be removed twice, the contract must be directly attributed to the principal who has the right to remove it. In contrast, in sale, the subject matter is a commodity that can be owned and transferred, so the contract can be separated from its cause: the agent can act as the principal in the transaction, and the ruling (ownership) can transfer from the seller to the buyer through the agent.

The passage concludes by listing other contracts that follow the same rule as marriage: manumission for compensation, kitabah, gift, charity, loan for use (i'arah), deposit, lending (qard), pawn, partnership, and mudarabah. In all these, the ruling is established by taking possession (qabd), and the agent cannot be considered the original party.

The dispute within this passage centers on whether idafah (attribution to the principal) is required only in marriage or in other contracts as well. The author of al-Bahr suggests it is only required in marriage, but the majority opinion (as stated by 'alama') seems to require it in all contracts where the agent is a safir. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the agent must attribute the contract to the principal; otherwise, the contract is considered for the agent personally.

Key Terms
سفير (safir): A pure intermediary who merely conveys the words of another and does not bear legal consequences.
الإضافة (al-idafah): Attributing the contract to the principal by stating that the act is for him.
إسقاطات (isqatat): Contracts that remove a right or prohibition, such as marriage removing the prohibition of intercourse.
وكيل (wakil): An agent appointed to act on behalf of another (principal) in legal transactions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what happens if an agent appointed to give a loan says 'I lend you' instead of 'My principal lends you'?
A) The loan is invalid.
B) The loan is attributed to the principal because the agent is a safir.
C) The loan is for the agent personally.
D) The principal must ratify the contract.

Key Takeaway
In removal contracts, the agent is a safir; attribution to the principal is mandatory, otherwise the contract falls on the agent.

Buyer's Right to Withhold Price from the Principal of the Seller
Ruling with proof by talil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a buyer paying the principal of the agent-seller and explain why the payment is valid and bars the agent from demanding again.

Core Idea
Paying the principal (owner) is valid and discharges the buyer because the price belongs to him, even though the agent is the contracting party.

In agency sales, the buyer may face a demand from both the agent and the principal for the price. The Hanafi school clarifies that the buyer can withhold payment from the principal and, if he chooses to pay the principal directly, the payment is valid and the agent cannot demand it again. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this ruling, based on the distinction between the agent as the immediate contractual party and the principal as the ultimate owner of the price.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage considers a case where an owner (principal) appoints an agent to sell an item. The agent sells to a buyer. Then the principal demands the price directly from the buyer. What are the buyer's options?
The principal is the owner; the agent acts as seller.
2
State the two rulings
First: the buyer has the right to withhold the price from the principal. Second: if the buyer pays the principal, the payment is valid and the agent cannot demand the price again.
These are two separate but related rulings: a right to refuse, and the effect of voluntary payment.
3
Understand the reasoning (talil)
The principal is a stranger (ajnabi) to the contract because the agent acted as the principal (asil) in the sale. The price is the right of the principal, so if it reaches him, there is no benefit in the agent collecting it and then handing it over. Hence, payment to the principal extinguishes the buyer's obligation.
The agent is 'asil fi al-huquq' — the immediate party for rights and obligations.
4
Apply the ruling
In practice, the buyer may safely pay either the agent or the principal. Paying the principal is valid and bars any subsequent claim from the agent.
This protects the buyer from double liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللمشتري منعُ الثَّمن من موكل بائعه، فإذا دفع إليه صح ، ولم يطالبه بائعه ثانياً. قوله : وللمشتري منع ... الخ ؛ يعني إذا وكُل رجلاً بيع شيء فباعه، ثمَّ إِنَّ الموكل طلب من المشتري الثمن له منعه ؛ لأنَّ الموكَّل أجنبي عن العقد، والوكيل أصيل في الحقوق ؛ ولذا له أن يوكل الآخر بهذه الحقوق، وإن لم يكن له حق التوكيل. قوله : صح؛ لأنَّ الثمن المقبوض حق الموكل ، وقد وصل إليه، ولا فائدة في الأخذ منه ، ثم الدفع إليه. قوله : ولم يطالبه بائعه ثانياً ؛ لعدم الفائدة، نعم لو كان للمشتري على الموكل
And the buyer has the right to withhold the price from the principal of his seller; if he pays him, it is valid, and his seller cannot demand from him a second time. His saying: 'And the buyer has the right to withhold... etc.' means: If someone appoints a man to sell something, and he sells it, then the principal demands the price from the buyer, he (the buyer) can withhold it from him because the principal is a stranger to the contract, and the agent is the principal (asil) in the rights; hence the agent may appoint another to these rights even if he does not have the right of delegation. His saying: 'It is valid' because the received price is the right of the principal, and it has reached him, and there is no benefit in taking it from him (the buyer) and then giving it to him (again). His saying: 'And his seller cannot demand from him a second time' due to lack of benefit. Yes, if the buyer had a claim against the principal...
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This ruling addresses a specific scenario in the law of agency: when an agent is appointed to sell an item and sells it to a buyer. The principal (the owner) then asks the buyer for the price directly. The rule is that the buyer has the right to withhold the price from the principal. Why? Because the principal is an outsider (ajnabi) to the contract; the agent was the one who contracted as the principal (asil) in the transaction. Therefore, the buyer's obligation is to pay the agent, not the principal. However, if the buyer chooses to pay the principal, the payment is valid because the price ultimately belongs to the principal. Once the principal receives it, the seller (agent) cannot demand the price again from the buyer, as there is no benefit in taking it from the buyer and then returning it to the principal. The commentary also notes that the agent has the right to delegate another person to collect the price, even if he does not have general authority to delegate. The text ends with a conditional: 'Yes, if the buyer had a claim against the principal...' which likely discusses a scenario of set-off, but the page cuts off. This ruling is based on the principle that the agent is the immediate party in the contract, and the principal's rights are derivative. The buyer is protected from double liability by paying either the agent or the principal.

Key Terms
موكل (muwakkil): The principal who appoints an agent; the one who owns the goods and is entitled to the price.
أصيل (asil): The original party to the contract; here, the agent is considered the asil in rights, meaning he stands in the place of the seller for the purpose of collecting the price.
ثمن (thaman): The price paid in exchange for the sold item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a buyer pays the price of a purchased item to the principal of the seller (the owner) instead of the agent?
A) The payment is invalid and the buyer must pay again to the agent.
B) The payment is valid and the seller (agent) cannot demand the price again.
C) The payment is valid only if the principal was present at the sale.
D) The payment is valid but the agent can still demand the price from the buyer.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a buyer may pay the principal of the agent-seller directly; such payment is valid and bars the agent from demanding the price again.

Classification of Contracts in Agency: Principal vs. Mediator
Differentiating contracts that require mention of principal vs. those that don't

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify agency contracts into those requiring mention of the principal and those not, and apply the set-off rule distinguishing agent's debt from principal's debt.

Core Idea
The agent's legal capacity varies by contract—sometimes as principal, sometimes as pure mediator.

This passage from the Hanafi text on agency (wakala) clarifies which contracts attribute rights to the agent versus the principal. Understanding this classification is crucial for determining liability in set-off and other financial transactions. The passage also resolves an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding set-off with the agent's personal debt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of contracts
First, note that some contracts (sale, rental) are independent of mentioning the principal—the agent acts as principal in rights. Others (marriage, khul') require mentioning the principal—the agent is a mere mediator.
The text states: 'أما البيع والإجارة فلا شَكٍّ أنَّهما مستغنيان عن ذِكْرِ الموكل' and 'النِّكاحُ والخلع لا يستغنيان عنه'.
2
Understand the agent's status in sulh (settlement)
For sulh (settlement), it depends: if from acknowledgment (iqrar), it is like sale—agent is principal; if from denial (inkar), it is a ransom for oath—agent is pure mediator (safir).
Crucial: rights go to the agent in the former, not in the latter.
3
Apply the set-off rule: agent's debt vs principal's debt
Set-off occurs with the principal's debt immediately. If the buyer has a debt against the agent only, set-off occurs and the agent guarantees the principal because he paid his own debt with the principal's property.
Abu Yusuf disagrees: set-off does not occur with the agent's debt.
4
Note the exceptions and analogies
The text contrasts with cases like orphan property (guardian must receive) and currency exchange (principal's receipt voids the transaction) to show when set-off or receipt is invalid.
These exceptions reinforce the rule that the agent's role matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أخوات هذا النوع العتق على مال والكتابة والهبة، والتصدق والإعارة، والإيداع والإقراض، والرهن، والشركة، والمضاربة، فإنَّ الحكم فيها يثبت بالقبض، وإنه يلاقي محلها مملوكاً للغير، فلا يجعل أصيلاً فيه بل سفيراً ومعبراً. [1] قوله: وللمشتري منع ... الخ؛ يعني إذا وكُل رجلاً بيع شيء فباعه، ثمَّ إِنَّ الموكل طلب من المشتري الثمن له منعه؛ لأنَّ الموكَّل أجنبي عن العقد، والوكيل أصيل في الحقوق؛ ولذا له أن يوكل الآخر بهذه الحقوق، وإن لم يكن له حق التوكيل. [2] قوله: صح؛ لأنَّ الثمن المقبوض حق الموكل، وقد وصل إليه، ولا فائدة في الأخذ منه، ثم الدفع إليه. [3] قوله: ولم يطالبه بائعه ثانياً؛ لعدم الفائدة، نعم لو كان للمشتري على الموكل دين فلا تبرأ ذمته بدفع الثمن إليه، بل يجب عليه الدفع إلى الوكيل.  كتاب الوكالة تعريفها وأحكامها ۲۱ اعلم أنّ في بعض هذه الأمثلة نظراً في أنَّها تضاف إلى الوكيل أو الموكل. أما البيع والإجارة فلا شَكٍّ أنَّهما مستغنيان عن ذِكْرِ الموكل، فهما من القسم الأول، والنِّكاحُ والخلع لا يستغنيان عنه، فهما من القسم الثَّاني. وأمَّا الصَّلحُ فلا فَرْقَ فيه بين أن يكون عن إقرار أو إنكار في الإضافة، فإنَّ زيداً إذا ادعى داراً على عمرو، فوكَّل عمرو وكيلاً على أن يصالح بالمئة، فيقولُ زيد: صالحتُ عن دعوى الدار على عمرو بالمئة، ويقبلُ الوكيل هذا الصلح، يَتِمُّ الصُّلْحُ سواء كان عن إقرار أو عن إنكار، إلا أنه إذا كان عن إقرار يكون كالبيع، فيرجعُ الحقوق إلى الوكيل كما في البيع، فتسليـم بـدل الصلح علـى الوكيل، وإذا كان عن إنكار، فهو فداءً يمين في حق المدعى عليه، فالوكيل سفير محض فلا يرجع إليه الحقوق. تقعُ المقاصة بمجرد العقد بوصول الحقِّ إليه بطريق التقاص، ولو كان له دين عليهما تقعُ بدين الموكل دون دين الوكيل، ولو كان له دين على الوكيل فقط وقعت المقاصة به، ويضمن الوكيل للموكل؛ لأنه قضى دينه بمال الموكل. وقال أبو يوسف: لا تقع المقاصة بدين الوكيل، بخلاف ما إذا باع مال اليتيم ودفع المشتري الثمن إلى اليتيم حيث لا تبرأ ذمته بل يجب عليه أن يدفع الثمن إلى الوصي؛ لأنَّ اليتيم ليس له قبض ماله أصلاً، فلا يكون له الأخذ من المدين، فيكون الدفع إليه تضييعاً، فلا يعتد به، وبخلاف الوكيل في الصرف إذا صارَفَ وقبض الموكَّلُ بدل الصرف حيث يبطل الصرف ولا يعتد بقبضه. ذكره العَيْنِيُّ والزَّيْلَعِيُّ وغيرهما. [1] قوله: اعلم أن...الخ؛ المقصود وهو الإيراد على المصنف مع الإيماء إلى جوابه، أما تقرير الإيرادِ فهو أنَّ العقود في باب الوكالة لا تخلو: إما أن تستغني عن ذكر الموكل فيها، أو لا.
And among the sisters of this type are: manumission for money, kitabah, gift, charity, loan, deposit, lending, pledge, partnership, and mudarabah; for the ruling therein is established by possession, and it meets its subject-matter owned by another, so the agent is not made a principal therein but rather a mediator and transmitter. [1] His statement: 'The buyer has the right to withhold...' means... [2] ... [3] ... Page 21: Book of Agency: Its Definition and Rulings. Know that in some of these examples there is consideration as to whether they are attributed to the agent or the principal. As for sale and rental, there is no doubt that they are independent of mentioning the principal, so they are of the first category. Marriage and khul' are not independent of him, so they are of the second category. As for sulh (settlement), there is no difference in attribution whether it is from acknowledgment or denial. For if Zayd claims a house from 'Amr, and 'Amr appoints an agent to settle for one hundred, and Zayd says: 'I have settled the claim of the house against 'Amr for one hundred,' and the agent accepts this settlement, the settlement is complete whether it is from acknowledgment or denial, except that if it is from acknowledgment, it is like sale, so rights return to the agent as in sale, so delivery of the consideration is upon the agent; and if it is from denial, it is a ransom for oath in the right of the defendant, so the agent is a pure mediator and rights do not revert to him. Set-off occurs by the mere contract by way of the right reaching him through set-off. If he had a debt against both, set-off occurs with the debt of the principal, not the debt of the agent. If he had a debt against the agent only, set-off occurs with it, and the agent guarantees for the principal because he paid his debt with the principal's property. Abu Yusuf said: Set-off does not occur with the debt of the agent, contrary to the case where one sells the property of an orphan and the buyer pays the price to the orphan, for his liability is not discharged; rather he must pay the price to the guardian because the orphan has no right to take possession of his property at all, so he cannot take from the debtor, so payment to him is wasteful and not considered. And contrary to the agent in currency exchange if he exchanges and the principal takes possession of the exchange, for the exchange becomes void and his possession is not considered. Mentioned by al-'Ayni and al-Zayla'i and others. [1] His statement: 'Know that...' etc.; the intent is the objection to the author with a hint to its answer. As for the formulation of the objection, it is that contracts in the chapter of agency are not free from either being independent of mentioning the principal or not.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and analytical commentary on agency contracts. The author first lists contracts where the ruling is established by possession (qabd), such as manumission for money, kitabah, gift, etc., and notes that the agent is merely a mediator. Then he moves to page 21 to classify contracts into two categories: (1) those independent of mentioning the principal (sale, rental) and (2) those that require it (marriage, khul'). For sulh (settlement), it depends: if from acknowledgment (iqrar), it is like sale (rights go to agent); if from denial (inkar), it is a ransom for oath and the agent is a pure mediator. The passage then discusses set-off (muqassah) in agency: it occurs with the principal's debt, not the agent's; Abu Yusuf differs on set-off with the agent's debt. Examples from orphan property and currency exchange illustrate when payment to the principal is invalid. The commentary [1] clarifies that the passage is an objection to the author's earlier classification.

Key Terms
سفير (safīr): A pure mediator who merely conveys the action without acquiring rights or obligations.
مقاصة (muqāṣṣah): Set-off, the mutual extinguishment of debts between two parties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of the agent in a sulh (settlement) from denial (inkar)?
A) The agent is a principal in rights
B) The agent is a pure mediator (safir mahd)
C) The agent has no role
D) The agent is the defendant

Key Takeaway
The agent's legal capacity differs by contract type; set-off with the agent's personal debt triggers guarantee unless it is a pure mediator role.

Classification of Contracts in Wakalah: General Categories and the Special Case of Sulh
Commentary classifying agency contracts and resolving the attribution of sulh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify contracts in agency into those independent of the principal's mention and those dependent, and apply the distinction to settlement based on admission vs denial.

Core Idea
Settlement in agency is attributed to the agent if based on admission, to the principal if based on denial.

In the chapter of agency (wakalah), knowing which contract is attributed to the agent and which to the principal is crucial for determining rights and obligations. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies the classification and resolves an apparent contradiction in the matn regarding settlement (sulh).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Contracts in agency are either independent of mentioning the principal (e.g., sale, lease) or dependent on it (e.g., marriage, khul'). The independent ones are attributed to the agent; the dependent ones to the principal.
Independence means the contract is valid without naming the principal.
2
Understand the basis of independence
A contract is independent if it can be concluded without specifying the principal, because the agent has authority to act generally. Dependent contracts require the principal's identity for validity.
For example, 'I sell this for one hundred' is valid without naming the seller's principal.
3
Analyze the special case of sulh
The matn states that sulh is always attributed to the principal. The commentator notes an objection: the author also classifies sulh 'an iqrar as independent (like sale). The resolution is that attribution is based on the underlying nature: sulh 'an iqrar is like a sale, so rights revert to the agent; sulh 'an inkar is a ransom for oath, so the agent is a pure messenger.
The commentator distinguishes between form (surah) and reality (haqiqah) to resolve the apparent contradiction.
4
Distinguish the two types of sulh
For sulh 'an iqrar: the defendant admits liability, so the settlement is a transfer of property – like a sale – and the agent is responsible for delivery. For sulh 'an inkar: the defendant denies, so the settlement is a payment to avoid an oath – the agent is merely a messenger, and the principal bears the obligation.
This distinction affects who must deliver the settlement substitute and to whom rights revert.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أنّ في بعض هذه الأمثلة نظراً في أنَّها تضاف إلى الوكيل أو الموكل. أما البيع والإجارة فلا شَكٍّ أنَّهما مستغنيان عن ذِكْرِ الموكل، فهما من القسم الأول ، والنِّكاحُ والخلع لا يستغنيان عنه ، فهما من القسم الثَّاني. وأمَّا الصَّلحُ فلا فَرْقَ فيه بين أن يكون عن إقرار أو إنكار في الإضافة، فإنَّ زيداً إذا ادعى داراً على عمرو ، فوكَّل عمرو وكيلاً على أن يصالح بالمئة، فيقولُ زيد : صالحتُ عن دعوى الدار على عمرو بالمئة، ويقبلُ الوكيل هذا الصلح ، يَتِمُّ الصُّلْحُ سواء كان عن إقرار أو عن إنكار، إلا أنه إذا كان عن إقرار يكون كالبيع ، فيرجعُ الحقوق إلى الوكيل كما في البيع، فتسليـم بـدل الصلح علـى الوكيل، وإذا كان عن إنكار، فهو فداءً يمين في حق المدعى عليه، فالوكيل سفير محض فلا يرجع إليه الحقوق.
[١]قوله : اعلم أن...الخ ؛ المقصود وهو الإيراد على المصنف مع الإيماء إلى جوابه، أما تقرير الإيرادِ فهو أنَّ العقود في باب الوكالة لا تخلو : إما أن تستغني عن ذكر الموكل فيها، أو لا.
كما أنَّ البيع والإجارة من القسم الأول فإنّهما مستغنيان عن ذكر الموكل فيهما. والنكاح والخلع من القسم الثاني، فإنّهما لا يستغنيان عن ذكر الموكل فيهما. والصلح يضاف إلى الموكل سواء كان عن إقرار أو إنكار، ألا ترى أنَّ زيداً إذا ادعى على عمرو، فوكل عمرو وكيلاً على أن يصالح على المئة، فيقول زيد : صالحتُ عـن دعـوى الـدار على عمرو بالمئة، ويقبل الوكيل هذا الصلح، يتم الصلح سواء كان عن إقرار أو إنكار. وقد عد المصنف له الصالح عن إقرار من القسم الأول، وذكر في أفراده، حيث قال : كبيع وإجارة وصلح عن إقرار. وأما تقرير الجواب عن هذا الإيراد، فهو : إنَّ الصلح عن إقرار في معنى البيع ، فهو مضاف إلى الوكيل حقيقةً، وإن أضيف إلى الموكل صورة ؛ ولهذا ترجع الحقوق إلى الوكيل، ويكون عليه التسليم بدل الصلح، والصلح عن إنكار فهو فداء يمين في حق المدعى عليه، فلا معنى لإضافته إلى الوكيل ؛ لأنَّ الوكيل فيه سفير محض ؛ ولذا لا يرجع إليه الحقوق.
Know that in some of these examples there is consideration regarding whether they are attributed to the agent or the principal. As for sale and lease, there is no doubt that they are independent of mentioning the principal, so they are of the first category. Marriage and khul' are not independent of him, so they are of the second category. As for settlement (sulh), there is no difference whether it is based on admission or denial in terms of attribution. For if Zayd claims a house from 'Amr, and 'Amr appoints an agent to settle for one hundred, then Zayd says: 'I have settled the claim of the house against 'Amr for one hundred,' and the agent accepts this settlement, the settlement is completed whether it is based on admission or denial. However, if it is based on admission, it is like a sale, so rights revert to the agent as in sale, and delivery of the settlement substitute is upon the agent. If it is based on denial, it is a ransom for oath in favor of the defendant, so the agent is a pure intermediary and rights do not revert to him.
[1] His statement: 'Know that...' etc.; the intent is the objection to the author with an indication of its answer. As for the formulation of the objection, it is that contracts in the chapter of agency are not free from either being independent of mentioning the principal or not. Just as sale and lease are of the first category, they are independent of mentioning the principal. Marriage and khul' are of the second category, they are not independent of mentioning the principal. Settlement is attributed to the principal whether based on admission or denial. Do you not see that if Zayd claims against 'Amr, and 'Amr appoints an agent to settle for one hundred, then Zayd says: 'I have settled the claim of the house against 'Amr for one hundred,' and the agent accepts this settlement, the settlement is completed whether based on admission or denial. And the author counted settlement based on admission in the first category and mentioned it among its subdivisions, where he said: 'Like sale, lease, and settlement based on admission.' As for the formulation of the answer to this objection, it is that settlement based on admission is in the meaning of sale, so it is attributed to the agent in reality, even if attributed to the principal in form. Hence rights revert to the agent, and delivery of the settlement substitute is upon him. Settlement based on denial is a ransom for oath in favor of the defendant, so there is no meaning to attributing it to the agent, because the agent therein is a pure intermediary, hence rights do not revert to him.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the classification of contracts in agency (wakalah). The commentator addresses an objection regarding the author's categorization of settlement (sulh). The matn states that some contracts are independent of mentioning the principal (sale, lease) and are attributed to the agent, while others (marriage, khul') require mentioning the principal and are attributed to him. Settlement is then discussed: it is attributed to the principal regardless of whether it is based on admission (iqrar) or denial (inkar). However, the commentator notes that when based on admission, it functions like a sale, so rights and obligations fall on the agent; when based on denial, the agent is merely a messenger (safir), so rights do not transfer to him. The objection is that the author placed settlement based on admission in the first category (like sale), but in the matn he said settlement is always attributed to the principal. The commentator clarifies that the attribution to the principal is only in form (surah), but in reality (haqiqah) it is attributed to the agent because the rights revert to him. This preserves the consistency of the author's classification.

Key Terms
وكيل (wakīl): The agent appointed by the principal to conduct a legal act on his behalf.
موكل (muwakkil): The principal who appoints an agent to act on his behalf.
صلح (ṣulḥ): A settlement contract resolving a dispute, which can be based on admission (iqrar) or denial (inkar).
إقرار (iqrār): Admission of liability by the defendant, making the settlement a transfer of property like sale.
إنكار (inkār): Denial of liability by the defendant, making the settlement a ransom to avoid the oath.
سفير (safīr): A pure intermediary who merely conveys the acceptance without incurring personal liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because both involve a transfer of property from one party to another.
B) Because both are independent of mentioning the principal.
C) Because both require the agent to be a pure intermediary.
D) Because both are invalid if the principal is not mentioned.

Key Takeaway
Settlement in agency is attributed to the agent if based on admission (like sale), but to the principal if based on denial (agent is a pure messenger).

Classification of Sulh in Agency: Between Sale and Oath Redemption
Classifying sulh in agency contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sulh 'an iqrar and sulh 'an inkar in agency contracts and state the legal consequences for the agent's authority.

Core Idea
Sulh upon acknowledgment is like a sale (agent acts independently); sulh upon denial is like redeeming an oath (agent is a mere messenger).

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies an apparent inconsistency in the classification of sulh (compromise) within contracts of agency. Although sulh is generally attributed to the principal, the author of al-Hidayah counts sulh based on acknowledgment in the same category as sale and hire, where the agent acts independently. Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining the agent's rights and obligations in a compromise settlement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Categories of Agency Contracts
First, note that agency contracts are divided into two categories: (1) those independent of mentioning the principal, like sale and hire, where the agent acts autonomously; (2) those requiring mention of the principal, like marriage and khul', where the agent is a mere messenger.
The key difference is who bears the rights and obligations.
2
Locate Sulh in the Author's Classification
The author (al-Hidayah) lists sulh 'an iqrar (based on acknowledgment) in the first category alongside sale and hire. This seems contradictory because sulh is usually attributed to the principal.
The passage notes an apparent objection: sulh is attributed to the principal whether based on acknowledgment or denial.
3
Understand the Objection and the Need for Resolution
The objection arises because if sulh is always attributed to the principal, it should belong to the second category. The commentator must explain why sulh 'an iqrar is treated differently.
The resolution lies in the nature of sulh 'an iqrar as a sale-like transaction.
4
Follow the Resolution: Two Types of Sulh
The commentator distinguishes: sulh 'an iqrar is in the meaning of sale (exchange of property), so it is truly attributed to the agent, even if formally attributed to the principal. Sulh 'an inkar is a redemption of the oath for the defendant, so the agent is merely a messenger with no personal liability.
This distinction determines whether rights revert to the agent or not.
5
Apply the Distinction to Agent's Authority and Liability
For sulh 'an iqrar, the agent is liable for delivery and acquires rights; for sulh 'an inkar, the agent does not bear liability and rights stay with the principal.
This is the practical outcome of the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كما أنَّ البيع والإجارة من القسم الأول فإنّهما مستغنيان عن ذكر الموكل فيهما. والنكاح والخلع من القسم الثاني، فإنّهما لا يستغنيان عن ذكر الموكل فيهما. والصلح يضاف إلى الموكل سواء كان عن إقرار أو إنكار، ألا ترى أنَّ زيداً إذا ادعى على عمرو، فوكل عمرو وكيلاً على أن يصالح على المئة، فيقول زيد : صالحتُ عـن دعـوى الـدار على عمرو بالمئة، ويقبل الوكيل هذا الصلح، يتم الصلح سواء كان عن إقرار أو إنكار. وقد عد المصنف له الصالح عن إقرار من القسم الأول، وذكر في أفراده، حيث قال : كبيع وإجارة وصلح عن إقرار. وأما تقرير الجواب عن هذا الإيراد، فهو : إنَّ الصلح عن إقرار في معنى البيع ، فهو مضاف إلى الوكيل حقيقةً، وإن أضيف إلى الموكل صورة ؛ ولهذا ترجع الحقوق إلى الوكيل، ويكون عليه التسليم بدل الصلح، والصلح عن إنكار فهو فداء يمين في حق المدعى عليه، فلا معنى لإضافته إلى الوكيل ؛ لأنَّ الوكيل فيه سفير محض ؛ ولذا لا يرجع إليه الحقوق.
Just as sale and hire are from the first category—they are independent of mentioning the principal therein. And marriage and khul' are from the second category—they are not independent of mentioning the principal therein. And sulh (compromise) is attributed to the principal whether it is based on acknowledgment or denial. Do you not see that if Zayd sues 'Amr, and 'Amr appoints an agent to compromise for one hundred, and Zayd says, 'I have compromised regarding the claim of the house from 'Amr for one hundred,' and the agent accepts this compromise, the compromise is completed whether based on acknowledgment or denial. And the author [al-Hidayah] has counted sulh based on acknowledgment as from the first category and mentioned it among its examples, where he said: 'like sale, hire, and sulh based on acknowledgment.' As for the articulation of the answer to this objection, it is: indeed sulh based on acknowledgment is in the meaning of sale, so it is attributed to the agent in reality, even if it is attributed to the principal in form; and for this reason, rights revert to the agent, and delivery of the subject of compromise is incumbent upon him. And sulh based on denial is a redemption of the oath from the defendant's perspective, so there is no meaning to attributing it to the agent, because the agent therein is a mere messenger; hence rights do not revert to him.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-statement that clarifies the classification of contracts of agency in Hanafi fiqh, specifically addressing an apparent inconsistency regarding sulh (compromise). The author first establishes two categories: (1) contracts that are independent of mentioning the principal (muwakkil), such as sale and hire, where the agent acts autonomously; and (2) contracts that require mentioning the principal, such as marriage and khul', where the agent acts merely as a messenger. Sulh, however, appears problematic because it is attributed to the principal whether based on acknowledgment (iqrar) or denial (inkar). The author (al-Hidayah) counts sulh upon acknowledgment in the first category alongside sale and hire. The commentator then resolves the objection by distinguishing: sulh upon acknowledgment is essentially a sale (exchange of property for property), so it is truly attributed to the agent—rights and obligations belong to the agent, not the principal. In contrast, sulh upon denial is a redemption of the oath (fida' yamin)—it is a concession from the defendant to avoid swearing, so the agent is merely a messenger (safir) with no personal liability. Thus, the agent does not acquire rights or duties, and the compromise is attributed directly to the principal.

Key Terms
صلح عن إقرار (sulh 'an iqrar): A compromise where the defendant acknowledges the claim; treated like a sale.
صلح عن إنكار (sulh 'an inkar): A compromise where the defendant denies the claim; treated as a redemption of oath.
وكيل (wakil): Agent appointed to act on behalf of another (muwakkil).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Sulh 'an inkar (based on denial)
B) Sulh 'an iqrar (based on acknowledgment)
C) Both types
D) Neither type

Key Takeaway
The classification of sulh in agency hinges on whether the claim is admitted or denied, determining the agent's role as independent actor or mere messenger.

Interpreting 'Food' in Agency: Custom Overrides Literal Meaning
Ruling on interpreting 'food' in agency contracts according to custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how Hanafi jurists rely on custom ('urf) to interpret ambiguous terms in agency contracts and evaluate the role of istihsan in overriding qiyas.

Core Idea
Custom ('urf) overrides literal meaning in agency: when your agent buys 'food', he buys wheat, not everything edible.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on interpreting the term 'food' (طعام) in an agency contract to purchase food. The source text presents a dispute: should 'food' be taken literally (all edibles) or according to market custom? The Hanafi school, through istihsan, gives preference to custom to avoid contractual uncertainty. You will trace the reasoning and understand when custom trumps literal interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Ruling
The agency to purchase 'food' is valid, but the interpretation of 'food' depends on the prevailing custom in transactions.
This is the mufta bihi position from later Hanafi authorities.
2
Contrast Qiyas vs Istihsan
Qiyas (literal meaning) would include all edibles, but istihsan (juristic preference based on custom) restricts the meaning to wheat, flour, or bread.
Istihsan overrides qiyas when custom provides a clear convention.
3
Examine the Opposing View
The three imams (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and al-Shafi'i) held that 'food' should be interpreted literally, citing consistency with its use in oaths.
They argued that literal meaning avoids alteration of the principal's intent.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response
Hanafi mashayikh noted that in sale and purchase contexts, custom restricts 'food' to grain, whereas in oaths no such custom exists; thus istihsan is justified.
The Kufan grain market was literally called the 'food market'.
5
Survey Intra-Hanafi Variations and Final Choice
Some Transoxiana scholars defined 'food' as meat dishes; the author synthesized: if a feast, food is bread; otherwise based on money amount: wheat for many, bread for few, flour for moderate.
The ruling adapts to changing custom in every era.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى الخبز في قليلة، وعلى الدقيق في متوسطة، وفي متخذ الوليمة على الخبز بكل حال هذه الوكالة ينبغي أن تكونَ باطلةً ؛ لأنَّ الطَّعامَ يقعُ على كلِّ ما يُطْعَم ، فيكون جهالة . جنسه فاحشة ، لكن المتعارف في قوله : اشترلي طعاما : أن يراد به الحنطة ، أو الدقيق، أو الخبز. وقال له : اشترلي بهذه الدراهم طعاماً ، ودفع الدراهم إليه، والطعام يحملُ هاهنا على البرِّ إن كانت الدراهم كثيرة، وعلى الخبز إن كانت قليلة، وعلى الدقيق إن كانت متوسطة، وهذا قولُ الفقيه أبي جعفر واعلم أنَّ القياس كان يقتضي أن يَحِلَّ الطعام على كل مطعوم اعتباراً بالحقيقة ؛ لأنَّ الطعام اسم لما يطعم ، كما يحملُ عليه في باب اليمين، فإنه إذا حلف أنه لا يأكل طعاماً فأكل فاكهة يحكم بكونه حانثاً ، فكذا يحمل هاهنا، وبه قالت الثلاثة، وعليه الفتوى، قال الصدر الشهيد له. كما صرح به العَيْنِي ) ، وغيره. لكن مشايخنا لما نظروا إلى أن العرف حمل الطعام على الحنطة ودقيقها إذا ذكر الطعام مقروناً بالبيع والشراء، ولا عرف في اليمين بالأكل، حكموا هاهنا بأن يحمل الطعام على الحنطة ودقيقها استحساناً ، قيل : هذا في عرف أهل الكوفة، فإنَّ سوق الحنطة ودقيقها يسمى سوق الطعام، فأما في عرف غيرهم فينصرف إلى شراء كـل مطعوم. وقال بعض مشايخ ما وراء النهر : إنَّ الطعام في عرف ديارنا ما يمكن أكله من غير إدام، كاللحم المطبوخ والمشوي ونحوه، فينصرف إليه التوكيل دون الحنطة والدقيق والخبز. وقيل : إن كان ثمة وليمة فيحملُ الطعام على الخبز، سواء كانت الدراهم قليلةً أو كثيرة، وإلا فعلى الحنطة إن كانت الدراهم كثيرة، وعلى الخبز إن كانت قليلة، وعلى الدقيق إن كانت متوسطة، وهذا ما اختاره المصنف الله ، ولا منافاة بين ما ذكر في الاستحسان من الأقاويل، فإن كلها معوّل على العرف والعرفُ يختلف في كل عصر.
And upon bread when the dirhams are few, and upon flour when moderate, and for the one arranging a feast, upon bread in all cases. This agency should be invalid because food applies to everything edible, so there is gross ignorance of its genus. But the customary understanding when one says 'buy food for me' is that wheat, flour, or bread is intended. And if he said 'buy food for me with these dirhams' and handed the dirhams to him, then food is interpreted here as wheat if the dirhams are many, and as bread if few, and as flour if moderate. This is the saying of the jurist Abu Ja'far. Know that qiyas would require that food be interpreted as every edible thing, considering the literal meaning, because food is a name for what is eaten, as it is carried in the matter of oaths. For if one swears that he will not eat food and then eats fruit, it is judged that he has broken his oath. So likewise it should be interpreted here. This is what the three (imams) said, and the fatwa is upon it, as stated by al-Sadr al-Shahid and others. But our mashayikh, when they looked at the fact that custom restricts food to wheat and its flour when food is mentioned in connection with sale and purchase (and there is no custom in oaths regarding eating), ruled here that food is interpreted as wheat and its flour by istihsan. It is said that this is in the custom of the people of Kufa, for the market of wheat and its flour is called the food market. As for the custom of others, it refers to the purchase of every edible thing. And some mashayikh of Transoxiana said: 'Food in the custom of our lands is what can be eaten without condiment, like cooked meat, grilled meat, and the like. So the agency refers to that, not to wheat, flour, or bread.' And it was said: If there is a feast, food is interpreted as bread, whether the dirhams are few or many. Otherwise, as wheat if the dirhams are many, bread if few, and flour if moderate. This is what the author chose, and there is no contradiction among the statements regarding istihsan, for all of them rely on custom, and custom varies in every age.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage discusses the interpretation of the word 'food' (طعام) in the context of an agency contract (wakala) to purchase food. The base text likely stated that the agency is valid and that 'food' is interpreted according to the quantity of dirhams given. The commentator expands by presenting multiple views. He begins by noting that if the agency is taken literally (qiyas), it would be invalid due to gross ignorance of genus because 'food' includes everything edible. However, the three imams (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and al-Shafi'i) held that the literal meaning applies, making the agency valid but referring to all edibles, and the fatwa is on that. The Hanafi mashayikh (such as the author's teachers) considered custom (urf): when 'food' is used in the context of buying and selling, custom restricts it to wheat and its flour in the Kufan custom. In oaths, no such custom exists. So they ruled by istihsan that 'food' means wheat/flour. Some Transoxiana scholars defined 'food' as meat dishes. Others tied the interpretation to context (feast or not) and quantity of money. The author chose the latter view, which integrates custom. The key point is that the ruling is not fixed; it adapts to changing customs.

Key Terms
طعام (ta'am): In this passage, 'food' is interpreted restrictively in agency contexts based on custom, not literally as all edibles.
عرف (urf): Customary practice that determines the intended meaning of a word in a specific context, here the marketplace convention for 'food'.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that departs from strict qiyas in favor of custom or necessity; here it allows restricting 'food' to wheat/flour.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author's chosen opinion in the passage, if an agent is given 500 dirhams (a moderate amount) to buy 'food' and there is no feast, what should he buy?
A) Wheat
B) Bread
C) Flour
D) Any edible item

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, custom ('urf) governs the interpretation of ambiguous terms in contracts, overriding literal meaning through istihsan.

Invalidity of Agency with Gross Ignorance of Genus
Ruling on agency to buy items where genus is grossly unknown

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why an agency contract is invalid when the object's genus is grossly unknown, and identify when specifying a type renders it valid.

Core Idea
If the principal orders purchase of something whose genus includes multiple distinct categories, the agency is void even if the price is fixed.

This lesson examines a key principle in Hanafi agency law: the invalidity of an agency when the object to be purchased suffers from gross ignorance (jahalah fahishah) regarding its genus. The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains that terms like 'slave,' 'cloth,' and 'animal' cover multiple genera, making it impossible for the agent to know the principal's true intent. We will see why specifying the price does not cure this defect, and how mentioning a lower genus (type) can salvage the contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject matter and the core ruling
Recognize that the passage deals with agency (wakala) to purchase an object. The core ruling: if the object's genus is grossly unknown, the agency is invalid (batil).
The ruling is stated at the beginning: 'ولا يصح بشراء شيءٍ فَحُشَ جَهْلُ جنسه'.
2
Understand the definition of genus (jins)
The passage defines genus: two things are of the same genus if they share reality (haqiqah) and purpose (maqasid). If either differs, they are different genera.
This definition is stricter than other schools and is key to determining gross ignorance.
3
Apply the definition to the examples
Consider the examples given: slave (male/female differ in purpose), cloth (many types), animal (horse, mule, donkey). Each term encompasses multiple genera, leading to gross ignorance.
The agent cannot know which specific item the principal intends, even if the price is specified.
4
Recognize the exception: specifying a type (naw')
If the principal mentions a type (e.g., donkey, Harawi cloth), the ignorance is reduced, and the agency becomes valid. The type is the 'lower genus' in fiqh terminology.
The secret is that such specification makes the purpose achievable and the agency enforceable.
5
Determine the consequences of an invalid agency
If the agent purchases despite the invalid agency, the purchase is for the agent himself, not the principal. This is the relied-upon view as stated in al-Nihayah.
This protects the principal from unintended obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يصح بشراء شيءٍ فَحُشَ جَهْلُ جنسه كالرقيق، والثوب، والدابة، وإن بين ثمنه، اعلم أن كل شيئين يتحدُّ حقيقتهما ومقاصدهما ، فهما من جنس واحد، وإن اختلفت الحقيقة أو المقاصد فهما من جنسين، فإن فَحُشَ جهالةُ الجنس بأن قد ذكر جنساً تحتَهُ أجناس كالرقيق فإنَّهُ ينقسم إلى ذكرٍ وأُنثى، وهما في بني آدم جنسان ؛ لاختلاف المقاصد ، ثُمَّ كلّ منهما قد يقصد منه الجمال كما في التركي، وقد يقصد منه الخدمة كما في الهندي، وكذا الثَّوب والدَّابة فلا تصح الوكالة بشراء هذه الأشياء وإن بينَ الثَّمن. إلا إذا ذكر نوع الدابة كالحمار : المراد بالنوع هاهنا الجنس الأسفل في اصطلاح الفقهاء [١] قوله : ولا يصح ... الخ ؛ يعني لا يصح التوكيل بشراء شيء يشمل أجناساً فيفحش جهالة جنسه كالرقيق والثوب ،والدابة فإنّ فيها جهالة فاحشة، فإنَّ الدابَّةَ اسم لما يدب على وجه الأرض لغةً، وعرفاً : للخيل والبغل والحمار فقد جمع أجناساً، وكذا الثوب فإنّه يتناولُ الملبوس من الأطلس إلى الكساء ، ولا يقبح تسمية المهر، وكذا الرقيق، فإنّه شامل للذكر والأنثى المختلفين في بني آدم ووجه عدم صحة التوكيل ظاهر ، إذا لم يبيّن الثمن، وكذا إذا بين؛ لأنه يتعذَّر الامتثال لأمر الموكل ؛ لأنَّ بذلك الثمن يوجد من كل جنس، ولا يدري مراد الأمر ؛ لتفاحش الجهالة، وإذا اشترى الوكيل في هذه الصور يقع الشراء للوكيل لا للموكل. صرَّح به في «النهاية»، وغيرها. [٢] قوله : إلا إذا ذكر ...الخ ؛ يعني إلا إذا ذكر نوع الدابة كالحمار، فجاز التوكيل به، وكذا إذا ذكر نوع الثوب كالهروي ،مثلاً ، والسرُّ فيه أنَّ مثل هذه الجهالة لا تخل بالمقصود، ويمكن رفعه بصرف التوكيل إلى ما يليق بحال الموكل كما تقدم. (۳) قوله : المراد.... الخ ؛ اعلم أنهم قالوا : إنَّ الجنس ما يدخل تحته أنواع شعائرية، والنوع اسم لأحد ما يدخل تحت اسم فوقه ، وقيل : الجنس اسم دال على كثيرين
And it is not valid to buy something whose ignorance of its genus is gross, such as a slave, cloth, and an animal, even if its price is specified. Know that any two things that share the same reality and purposes are of one genus, and if reality or purposes differ, they are of two genera. So if ignorance of the genus is gross, such as if a genus is mentioned that includes genera under it, like a slave (raiq) — for it divides into male and female, and in humans they are two genera due to differing purposes; then each of them may be desired for beauty, as in the Turkish, or for service, as in the Indian — and similarly for cloth and animal, the agency to buy these things is not valid even if the price is specified. Except if he mentions the type of animal, like a donkey: 'type' here means the lower genus in the terminology of the jurists. [1] His saying: 'And it is not valid...' meaning: it is not valid to authorize the purchase of something that encompasses genera, causing gross ignorance of its genus, like a slave, cloth, and an animal. For in them there is gross ignorance, because the term 'animal' linguistically means what walks on the earth, and customarily it refers to horses, mules, and donkeys, thus it includes genera. Similarly, cloth encompasses garments from atlas to coarse wool, and it is not inappropriate to call something a 'mare' (?) ; likewise, a slave includes male and female, who differ among humans. The reason for invalidity is clear: if the price is not specified, and also if it is specified, because compliance with the principal's order is impossible — for with that price, items from every genus exist, and the intent of the order is unknown due to gross ignorance. If the agent buys in these cases, the purchase is for the agent, not the principal. This is stated in al-Nihayah and others. [2] His saying: 'Except if he mentions...' meaning: except if he mentions the type of animal, like a donkey, then the agency is valid. Likewise, if he mentions the type of cloth, like 'Harawi', for example. The secret is that such ignorance does not defeat the purpose, and it can be resolved by directing the agency to what suits the principal's condition, as previously explained. (3) His saying: 'What is meant...' Know that they said: Genus is that under which there are sub-types (anwa' shai'iriyyah), and type is a name for one of the things that fall under a higher name. And it was said: Genus is a name indicating many...
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage explains a principle in agency (wakala): if a principal authorizes an agent to purchase something that includes multiple distinct genera (ajnas), such as a slave (which includes male and female, with different purposes), cloth (which includes many types), or an animal (which includes horses, mules, donkeys), the agency is invalid even if the price is specified. The reason is that the agent cannot know which specific item the principal intends, making compliance impossible. The commentator then defines jins (genus) and naw' (type): if two things share reality and purpose, they are one genus; otherwise, different genera. Gross ignorance occurs when the term covers multiple genera. The exception is if the principal specifies a type (naw') like a donkey (the lower genus in fiqh terminology), which removes the gross ignorance. The secret is that such specification makes the purpose achievable. The purchase by the agent in an invalid agency becomes for the agent himself, not the principal.

Key Terms
جنس (jins): Genus: a category that includes sub-categories; two things are of the same genus if they share reality and purpose, otherwise different genera.
نوع (naw'): Type: a lower sub-category under a genus; in fiqh terminology, it is the 'lower genus' (al-jins al-asfal) that specifies the intended item.
جهالة فاحشة (jahalah fahishah): Gross ignorance: uncertainty that prevents the agent from knowing the principal's intent and makes compliance impossible, invalidating the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is an agency to buy a 'slave' invalid even if the price is specified?
A) Because slaves cannot be sold
B) Because 'slave' includes both males and females with different purposes, causing gross ignorance of genus
C) Because the price must be exactly determined
D) Because the agent must be present at the time of the contract

Key Takeaway
An agency contract is void if the object's genus is grossly unknown, even if the price is fixed; specifying a type (lower genus) removes the ignorance and validates the contract.

Agency to Buy with Debt: Unspecified Item
Intra-Hanafi dispute on validity of agency with unspecified debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on agency with unspecified debt and identify which ruling is mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Dirhams and dinars are specified in agency: if the debt is not a specific item, the agency is invalid per Abu Hanifa.

This passage examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: when a creditor orders his debtor to purchase an unspecified item using the debt, is the agency valid? The answer determines who bears the loss if the item perishes. Abu Hanifa and his two companions differ on whether dirhams and dinars are considered specified (mut'ayyan) in agency contracts. Understanding this disagreement sharpens your ability to trace how a single legal principle—specification of currency—leads to divergent rulings and practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and the legal question
Identify the parties: a creditor (principal) instructs his debtor (agent) to buy a slave using the debt, without specifying which slave. The purchased slave perishes in the debtor's hand. The question: Is the agency valid, and who bears the loss?
The debt is a specific sum (e.g., 1000 dirhams) owed by the agent to the principal.
2
Identify Abu Hanifa's position and his dalil
Abu Hanifa rules the agency invalid. His proof: dirhams and dinars are specified in agency contracts, so restricting the agency to this debt makes the purchase a transfer of debt (tamlik al-dayn) without the debtor's consent, which is invalid. Therefore, the purchase is on the agent; the item belongs to the principal only if he takes possession.
Key principle: debts are not owned in hand, so transferring them to a third party is invalid without authorization.
3
Identify the Sahibayn's position and their dalil
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold the agency valid. Their proof: dirhams and dinars are not specified in transactions (mu'awadat), so the agency is unrestricted. Upon the agent's receipt, the item becomes the principal's property. The loss after receipt is on the principal.
They analogize to a debtor ordered to give charity from the debt.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response and tarjih
The commentator rejects the analogy to charity because charity is for Allah, which specifies the purpose like specifying a seller. They reaffirm that dirhams/dinars are specified in agencies: if the agency is tied to them, their loss or waiver terminates the agency. The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.
The specification is proven by the fact that restricting an agency to a particular debt makes the debt a specified entity.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa, if the item perishes before the principal takes possession, the agent bears the loss. Under the Sahibayn, the principal bears the loss after the agent's receipt. This difference affects financial liability in such agency transactions.
The thamarah al-khilaf is the allocation of risk between principal and agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوكالة باب الوكالة بالبيع والشراء وفي غير عين إن هلك في يد الوكيل هلك عليه، فإن قبضه آمره فهو له. أي أمره أن يشتري بالألف الذي له على المأمور عبداً، ولم يعين العبد، فاشتراه فمات في يد المأمور فهلاكه عليه، ولا يصير للآمر إلا أن يقبضه، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة بناءً على أن الوكالة لم تصح؛ لأن الدراهم والدنانير تتعين في الوكالات، فيكون الشراء مقيداً بذلك الدين، فيصير تمليك الدين من غير من عليه الدين بلا توكيل ذلك الغير، وهذا لا يصح بخلاف ما إذا كان العبد متعينا، فإن البائع يصير حينئذ وكيلاً بقبض الدين فيصح تمليك الدين. وعندهما: إذا قبض المأمور يصير ملكاً للآمر؛ لأن الدراهم والدنانير لم تتعين فلم يتقيد التوكيل بالدين، فصحت الوكالة، فيكون للآمر. وجوابه: ما مر أنها تتعين في الوكالات، فإنه إذا قيد الوكالة بها عيناً كانت أو ديناً، فهلكت أو سقط الدين تبطل الوكالة. وهذا كما إذا قال: أعطه ما لي عليك من شئت، فإنه باطل؛ لأنه أمر بصرف ما لا يملكه إلا بالقبض إلى من يختاره المديون بنفسه، بخلاف ما إذا عين الموكل البائع، فإن التوكيل صحيح لازم للآمر؛ لأنه يصير البائع أولاً وكيلاً عنه في القبض ثم يتملكه، وهذا ليس تمليك من غير من عليه، وليس أمراً بصرف ما لم يقبض، كما لو وهب دينه على غيره، ووكل الموهوب له لقبضه. كذا في شروح الهداية.
Book of Agency, Chapter of Agency for Sale and Purchase. And regarding that which is not a specific thing: if it perishes in the hand of the agent, the loss is upon him; if the principal takes possession of it, it belongs to him. That is, he ordered him to buy a slave with the one thousand that he is owed by the agent, and he did not specify the slave, so he bought it and it died in the agent's hand, so its loss is upon him, and it does not become for the principal unless he takes possession. And this is according to Abu Hanifa, based on the fact that the agency was not valid, because dirhams and dinars are specified in agencies; thus the purchase becomes restricted to that debt, so it becomes a transfer of the debt from the one who is not the debtor without authorization of that other person, and this is invalid, unlike when the slave is specified, for then the seller becomes at that time an agent for receiving the debt, so the transfer of the debt is valid. And according to the two companions: if the agent takes possession, it becomes property for the principal, because dirhams and dinars are not specified, so the agency was not restricted by the debt, thus the agency is valid, so it belongs to the principal. And the response to them is what has passed: that they are specified in agencies, for if he restricted the agency to them whether they were a specific object or a debt, then if they perish or the debt is waived, the agency becomes void. And this is like when he said: Give what I have owed to you to whomever you wish, for it is invalid, because it is an order to spend what he does not own except after receipt to whomever the debtor chooses himself, unlike when the principal specifies the seller, for then the agency is valid and binding upon the principal, because the seller first becomes an agent for receipt then takes ownership, and this is not a transfer from one who is not the debtor, and it is not an order to spend what has not been received, just as if he gifted his debt to someone else and appointed the donee to receive it. Thus in the commentaries of Al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding agency when a creditor instructs his debtor to purchase an unspecified item (e.g., a slave) using the debt owed. The core issue is whether the dirhams and dinars (the debt) are considered specified (تعين) in agency contracts. Abu Hanifa holds that they are specified, thus the agency is invalid because it amounts to an unauthorized transfer of debt to a third party. If the agent buys the slave and it perishes before the principal takes possession, the loss is on the agent. If the principal later takes possession, it becomes his. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that dirhams and dinars are not specified in transactions, so the agency is valid; the agent's purchase binds the principal, and if the item perishes after the agent takes delivery, it is the principal's loss. The commentary follows Abu Hanifa's position, reinforcing that dirhams/dinars are specified in agency contexts: restricting an agency to a specific debt or coinage makes them specified, and their loss or waiver terminates the agency. The text further distinguishes the case from a valid agency by analogizing to an invalid order to give unspecified property to a third party, versus a valid agency where the seller is specifically named as sub-agent for receipt.

Key Terms
تتعين (tata'ayyan): Dirhams and dinars are considered specified (assigned to a particular debt or object) in agency contracts, meaning they are treated as distinct entities rather than generic currency.
تمليك الدين (tamlik al-dayn): Transfer of ownership of a debt from the creditor to another person without the debtor's consent, which is generally invalid in Hanafi law.
وكيل (wakil): An agent appointed to perform a transaction (here, purchase) on behalf of the principal. The validity of the agency depends on the specification of the subject matter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a creditor instructs his debtor to buy a slave using the debt without specifying the slave, and the purchased slave perishes in the debtor's hand, who bears the loss?
A) The creditor (principal)
B) The debtor (agent)
C) The seller
D) The loss is shared equally

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school follows Abu Hanifa's view that dirhams and dinars are specified in agency, rendering an agency to purchase an unspecified item with a debt invalid; the agent bears the loss until the principal takes possession.
