Ruling on a Slave Buying Himself Through an Agent
Explaining the legal status of a slave's self-purchase via agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a slave's self-purchase via agency, explain the analogy to the depositee, and apply the principle that the transaction is treated as manumission for a price.

Core Idea
A slave's self-purchase is valid as manumission for a price, not a sale, and possession is immediate by contract.

This passage from the Hanafi commentary explains the legal status of a slave who buys himself, either directly or through an agent. It resolves apparent contradictions by treating the transaction as manumission rather than sale, using an analogy to a depositee buying deposited goods. The commentary also addresses objections regarding agency and literal interpretation.

Guided Path
1
State the baseline principle
Identify that a slave is legally capable of buying himself for himself or as an agent for another, because purchase is based on financial capacity.
2
Identify the key effect
Note that the seller (master) cannot detain the slave after the contract because possession is deemed immediate—the slave is already in his own hand.
This is based on the analogy to a depositee buying the deposit.
3
Trace the analogy
Compare to a depositee who buys the deposit: possession occurs by mere contract, so the seller cannot withhold the item.
The slave's possession of himself is analogous.
4
Handle the agency objection
If the slave is an agent to buy a specific item, buying himself is a different transaction (manumission) and is enforced on the agent as a contrary action.
The agent is bound by his contrary action.
5
Resolve the absolute utterance
When the slave says 'Sell me myself' without specifying for whom, the ambiguity prevents it from being compliance with agency, so it operates for the slave himself, resulting in freedom.
The master's intention is presumed to be manumission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأصلُه : أنَّ العبد يصلح أن يشتري نفسه لنفسه ، ولغيره بطريق الوكالة ؛ لأنَّ جواز الشراء باعتبار المالية والعبد أجنبي عن نفسه في حكم المالية ؛ إلا أنَّ البائع لا يملك حبس العبد حتى يستوفي البدل ؛ لأنَّ العبد في يد نفسه، فيكون قابضاً لنفسه بمجرد العقد، كما أنَّ المودع إذا اشترى الوديعة لا يكون للبائع حبس البيع ؛ لوجود القبض بمجرد العقد، سواء اشتراه لنفسه أو لغيره.
وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إذا أضاف العقد إلى الموكل كان ملكاً للموكل ، وإذا أضاف الشراء إلى نفسه بأن قال : بعني نفسي لنفسي، عتق لكونه قابضاً لنفسه بمجرد العقد كما تقدم آنفاً.
فإن قلت : إنَّ العبد وكيل بشراء شيء معين، فوجب أن لا يجوز له شراؤه لنفسه، بل يقع عن الآمر.
قلنا : الوكيل أتى بجنس آخر من التصرُّف ؛ لأنَّ بيع العبد من نفسه إعتاق على مال، وشراؤه قبول العتق ، فيكون مخالفاً ، فينفذ عليه ؛ لأنَّ الوكيل بشراء شيء معين ينفذ عليه عند المخالفة. وإن أطلق بأن قال : بعني نفسي، ولم يقل : لي ولا لفلان، عتق أيضاً ؛ لأنَّ المطلق يحتمل الوجهين فلا يقع امتثالاً بالشك، فيبقى التصرُّف واقعاً لنفسه. فإن قلت : إنَّ البيع حقيقة فيه، والعتق مجاز، فينبغي أن يحمل على الحقيقة عند التردد، إذ الحمل على الحقيقة هو الأصل بالاتفاق.
قلنا : الأصلُ إنَّ الإنسانَ يتصرُّفُ لنفسه فتعارض الأصلان فتساقطا، فيرجع إلى غرض المولى، فإنه لما اختلف التصرُّفان، فالظاهرُ أنَّ المولى يريدُ الإعتاق ، إذ بيع العبد من نفسه مطلقاً [إعتاق)، واقتصاره على إضافته إلى العبدِ دليل عليه، ولا يرضى بخروجه عن ملكه إلا الحرية، فيثبت له الولاء عليه.
ثمَّ الثمن هاهنا يكون في ذمَّةِ العبد في الوجهين، دون ذمة الآمر، أما إذا وقع الشراء له فظاهر، وأما إذا وقع للآمر ؛ فلأنه هو المباشر للعقد، فيرجع إليه الحقوق، فيطالب بالثمن ويرجع به على الآمر.
وفي شراء نفس الآمر من سيِّدِهِ بألف دَفَعَ إن قال لسيده : اشتريته لنفسه، فباعَهُ عتق عليه ، فإن لم يقل لنفسه كان لوكيله، وعليه ثمنه، والألف لسيده : أي قال عبد لرجل اشترِ لي نفسي من مولاي بألف ، ودَفَعَها إليه، فقال الوكيل : اشتريته لنفسه، فباعه، يكون إعتاقاً على مال ، وإن لم يقل لنفسه كان الشراء واقعاً من الوكيل فيكونُ الثَّمنُ على المشتري، وهذه الألف للمولى ؛ لأنَّه كسب عبده.
فإن قلت : العبد هاهنا محجور عليه، والوكيل إذا كان محجوراً عليه لا ترجع الحقوق إليه.
قلنا : إنَّ الحجر قد زال هاهنا بالعقدِ الذي باشره مقترناً بإذن المولى. ثم إذا كان الشراء للآمر فلا بُدَّ من قبولِ العبد ؛ لأنه بيع، فلا ينعقد إلا بالإيجاب والقبول، وإن وقع للعبد يكتفي بقول المولى بعت ولا يحتاج فيه إلى قبول العبد بعد قوله : بعني نفسي ؛ لأنه إعتاق، فيستبد به المولى، بناءً على أن الواحد يتولّى طرفي العقد في العتق كالنكاح ، ولا يتولى طرفي العقد في البيع. كذا في «التبيين»(۱)، وغيره.
(۱) تبيين الحقائق» (٤ : ٢٦٩).
And its principle: that the slave is fit to buy himself for himself, and for others by way of agency; because the permissibility of purchase is based on financial consideration, and the slave is a stranger to himself in financial rulings; except that the seller does not have the right to detain the slave until he receives the price, because the slave is in his own hand, so he becomes a possessor of himself by the mere contract, just as when a depositee buys the deposit, the seller does not have the right to detain the sale due to the existence of possession by the mere contract, whether he bought it for himself or for another. And when this is established, we say: If he attributes the contract to the principal, it becomes ownership of the principal; and if he attributes the purchase to himself, such as by saying: 'Sell me myself to myself', he becomes free because he is a possessor of himself by the mere contract as mentioned above. If you say: The slave is an agent for purchasing a specific thing, so it must not be permissible for him to buy it for himself, but it should be for the commander. We say: The agent has performed a different type of transaction, because the slave's selling himself is manumission for a price, and his purchasing himself is acceptance of manumission; thus he acts contraryly, and it is enforced upon him; because the agent for purchasing a specific thing is bound by his contrary action. And if he speaks absolutely, by saying: 'Sell me myself', without saying 'for me' or 'for so-and-so', he also becomes free, because the absolute utterance admits both possibilities, so it does not occur as compliance due to doubt, so the transaction remains effective for himself. If you say: The sale is literal in it, and manumission is metaphorical, so it should be interpreted literally when in doubt, because literal interpretation is the principle by consensus. We say: The principle is that a person transacts for himself, so the two principles oppose each other and cancel out; then we refer to the master's intention, because when two transactions differ, it is apparent that the master intends manumission, since the slave's selling himself absolutely is [manumission], and his restricting it to the slave is evidence of it, and he is not pleased with his leaving his ownership except by freedom, so his patronage is established over him. Then the price here is in the liability of the slave in both cases, not in the liability of the commander. As for when the purchase is for him, it is apparent; and as for when it is for the commander, because he is the direct actor in the contract, so rights revert to him; thus he is demanded for the price and then claims it back from the commander. And concerning the purchase of the commander himself from his master for a thousand paid: if he says to his master, 'I bought him for himself', and he sells him, he becomes free; and if he does not say 'for himself', it is for his agent, and the price is due from him, and the thousand is for his master. That is: a slave said to a man, 'Buy me myself from my master for a thousand', and gave it to him; then the agent said, 'I bought him for himself', and he sold him, it is manumission for a price; and if he did not say 'for himself', the purchase is effective from the agent, so the price is on the buyer, and this thousand is for the master because it is his slave's earnings. If you say: The slave here is under interdiction, and if the agent is under interdiction, rights do not revert to him. We say: The interdiction has been removed here by the contract that he directly performed, connected with the master's permission. Then if the purchase is for the commander, acceptance by the slave is necessary because it is a sale, so it is not concluded except by offer and acceptance; and if it is for the slave, the master's utterance 'I sold' suffices, and acceptance by the slave is not needed after his saying 'Sell me myself' because it is manumission, so the master acts independently, based on the principle that one person may handle both sides of a contract in manumission as in marriage, and does not handle both sides in a sale. Thus in 'al-Tabyin' (1) and others. (1) Tabyin al-Haqa'iq (4:269).
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling from the Hanafi school on the legal status of a slave who acts as an agent to purchase himself. The commentator begins by establishing the principle: a slave is legally capable of buying himself for himself or for others via agency, because the validity of purchase depends on financial capacity, and the slave is treated as a stranger to himself in financial matters. However, the seller cannot detain the slave after the contract because the slave is already in possession of himself by virtue of the contract, analogous to a depositee buying the deposit—possession is deemed to occur immediately. If the slave attributes the purchase to the principal (e.g., his master), the ownership goes to the principal; if he attributes it to himself (e.g., saying 'sell me myself'), he becomes free. The author then addresses an objection: if the slave is an agent to buy a specific item, he cannot buy it for himself. The response is that buying oneself is a different type of transaction (manumission for a price), so the agent acts contrary to his agency, and the contract is enforced on him. If the slave speaks absolutely without specifying, he also becomes free because the utterance is ambiguous and cannot be considered compliance with the agency. Another objection is raised: the sale is literal and manumission is metaphorical, so literal interpretation should prevail. The response refutes this by invoking two competing principles—literal interpretation vs. the principle that a person transacts for himself—which cancel out, leaving the master's intention to determine the outcome. The master is presumed to intend manumission because he would not accept losing his slave without compensation except for freedom. The price is in the slave's liability in both scenarios. The passage then gives a concrete example: a slave hires an agent to buy him from his master for 1000 dirhams, and the money is given. If the agent says 'I bought him for himself', the slave is freed; otherwise, the agent becomes the owner. A further objection about the slave's legal incapacity (hijr) is answered: the master's permission in the contract removes the interdiction for this transaction. The commentary concludes with a key procedural difference: if the purchase is for the commander (the slave's master), acceptance by the slave is required because it is a sale; if for the slave, the master's offer alone suffices because it is manumission, where one person can handle both sides of the contract, as in marriage. This ruling is sourced from 'al-Tabyin' and other Hanafi works.

Key Terms
الوكالة (al-wakālah): A contract by which one person authorizes another to perform a legal act on his behalf.
إعتاق على مال (i‘tāq ‘alā māl): Manumission of a slave in exchange for payment, where the slave is freed and owes a price.
القبض (al-qabḍ): Physical or constructive possession; here, the slave's being in his own hand qualifies as immediate possession by the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a slave says to his master 'Sell me myself' without specifying 'for me' or 'for so-and-so', what is the legal effect?
A) The slave becomes free because the transaction is interpreted as manumission.
B) The transaction is invalid because the utterance is ambiguous.
C) The slave remains a slave because no intention is established.
D) The master retains ownership because the slave is under interdiction.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a slave's self-purchase is legally manumission for a price, not a sale, and possession is deemed immediate by contract.

Agent Prohibited from Buying Specified Item for Himself
Ruling on agent appointed to buy a specific item not allowed to buy it for himself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on an agent buying a specified item for himself and identify the exception when the principal is present and the agent explicitly declares.

Core Idea
An agent appointed to buy a specific item cannot buy it for himself unless the principal is present and the agent explicitly declares the purchase for himself.

This lesson examines a specific rule of agency (wakalah) in Hanafi fiqh. When a principal appoints an agent to purchase a particular item, the agent is prohibited from buying that same item for himself. The ruling stems from the agent's fiduciary duty to act in the principal's interest. However, an exception exists if the principal is present at the time of the contract and the agent explicitly states that he is buying for himself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and general ruling
Recognize the act: the agent purchasing the specified item for himself. The general ruling: it is not permissible (la yajuz).
2
Differentiate between intention and explicit declaration
The prohibition applies whether the agent merely intends to buy for himself or explicitly states it, as long as the principal is absent.
3
Recognize the solitary exception
If the principal is present at the time of the contract and the agent explicitly declares the purchase for himself (e.g., 'Bear witness I bought this for myself'), the purchase becomes valid for the agent.
Both conditions must be met: principal present and agent declares.
4
Apply the exception to a hypothetical
Practice: Principal is at the shop, agent says 'I'm buying this for myself.' The purchase is valid. Contrast with principal absent: invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس للوكيل بشراء عين شراؤه لنفسه قوله : وليس للوكيل ... الخ؛ يعني من وكله رجل بشراء شيء معين فليس له أن يشتريه لنفسه، سواء نوى عند العقد الشراء لنفسه، أو صرح بالشراء لنفسه، بأن قال : اشهدوا أني قد اشتريت هذا الشيء لنفسي، وهذا إذا كان الموكل غائباً، فإن كان حاضراً وصرح الوكيل بالشراء لنفسه يصير مشترياً لنفسه.
And it is not for the agent to buy a specific item that he was appointed to buy for himself. His saying: 'And it is not for the agent...' etc. Means: If a man appoints him to purchase a specific thing, it is not permissible for him to purchase it for himself, whether he intends at the contract to purchase for himself, or he explicitly states that he is purchasing for himself by saying: 'Bear witness that I have purchased this thing for myself.' This is when the principal is absent. But if he is present and the agent explicitly states the purchase for himself, he becomes a purchaser for himself.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This is a concise ruling from the text of Al-Wiqayah stating that an agent who is appointed to buy a specific item cannot buy that same item for himself. The commentary clarifies that this prohibition applies regardless of whether the agent intends to buy for himself or explicitly declares it. The reason is that by appointing him, the principal has effectively prevented the agent from acting against his interest. However, there is an exception: if the principal is present at the time of the contract and the agent declares that he is buying for himself, then the purchase is valid for the agent. The commentary does not elaborate on the rationale here, but the general principle is that the agent's duty of loyalty precludes self-dealing without the principal's knowledge and consent.

Key Terms
عين (ayn): A specified item; here it means a particular thing that the agent is appointed to buy.
صرح بالشراء لنفسه (sarraha bil-shira'i li-nafsihi): To explicitly declare that the purchase is for himself, e.g., by saying 'I bear witness that I have bought this for myself.'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) If the principal is absent and the agent states the purchase is for himself
B) If the principal is present and the agent explicitly declares the purchase for himself
C) If the agent pays a higher price than the principal agreed to
D) Never, under any circumstances

Key Takeaway
An agent appointed to buy a specific item cannot buy it for himself; the only exception is when the principal is present and the agent explicitly declares the purchase for himself.

Agent Purchasing a Specified Item for Himself: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Liability
Ruling with intra-Hanafi dispute on agent buying for himself

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an agent purchasing a specified item for himself and evaluate the differences between Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and Zufar regarding liability.

Core Idea
An agent cannot buy for himself what he was appointed to buy; if he does, the purchase is for himself and liability depends on which imam's analogy is followed.

Agency law governs who owns an item when the agent steps beyond his mandate. This passage from the matn presents the ruling that an agent appointed to purchase a specific item may not buy it for himself, and then explores the fiqhi consequences if he nevertheless does. The debate revolves around whether the agent's detention of the item resembles a mortgage or a sale, leading to different determinations of liability upon destruction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
State that the agent appointed to buy a specific item may not purchase it for himself. This is the basic prohibition.
The prohibition applies when the principal is absent. If present and the agent declares, it becomes valid for the agent.
2
Note the key exception
If the principal is present and the agent explicitly declares the purchase for himself, the purchase is valid for the agent.
The text specifies 'if the principal is present and the agent declares, he becomes a purchaser for himself.'
3
Understand the base case scenario
If the agent buys for himself while the principal is absent, the purchase is for the agent, and he becomes liable for the price to the seller. The agent may detain the item from the principal to secure the price.
Before detention, the principal is considered to have taken possession through the agent's hand; loss falls on principal.
4
Explore the intra-Hanafi dispute on liability after detention
Abu Yusuf analogizes to mortgage: liability is the lesser of value and debt. Muhammad says it is guaranteed by the price. Zufar says full value due to transgression.
Abu Hanifa's view, upheld by the commentary, is that after detention it is like a sale: the price falls upon destruction, extinguishing the principal's debt to the agent.
5
Analyze the analogies and the hanafi response
The commentary refutes the mortgage analogy: detention can apply to a half-share (unlike mortgage), and the contract is annulled between agent and principal (unlike mortgage). The correct analogy is sale (mubadala hukmiyya).
The response also uses the hadith-like principle that the agent's hand is like the principal's hand before detention, and like the seller's hand after detention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوكالة / باب الوكالة بالبيع والشراء وليس للوكيل بشراء عين شراؤه لنفسه
وكذا عند أبي يوسف له ؛ لأنَّ الرَّهْنَ يُضمَنُ بأقل من قيمته ومن الدين، وعند محمد له يكون مضمونا بالثمن، وهو خمسة عشر.
(وليس للوكيل" بشراء عين شراؤه لنفسه
يحبسه من موكله يهلك على مال الموكل لا الوكيل، ولا يسقط ثمن المبيع عن الموكل، فيرجعُ الوكيل عليه ؛ لأن يده كيد الموكل ، فإذا لم يحبس يصير الموكل قابضاً بيده. وإن هلك المشتري في يد الوكيل بعد حبسه إيَّاه سقط الثمن عند الطرفين ؛ لأنه بمنزلة البائع منه ، وكان حبسه لاستيفاء الثمن، فيسقط بهلاكه، وعند أبي يوسف ، هو كالرهن فيكون مضموناً بالأقل من قيمته ومن الدين ؛ لأنه مضمون بالحبس للاستيفاء بعد أن لم يكن مضموناً به وهذا هو معنى الرهن بخلاف المبيع ؛ لأن البيع ينفسخ بهلاكه، وهاهنا لا ينفسخ أصل العقد.
قلنا : إن بينهما مبادلة حكميّة، كما بيناه سابقاً، فيكون معتبراً بالمبادلة، وهو البيع، ولا نُسَلِّمُ أنَّ العقد لا ينفسخ بل ينفسخ بينهما ، وإن لم ينفسخ في حق البائع كما لو رضي الوكيلُ بالعيب ولم يرض به الموكل ينفسخ العقد بينهما، وإن لم ينفسخ في حق البائع، والدليل على أنه ليس كالرهن أنه يثبت في النصف الشائع، والحبس بحكم الرهن لا يثبت فيه. وعند أبي يوسف له يهلك بالأقل من قيمته ومن الثمن، حتى لو كان الثمن أكثر من قيمته رجع الوكيلُ بذلك الفضل على موكله، وعند زفر له يضمن جميع قيمته ؛ لأنه ليس له أن يحبسه عنده، فبالحبس يكون متعدياً كالمودع الذي يمنعُ الوديعة
(1)
عن صاحبها، وإذا تأملت فيما قرّرناه سابقاً ظهر لك الجواب عمّا استدل به زفر ) [١] قوله : وليس للوكيل ... الخ ؛ يعني من وكله رجل بشراء شيء معين فليس له أن يشتريه لنفسه، سواء نوى عند العقد الشراء لنفسه، أو صرح بالشراء لنفسه، بأن قال : اشهدوا أني قد اشتريت هذا الشيء لنفسي، وهذا إذا كان الموكل غائباً، فإن كان حاضراً وصرَّحَ الوكيل بالشراء لنفسه يصير مشترياً لنفسه.

Book of Agency / Chapter of Agency in Sale and Purchase: And it is not for the agent to purchase a specific item for himself. Likewise, according to Abu Yusuf, he has it (the right) because the mortgage is guaranteed by the lesser of its value and the debt; according to Muhammad, he has it being guaranteed by the price, which is fifteen. (And it is not for the agent to purchase a specific item for himself.) He detains it from his principal; it is destroyed from the property of the principal, not the agent, and the price of the sold item does not fall from the principal, so the agent reclaims from him because his hand is like the hand of the principal. If he does not detain, the principal becomes a taker by his hand. And if the purchased item is destroyed in the agent's hand after he has detained it, the price falls according to both sides because he is like the seller from him, and his detention was to receive the price, so it falls by its destruction. And according to Abu Yusuf, it is like a mortgage, so it is guaranteed by the lesser of its value and the debt because it is guaranteed by detention for receipt after it was not guaranteed by it, and this is the meaning of mortgage, unlike the sold item because the sale is annulled by its destruction, and here the contract itself is not annulled. We say: There is a notional exchange between them, as we explained earlier, so it is considered by the exchange, which is sale, and we do not concede that the contract is not annulled, rather it is annulled between them, even if it is not annulled as far as the seller is concerned, just as if the agent was satisfied with a defect and the principal was not satisfied, the contract is annulled between them, even if it is not annulled as far as the seller is concerned. And the evidence that it is not like a mortgage is that it is established in half the share, and detention by way of mortgage is not established in it. And according to Abu Yusuf, it is destroyed by the lesser of its value and the price, such that if the price is more than its value, the agent reclaims that excess from his principal. And according to Zufar, he guarantees all of its value because he has no right to detain it with him, so by detention he becomes a transgressor like a depositary who prevents the deposit from its owner. And if you consider what we established earlier, the answer to what Zufar argued becomes clear to you. [1] His saying: 'And it is not for the agent... etc.' means: The one who appointed an agent to buy a specific item, he may not buy it for himself, whether he intended at the time of contract to buy for himself, or explicitly declared the purchase for himself, by saying: 'Bear witness that I have bought this thing for myself.' This is if the principal is absent. If he is present and the agent explicitly declares the purchase for himself, he becomes a purchaser for himself. (1) See: 'Al-Bahr al-Raiq' 7:157.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage opens with the ruling that an agent appointed to purchase a specific item may not purchase that item for himself. It then presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the consequences if the agent nevertheless does so. Imam Abu Hanifa's position (as understood from the matn) is that the purchase is not for himself but for the principal, and the agent's detention of the item is like a sale. Abu Yusuf analogizes it to a mortgage, where the item is guaranteed by the lesser of its value and the debt, and the contract is not annulled. Muhammad holds it is guaranteed by the price. Zufar holds the agent is a transgressor and guarantees the full value. The commentary explains that if the principal is absent, the agent cannot unilaterally appoint himself; if present and he declares the purchase for himself, it is valid for him. The preferred view is that the contract is annulled between the agent and principal, though not vis-à-vis the seller, analogous to a buyer accepting a defect without the principal's consent. The piece concludes by noting that the detention of the purchased item is not like a mortgage because it can apply to a half-share, unlike a mortgage.

Key Terms
وكالة (wakāla): Agency: a contract by which one person (principal) appoints another (agent) to perform a legal act on his behalf.
وكيل (wakīl): Agent: the person authorized by the principal to act on his behalf.
عين (ʿayn): A specific, identified item (as opposed to a generic description).
حبس (ḥabs): Detention: the agent detaining the purchased item to secure payment of the price.
رهن (rahn): Mortgage: a contract by which property is given as security for a debt.
مبادلة حكمية (mubadala hukmiyya): Notional exchange: a legal fiction by which the relationship between agent and principal is treated as a sale for the purpose of determining liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on an agent appointed to buy a specific item who purchases it for himself while the principal is absent?
A) The purchase is invalid and the item remains with the seller.
B) The purchase is valid for the agent, and he may not detain the item from the principal.
C) The purchase is valid for the agent, and if he detains the item and it is destroyed, the price falls (principal freed from paying).
D) The purchase is valid for the principal, and the agent must return the item.

Key Takeaway
An agent cannot buy for himself what he was appointed to buy; if he does, the purchase is for him, and after detention the principal's obligation to pay the price is extinguished upon destruction, placing the loss on the agent.

Agent's Purchase with Different Price Type – Hanafi Ruling
Ruling on agent buying with different price type, increase/decrease, and sub-agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling when an agent buys with a different type of price than specified, including the nuances of increase, decrease, and sub-agency.

Core Idea
An agent buying with a different price type buys for himself; a lower price may be valid if the description matches.

This passage from Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies the legal consequences when an appointed agent exceeds his authority regarding the price type. Understanding this ruling is essential for anyone dealing with commercial agency in Hanafi fiqh. The commentary not only states the basic rule but also explores subtle cases such as increase, decrease, and sub-agency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the violation
Determine what the agent did: bought the specified item with a different type of price (e.g., goods instead of cash), or bought someone else's item, or appointed a sub-agent.
The type of price (jins) includes cash vs. goods.
2
State the basic ruling
If the agent violates the price type instruction, the purchase is for the agent, not the principal. This applies whether the principal is absent or present (if present and commands, it is for principal).
The reasoning is that the agent's violation constitutes an invalid self-discharge in the principal's absence.
3
Distinguish between increase and decrease
Increasing the price is always a violation. Decreasing the price may be permissible if the item satisfies the description and the principal did not specify an exclusive price.
This nuance is from Al-Kafi of Al-Hakim.
4
Consider sub-agency
If the agent appoints another person to buy, the purchase falls on the original agent, not the sub-agent or principal.
The text explicitly states this.
5
Understand the reasoning principle
The agent's deviation from instructions is treated as a self-discharge, which is only valid in the principal's presence. Compare with agency in marriage where attributing the contract to oneself is a clear violation.
This principle explains the different outcomes.

Source Text

زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية
فلو شَرَى بخلاف جنسِ ثَمَن سُمِّي ، أو بغيرِ النُّقُود، أو غيره بأمرِهِ بغيبته وقع له ، وبحضرته لأمره.
[1] فلو شَرَى بخلاف جنسِ ثَمَنٍ سُمِّي ، أو بغيرِ النُّقُود، أو غيره بأمرِه بغيبته وقع له ، وبحضرته لأمره) : أي إن وكل بشراء شيء معين، فالوكيل إن لم يخالف أمر الموكل، فالمشترى للموكل وإن خالف فللوكيل ، فالموكَّلُ إِن سَمَّى الثَّمن، فالوكيلُ إن اشترى بخلاف ذلك الجنس كان مخالفة، وإن لم يسم الثَّمن. والوجه في ذلك : إنَّ في شرائه لنفسه عزل نفسه عن الوكالة، وهو لا يملك عزل نفسه حين غيبة الموكل ، فلو كان الموكل حاضراً وصرح بأنه يشتريه لنفسه ، كان المشترى له ؛ لأنَّ له أن يعزل نفسه بحضرة الموكّل، وليس له أن يعزل نفسه من غير علمه ؛ لأنَّ فيه تعزيراً له. بخلاف ما إذا وكله أن يزوجه امرأةٌ معيَّنةً ، حيث جاز له أن يتزوج بها ؛ لأنَّ النكاح الذي أتي به الوكيل غير داخل تحت أمره ؛ لأنَّ الداخل تحت الوكالة نكاح مضاف إلى الموكل ، فكان مخالفاً بإضافته لنفسه ، فانعزل، وفي الوكالة بالشراء الداخل فيها مطلق غير مقيد بالإضافة إلى أحد، فكلُّ شيءٍ أتى به لا يكون مخالفاً به. صرح به في «الرمز»)، وغيره. [۱] قوله : فلو شرى...الخ ؛ يعني لو اشترى الوكيل ذلك العين بخلاف جنس ثمن مسمَّى من الموكل له أو اشتراه بغير النقود بأن اشتراه بالعرض أو بالحيوان، وقع الشراء للوكيل ؛ لأنه خالف أمره فنفذ عليه، وظاهر قوله : بخلاف جنس ثمن يقتضي أن لا يكون مخالفاً بما إذا سمى له ثمناً ، فزاد عليه أو نقص عنه. لكن ظاهر ما في «الكافي» للحاكم: إنّه يكون مخالفاً فيما إذا زاد لا فيما إذا نقص لأنه قال : وإن سمَّى ثمناً فزادَ عليه شيئاً لم يلزم الأمر، وكذلك إن نقص من ذلك الثمن [إلا أن لا يكون له وصف بصفة، وسمّى له ثمناً ، فاشترى بتلك الصفة بأقل ذلك الثمن، فيجوز على الآمر. عنه ، من وكذا يقعُ الشراء للوكيل إن أمر الوكيلُ غيره فشراهُ الغير، وهو الوكيل الثاني (۱) رمز الحقائق»(۲ : ۱۲۳).
Cream of the Finale for the Support of Care: If he buys with a different type of price specified, or without cash (currency), or someone else by his command in his absence, it falls for him (the agent), and in his presence for his command. [1] 'If he buys with a different type of price specified, or without cash, or someone else by his command in his absence, it falls for him, and in his presence for his command.' Meaning: If he appoints to buy a specific thing, the agent, if he does not violate the principal's order, the purchase is for the principal, and if he violates, it is for the agent. So if the principal specified the price, and the agent buys with a different type, it is a violation, and if he did not specify the price. And the reasoning is that in his buying for himself he discharges himself from the agency, and he has no right to discharge himself during the absence of the principal. If the principal is present and he explicitly declares that he buys it for himself, it becomes a purchase for him because he can discharge himself in the presence of the principal, and he cannot discharge himself without his knowledge because that involves harming him. Contrary to the case where he appoints him to marry a specific woman, where it is permissible for him to marry her because the marriage performed by the agent is not included under his order, because what is included under the agency is a marriage attributed to the principal, so he violates by attributing it to himself, so he is discharged. In the agency for purchase, what is included is absolute, not restricted to attribution to anyone, so whatever he does is not a violation. This is explicitly stated in 'Al-Ramz' etc. [1] His saying: 'If he buys... etc.' means: If the agent buys that specific item with a different type of price specified by the principal, or buys it without cash, meaning with a commodity or animal, the purchase falls for the agent because he violated his order so it is binding on him. The apparent meaning of 'different type of price' implies that he would not be violating if he increased or decreased the amount when he specified a price. But the apparent in 'Al-Kafi' of Al-Hakim is that he is violating in case of increase, not in case of decrease, because he said: If he specified a price and he increases something, it is not binding on the principal; likewise if he decreases from that price, except if there is no description and he specified a price and he buys with that description at less than that price, it is permissible for the principal. And likewise, the purchase falls for the agent if the agent commands someone else and that person buys, and that person is the second agent. (1) 'Ramz al-Haqaiq' 2:123.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling that if an agent appointed to purchase a specific item violates the principal's instructions regarding the type of price (e.g., he uses a different currency or buys with goods instead of cash), the purchase is for the agent himself, not the principal, unless the principal is present and commands it. The commentary explains that the agent's violation constitutes a self-discharge from agency, which is impermissible without the principal's knowledge. It distinguishes between agency in purchase (where the act is absolute) and agency in marriage (where attributing to oneself violates the order). It also discusses the applicability of increase or decrease in price, citing Al-Kafi of Al-Hakim: increasing is a violation, but decreasing may be permissible under certain conditions. Finally, if the agent appoints a sub-agent, the purchase falls on the original agent.

Key Terms
ثمن (thaman): Price: the monetary consideration in a sale.
جنس (jins): Type/category: the kind of currency or commodity used as price.
نقود (nuquud): Cash: coins or currency as opposed to goods (urud).
عزل النفس (azl al-nafs): Self-discharge: the agent unilaterally terminating his own agency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if an agent appointed to buy a specific item with cash buys it with a commodity instead?
A) The purchase is void.
B) The purchase is for the principal.
C) The purchase is for the agent.
D) The agent must seek permission.

Key Takeaway
When an agent violates the price type instruction (different jins, increase), the purchase is for the agent; decrease may be allowed if description matches; sub-agency also places liability on the original agent.

Proxy Violates Instructions by Buying with Different Price Type
Explaining ruling when proxy violates purchase instructions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a proxy buys with a different price type or without currency, and explain the legal reasoning (ʿillah) that makes the purchase binding on the proxy.

Core Idea
A proxy who violates the specified price type buys for himself, not the principal.

This lesson examines a key principle in Hanafi agency law: the consequence of a proxy's deviation from the principal's instructions. The passage from ʿUmdat al-Riʿāyah clarifies that when a proxy purchases with a different currency or without currency, the purchase belongs to the proxy. Understanding the reasoning behind this ruling—the proxy's inability to unilaterally terminate the agency in the principal's absence—reveals the delicate balance between authority and fidelity in Islamic contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the agency scenario
A principal appoints a proxy to buy a specific item and specifies the price type (e.g., silver coins). The proxy then buys the item using a different price type (e.g., gold coins) or without currency (by barter).
2
Apply the ruling: purchase belongs to proxy
According to the text, if the proxy violates the instruction by using a different price type or non-monetary exchange, the purchase is valid but belongs to the proxy, not the principal.
The ruling is not that the purchase is void; it is valid but for the proxy.
3
Understand the rationale (ʿillah)
The reasoning: buying for himself is equivalent to removing himself from the agency. He cannot do so in the principal's absence because it causes harm. In the principal's presence, he can declare he is buying for himself, then the purchase is for him.
This contrasts with agency for marriage, where the proxy can marry the woman himself because the marriage contract is annexed to the principal, making it a violation.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on price variation
The text notes a difference: some scholars (al-Kāfī) hold that increasing the price is a violation, but decreasing it is not if the item lacks a described attribute. The author of ʿUmdat al-Riʿāyah does not clearly resolve this, leaving the matn's generality as the apparent position.
The practical difference: if the proxy decreases the price, some Hanafis allow it for the principal's benefit.
5
Contrast with other violation scenarios
The passage also covers the case where the first proxy appoints a second proxy. If the second buys in the absence of the first, it is a violation; if in the presence, it is not, because the second is considered the principal's agent.

[۱] قوله : فلو شرى...الخ ; يعني لو اشترى الوكيل ذلك العين بخلاف جنس ثمن مسمَّى من الموكل له أو اشتراه بغير النقود بأن اشتراه بالعرض أو بالحيوان، وقع الشراء للوكيل ; لأنه خالف أمره فنفذ عليه، وظاهر قوله : بخلاف جنس ثمن يقتضي أن لا يكون مخالفاً بما إذا سمى له ثمناً ، فزاد عليه أو نقص عنه. لكن ظاهر ما في «الكافي» للحاكم: إنّه يكون مخالفاً فيما إذا زاد لا فيما إذا نقص لأنه قال : وإن سمَّى ثمناً فزادَ عليه شيئاً لم يلزم الأمر، وكذلك إن نقص من ذلك الثمن [إلا أن لا يكون له وصف بصفة، وسمّى له ثمناً ، فاشترى بتلك الصفة بأقل ذلك الثمن، فيجوز على الآمر.

If he buys with a different type of price that was specified, or without currency, or another (person) by his command in his absence, it occurs for him (the proxy), and in his presence, for the principal. That is, if he appointed him to buy a specific thing, and the proxy did not violate the command, then the purchase is for the principal, and if he violated, then for the proxy. If the principal specified the price, and the proxy bought with a different type, it is a violation. If he did not specify the price... The reasoning is that his purchasing for himself is a removal of himself from agency, and he does not have the right to remove himself while the principal is absent. If the principal is present and he declares that he is buying for himself, then the purchase is for him because he can remove himself in the presence of the principal. He cannot remove himself without his knowledge because it harms him. Contrary to the case where he appointed him to marry a specific woman, where he is allowed to marry her because the marriage he performed is not included under his command; the marriage under agency is annexed to the principal, so he violated by annexing it to himself, thus he is removed. In the agency for purchase, the included matter is absolute, not restricted to annexation, so whatever he does is not a violation. As stated in al-Ramz and others. [Footnote:] The saying 'if he buys...' means if the proxy bought that specific thing with a different type of specified price from the principal, or bought without currency, e.g., with goods or animals, the purchase occurs for the proxy because he violated his command, so it is binding on him. The apparent meaning of 'with a different type of price' implies that he is not in violation if he specified a price and then increased or decreased it. But the apparent meaning in al-Kafi by al-Hakim is that he is in violation if he increases, but not if he decreases, because he said: If he specified a price and then increased something, the command is not binding; similarly, if he decreased from that price, except if it has no described attribute, and he specified a price for a described attribute, then he bought with that attribute for less than that price, then it is permissible for the principal. Likewise, the purchase occurs for the proxy if the proxy ordered another and he (the other) bought it, i.e., the second proxy.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on agency (wakalah), specifically the section on agency for sale and purchase. It states a clear ruling: when a principal appoints a proxy to buy a specific item and specifies a price, if the proxy buys with a different type of currency (e.g., gold instead of silver) or without currency (e.g., bartering with goods), the purchase is for the proxy himself because he violated the instructions. The reasoning given is that the proxy cannot unilaterally terminate the agency in the principal's absence, but he can do so in the principal's presence. The text then contrasts this with agency for marriage, where a proxy can marry the specified woman himself because the marriage contract is attached to the principal, so the proxy's action is a violation. The footnote clarifies that the term 'violation' (mukhalafah) has variant readings, and discusses a dispute from al-Kafi: some say increasing the price is a violation but decreasing is not, unless the item lacks a described attribute. The passage also covers the case where the first proxy appoints a second proxy; if the second buys in the first proxy's absence, it is a violation, but in his presence it is not. The key point is that the proxy's authority is limited to carrying out the principal's instructions exactly.

Key Terms
وكيل (wakīl): A person appointed by another (the principal) to perform a specific legal act on his behalf.
موكل (muwakkil): The person who appoints the proxy (wakil) to act on his behalf.
مخالفة (mukhālafah): The act of the proxy deviating from the instructions given by the principal.
ثمن (thaman): The price specified by the principal for the purchase.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The principal, because the proxy intended to benefit him.
B) The proxy, because he violated the instruction by not using silver coins.
C) The seller, because the transaction is invalid.
D) The cow is owned jointly by the principal and proxy.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi agency, a proxy who buys with a different price type or without currency violates the mandate, and the purchase is valid but belongs to him, not the principal.

The Proxy's Purchase of an Unspecified Item: Ownership and Intention
Explains default ruling and exceptions for unspecified item purchase.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default ruling when a proxy buys an unspecified item and identify the two exceptions that transfer ownership to the principal.

Core Idea
A proxy buying an unspecified item owns it himself unless he attributes the contract to the principal or intends for him.

When a principal appoints a proxy to buy an unspecified item—like any slave or car—the default rule is that the purchase belongs to the proxy, not the principal. However, this changes if the proxy explicitly links the transaction to the principal's property or has the intention to buy for the principal. The passage also explores the validity of a sub-proxy's purchase and draws a critical distinction between agency in sale/purchase and agency in divorce/manumission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling
Restate the ruling: when a proxy buys an unspecified item (e.g., any slave), the item belongs to the proxy himself. This is the starting point.
The item is not specified in the agency agreement.
2
Recognize the two exceptions
List the two conditions that make the purchase belong to the principal: (1) the proxy explicitly adds the contract to the principal's property (e.g., 'I bought this with your money'), or (2) the proxy is absolute (does not specify) but internally intends to buy for the principal.
Both exceptions require a connection to the principal, either verbal or intentional.
3
Understand the custom-based ruling on currency
Note that buying without currency (e.g., barter) when the norm is cash is a violation. Custom ('urf') is treated like a stipulated condition.
This shows how Hanafi fiqh incorporates custom into contract law.
4
Distinguish sub-proxy scenarios
Analyze why a sub-proxy's purchase is valid in the presence of the first proxy but not in his absence. The key is the presence of the first proxy's judgment (ra'y).
Presence of the first proxy means his discretion is being exercised; absence means the sub-proxy acts alone.
5
Contrast agency roles in sale vs. divorce/manumission
Explain why a sub-proxy's divorce/manumission does not take effect even in the presence of the first proxy. The proxy for divorce is merely a messenger (rasul), whereas the proxy for sale is like an owner (malik) with independent authority.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the limits of agency in different contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي غير عين هو للوكيل ، إلا إذا أضاف العقد إلى مال آمره، أو أطلق ونوى له فإن اشترى بغيرِ النُّقود، كان مخالفة ) ; لأنَّ المتعارف هو الشراء بالنقود، والمعروف عرفاً كالمشروط شرطاً ، وإن اشترى غير الوكيل بأمره، لكن بغيبته يكونُ مخالفةً ، وإن كان بحضرتِهِ لا يكون مخالفة ; لأنه حضر رأيه  
[٢]  
(وفي غير عين هو للوكيل، إلا إذا أضاف العقد إلى مالِ آمره ، أو أطلق ونوى له ) : أي قال الوكيلُ : اشتريت بهذا الألف، والألفُ ملك الموكل، أو أطلق : أي قال : اشترى بألف مطلق من غير أن يقيد بألف هو ملك الموكل لكن نَوَى الشراء للآمر يكون للأمر.  
بغيبة الوكيل الأول لمخالفة أمر الآمر ; لأنه مأمور بأن يحضر رأيه ولم يتحقق ذلك في حال غيبته. وإن اشتراه الوكيل بحضرة الوكيل الأول، فشراؤه يقعُ للموكل ; لأنه يحضر رأيه حينئذ، فلا يكون مخالفاً، بخلاف الوكيل بالطلاق والعتاق، إذا وكل غيره، فطلق الثاني أو أعتق بحضرة الأوَّل، حيث لا ينفذ، وإن حضر رأيه. صرح بـه في «مجمع الأنهر»، وغيره. والسر فيه : إنَّ الوكيل في الطلاق والعتاق كالرسول فلا يتصرف من عنده لشيء بل يبلغ الرسالة، فإذا تصرف فقد خالف، فلا ينفذ، والوكيل في البيع والشراء بمنزلة المالك، وهو أصيل فيه، فله أن يتصرف ; ولهذا لو فعل ذلك غيره من غير توكيل منه فأجازة الوكيل جاز، وفي الطلاق والعتاق لم يجز. كما صرحوا به.  
[١]قوله : : مخالفة ; في أكثر النسخ بصيغة اسم الفاعل مضافةً إلى الضمير الراجع إلى الآمر ، فمعناه حينئذ أنَّ الوكيل يكون مخالفاً للآمـر ، وفي بعض النسخ بصيغة المصدر، فمعناه أن يكون ذلك الشراء بخلاف ذلك الجنس مخالفة بما أمره الآمر.  
[٢] قوله : وفي غير عين...الخ ; حاصله : إنه لو وكله بشراء عبد غير معين مثلاً ، فاشترى الوكيل عبداً فهو للوكيل، إلا إذا قال : نويتُ الشراء للموكل، أو اشتراه بماله ، وهذه المسألة على وجوه ; لأنَّ الوكيل : إما أن يضيف العقد إلى ثمن معين أو إلى مطلق الثمن.
And in an unspecified item, it is for the proxy, except if he adds the contract to the principal's property, or is absolute and intends for him. Then if he buys without currency, it is a violation because the customary practice is purchase with currency, and what is known by custom is like a stipulated condition. And if other than the proxy buys by his command, but in his absence, it is a violation, and if in his presence, it is not a violation because his opinion is present. [Explanation:] The saying 'And in an unspecified item, it is for the proxy, except if he adds the contract to the principal's property, or is absolute and intends for him' means that the proxy said 'I bought with this thousand, and the thousand is the property of the principal', or he is absolute, i.e., he said 'I bought with a thousand' without specifying that the thousand is the principal's property, but he intended the purchase for the principal, then it is for the principal. In the absence of the first proxy, it is a violation of the principal's command because he was commanded to have his opinion present, and that is not realized in his absence. If the proxy buys in the presence of the first proxy, his purchase occurs for the principal because his opinion is present then, so he is not in violation. Contrary to the proxy for divorce and manumission, if he appoints another, and the second divorces or manumits in the presence of the first, it is not effective, even if his opinion is present. As stated in Majma' al-Anhur and others. The secret is that the proxy in divorce and manumission is like a messenger, so he does not act on his own but conveys the message. If he acts, he has violated and it is not effective. The proxy in sale and purchase is like an owner, and he is an agent with authority, so he can act. For this reason, if another did that without his appointment and the proxy authorized it, it is permissible, but in divorce and manumission it is not permissible, as they have stated. [Footnote:] The saying 'violation' in most copies is in the form of the active participle added to the pronoun referring to the principal, so it means then that the proxy is a violator of the principal. In some copies, it is in the infinitive form, so it means that buying with that different type is a violation of what the principal commanded. [Footnote:] The saying 'And in unspecified item...' its summary is that if he appointed him to buy an unspecified slave, for example, and the proxy bought a slave, it is for the proxy, except if he said 'I intended the purchase for the principal' or he bought it with his property. This issue has multiple aspects because the proxy either adds the contract to a specific price or to an absolute price.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This section continues the discussion of agency for purchase. It first addresses the case where the proxy is authorized to buy an unspecified item (e.g., any slave). The default ruling is that such a purchase belongs to the proxy himself, not the principal. However, there are two exceptions: (1) if the proxy expressly adds the contract to the principal's property (e.g., says 'I bought this with the principal's money'), or (2) if he does not specify but has the intention to buy for the principal. In both cases, the purchase goes to the principal. The passage then discusses the scenario where the first proxy appoints a second proxy (sub-wakil). If the second proxy buys in the absence of the first, it is a violation because the first proxy's judgment is not present; if in his presence, it is valid. The text then draws an important distinction between agency for sale/purchase and agency for divorce/manumission. In the latter, the proxy is merely a messenger (rasul) and cannot delegate authority; his actions are not binding unless he follows instructions exactly. In contrast, the proxy for sale/purchase is treated like an owner (malik) and has discretion, so he can delegate and his actions are effective even if he exceeds his mandate in some ways.

Key Terms
عين (ʿayn): A specific item; here 'غير عين' means an unspecified item (e.g., any slave).
أضاف العقد (aḍāfa al-ʿaqd): To attribute the contract to the principal by explicitly stating that the purchase is with the principal's property.
وكيل بالطلاق (wakīl bil-ṭalāq): A proxy appointed to pronounce divorce on behalf of the husband.
رسول (rasūl): A messenger who only conveys the principal's decision without independent discretion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does a sub-proxy's divorce in the presence of the first proxy not take effect, while a sub-proxy's purchase does?
A) Because the first proxy is not present
B) Because the proxy for sale has more authority than the proxy for divorce
C) Because the proxy for divorce is like a messenger and cannot delegate
D) Because divorce requires witnesses

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a proxy buying an unspecified item owns it unless he explicitly links the purchase to the principal, and proxy roles differ fundamentally: sale/purchase proxies are like owners with discretion, while divorce/manumission proxies are mere messengers.

Ruling on Agent's Purchase of Unspecified Item
Determines ownership when agent buys unspecified item

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ownership when an agent buys an unspecified item and distinguish when an agent's deviation is considered mukhalafah.

Core Idea
An agent buying an unspecified item owns it unless he explicitly connects the contract to the principal's property.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies a key principle in Hanafi agency law: when an agent is authorized to purchase an unspecified item, the default ownership is for the agent himself, not the principal. The text then examines various scenarios—customary payment methods, presence of the principal, and a contrast with agents for divorce and manumission. Understanding this ruling prevents confusion about the agent's authority and liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling
Read the first sentence: for unspecified items, the purchase is for the agent unless he adds the contract to the principal's property or intends for him. This is the baseline.
The default is that the agent acts for himself.
2
Recognize the role of custom
The text states that buying with other than cash (e.g., barter) is considered mukhalafah because the customary method is cash. Custom is treated like a stipulated condition.
Custom can create binding expectations in agency.
3
Analyze presence vs. absence
When the second agent buys in the presence of the first agent, it is not mukhalafah because the first agent's opinion is considered present. In absence, it is mukhalafah.
Presence of the original agent cures certain deviations regarding the object of purchase.
4
Contrast with divorce/manumission agents
Agents for sale/purchase are like owners, able to delegate and ratify. Agents for divorce/manumission are like messengers, with no discretion; any deviation invalidates the act.
The underlying cause (sirr) is the degree of autonomy: owner-like vs. messenger-like.
5
Derive practical implications
Apply these rules to concrete scenarios: if an agent buys a different item than commanded but in the principal's presence, the purchase stands for the principal. If he uses a non-customary payment, he violates the command and is liable.
The mufta bihi position is that the purchase is valid but the agent may be liable for mukhalafah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي غير عين هو للوكيل ، إلا إذا أضاف العقد إلى مال آمره، أو أطلق ونوى له فإن اشترى بغيرِ النُّقود، كان مخالفة ) ؛ لأنَّ المتعارف هو الشراء بالنقود، والمعروف عرفاً كالمشروط شرطاً ، وإن اشترى غير الوكيل بأمره، لكن بغيبته يكونُ مخالفةً ، وإن كان بحضرتِهِ لا يكون مخالفة ؛ لأنه حضر رأيه بغيبة الوكيل الأول لمخالفة أمر الآمر ؛ لأنه مأمور بأن يحضر رأيه ولم يتحقق ذلك في حال غيبته. وإن اشتراه الوكيل بحضرة الوكيل الأول، فشراؤه يقعُ للموكل ؛ لأنه يحضر رأيه حينئذ، فلا يكون مخالفاً، بخلاف الوكيل بالطلاق والعتاق، إذا وكل غيره، فطلق الثاني أو أعتق بحضرة الأوَّل، حيث لا ينفذ، وإن حضر رأيه. صرح بـه في «مجمع الأنهر»، وغيره. والسر فيه : إنَّ الوكيل في الطلاق والعتاق كالرسول فلا يتصرف من عنده لشيء بل يبلغ الرسالة، فإذا تصرف فقد خالف، فلا ينفذ، والوكيل في البيع والشراء بمنزلة المالك، وهو أصيل فيه، فله أن يتصرّف ؛ ولهذا لو فعل ذلك غيره من غير توكيل منه فأجازة الوكيل جاز، وفي الطلاق والعتاق لم يجز. كما صرحوا به.
And for unspecified [item], it is for the agent, except if he adds the contract to the property of his principal, or speaks absolutely and intends for him. And if he buys with other than cash, it is an opposition, because the customary is purchase with cash, and what is known by custom is like a stipulated condition. And if the agent buys other than what he was commanded, but in his absence, it is opposition, and if in his presence, it is not opposition because his opinion was present. The absence of the first agent is opposition of the principal's command, because he is commanded to have his opinion present and that is not achieved in his absence. And if the agent buys in the presence of the first agent, his purchase falls for the principal because his opinion is present then, so he is not in opposition, unlike the agent for divorce and manumission: if he authorizes another, and the second divorces or emancipates in the presence of the first, it does not execute, even if his opinion is present. This is stated in Majma' al-Anhur and others. And the secret is: the agent in divorce and manumission is like a messenger; he does not act on his own for anything but rather conveys the message. If he acts, he has opposed, so it does not execute. The agent in sale and purchase is in the position of the owner; he is an agent and can act. Hence, if someone else does that without his authorization and the agent ratifies it, it is valid; but in divorce and manumission it is not valid, as they have stated.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from custom and analogy. The commentator expands on the base text's ruling. The key point: when an agent is authorized to buy an unspecified item (e.g., any slave), the purchase is for the agent himself—he owns it—unless he explicitly states that he is buying for the principal's property or intends it for him. The reason is that the default is the agent acts for himself. However, custom and law dictate that if he buys with cash (the customary method), it is a valid fulfillment of the agency; if he buys by barter or credit, it is a violation (khilaf) because the customary is cash. The presence or absence of the principal or the first agent also matters: if the second agent buys in the presence of the first agent, it is considered as if the first agent's opinion is present, so no violation. This is unlike the case of agent for divorce or manumission, where even if the second agent acts in the presence of the first, it is invalid. The commentator explains the underlying cause (sirr): the agent in sale/purchase is like an owner (asil) and can delegate, so ratification is possible; the agent in divorce/manumission is like a messenger (rasul) with no discretion, so any deviation invalidates the act. This distinction is crucial in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
غير عين (ghayr 'ayn): Unspecified item; i.e., the agent is authorized to buy a generic item not individually identified.
مخالفة (mukhalafah): Opposition or violation of the principal's command; here it refers to the agent buying in a way contrary to customary practice (e.g., not with cash) or buying something not authorized.
الوكيل في الطلاق والعتاق كالرسول (al-wakil fi al-talaq wa al-'itaq ka al-rasul): The agent for divorce and manumission is like a messenger; he has no independent authority and merely conveys the principal's action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The purchase is for the principal because the agent's opinion was present.
B) The purchase is for the agent because he did not add the contract to the principal's property, and the payment method is a violation.
C) The purchase is void because the payment method violates custom.
D) The purchase is for the agent and he is not liable for any loss.

Key Takeaway
An agent buying an unspecified item owns it unless he explicitly connects the contract to the principal, and deviations in payment method or object may constitute mukhalafah with liability.

Agent's Purchase with Absolute Price and the Dispute of Intention
Expansion of agency purchase rules with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an agent's purchase with an absolute price when intention is absent, and explain the disagreement between Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf on whether payment determines ownership.

Core Idea
When an agent buys with absolute price and no intention, the default goes to the agent according to Muhammad, but to the payer according to Abu Yusuf.

This lesson examines the detailed rules of agency (wakalah) when an agent purchases goods with an absolute price — not tied to a specific fund. The passage explores several scenarios based on intention and payment, culminating in an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf. Understanding this dispute clarifies how Hanafi jurisprudence balances default attribution (to the contracting party) with the clarifying role of payment.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish specified vs absolute price
Identify the legal effect of adding the contract to a specific price (e.g., 'with Zaid's dirhams') — the purchase is for the owner of that price, based on custom and law. Then, contrast with an absolute price (e.g., 'for 100 dirhams') which creates ambiguity.
A specific price overrides hidden intention; an absolute price requires further evidence.
2
Analyze the absolute price scenarios
The passage lists three sub-cases: (1) both agree on intention for someone — then that person owns; (2) they differ on intention — then payment decides by consensus; (3) they agree no intention — this is where the dispute arises.
In scenarios 1 and 2, payment or consensus resolves ownership.
3
Compare the two positions on no intention
Imam Muhammad: the purchase is for the agent (contracting party) because absolute acts are attributed to the doer, analogized to the one ordered to perform Hajj without specifying. Abu Yusuf: payment decides because the absolute contract is ambiguous and payment clarifies it.
The analogy to Hajj is contested: worship requires intention, transactions do not require intention for validity.
4
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If the agent pays from his own money, Muhammad says the goods are his; Abu Yusuf says the goods belong to the principal. Conversely, if the agent pays from the principal's money, Muhammad still says the goods are the agent's (but he must guarantee), while Abu Yusuf says they are the principal's.
This difference impacts liability and ownership in real transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن أضافه إلى ثمن معيَّن كان الشراء لصاحب ذلك الثمن ؛ لأنَّ الظاهر أنه يضيف الشراء إلى مالِ مَن يشتريه له ، وهذا لأنَّ الثمن وإن كان لا يتعيَّن، لكن فيه شبهة التعين من حيث سلامة المبيع به وتعين قدره ووصفه ؛ ولهذا لا يطيب له الربح إذا اشترى بالدراهم المغصوبة ودينه يمنعه من أن يشتريه لنفسه بإضافة العقد إلى مال غيره ؛ لأنَّ ذلك مستنكر شرعاً وعادةً فلا يرتكبه. وقد جرى العرف فيما إذا اشترى لنفسه أن يضيف العقد إلى ماله، وهو ليس بمستنكر شرعاً وعرفاً، فيكون المشترى لمالك ذلك الثمن، وإن نوى خلاف ذلك جرياً على مقتضى العرف والشرع، ثمَّ إذا نقد من مال الموكل فيما اشتراه لنفسه يجب عليه الضمان. وإن أضافه إلى ثمن مطلق، فلا يخلو : إما أن يكون حالاً أو مؤجّلاً. فإن كان حالا فلا يخلو إمّا أن يتصادقا على وجود النية لأحدهما، أو على عدمها، أو يختلفا فيه. فإن كان الثمن حالاً واتَّفقا على وجودِ النيَّة لأحدهما كان من نوى له ؛ لأنَّ الثابت باتفاقهما كالثابت عياناً، واتفاقهما حجّة عليهما ، ولا يلتفت إلى النقد، ولا عبرة به في هذه الحالة ؛ لأنَّ له أن يشتري لنفسه وللموكل ، فإذا عينه بنية فقد تعيَّن ، ويكون بالنقد من مال الموكل غاصباً فيما إذا نواه لنفسه. وإن اختلفا في النية يُحَكِّمُ النقد بالإجماع ؛ لأنَّ دلالته على التعيين مثل دلالته إضافة الشراء إليه ؛ لأنَّ الظاهر أن يفعل ما يجوز له شرعاً، أو يجري على عوائده. وإن اتَّفقا على أنه لم تحضره النيّة ، فعند محمّد الله هو ا للعاقد ؛ لأنّ ما يطلقه الإنسان من التصرفات يكون لنفسه، فصار كالمأمور بالحج إذا أطلق ولم ينو أنـه للمحجوج عنه، وعند أبي يوسف يُحَكِّمُ النقد ؛ لأنَّ المطلق يحتمل، فبقي موقوفاً، فمن أي المالين نقد فقد عيَّنَ المحتمل به، فصار كحالة التكاذب. بخلاف المأمور بالحج، فإنّ الحج ،عبادة، وهو لا يتأدَّى إلا بالنية، فكان مأموراً بأن ينوي الحج عن المحجوج عنه ، فإذا لم يفعل كان مخالفاً، وأما المعاملات فالنية ليست
So if he adds [the contract] to a specific price, the purchase is for the owner of that price, because the apparent is that he adds the purchase to the property of the one for whom he buys. And this is because the price, though not determined [in itself], has a semblance of determination in terms of the soundness of the sale with it and the determination of its amount and description. Hence, profit is not lawful for him if he buys with usurped dirhams, and his religion prevents him from buying for himself by adding the contract to another's property, because that is disapproved in law and custom, so he would not do it. And custom has it that when he buys for himself, he adds the contract to his own property, and that is not disapproved in law or custom. So the purchase belongs to the owner of that price, even if he intended otherwise, following the dictates of custom and law. Then, if he pays from the principal's property for what he bought for himself, he must guarantee. And if he adds it to an absolute price, it is either cash or credit. If cash, then either they both agree on the existence of intention for one of them, or on its absence, or they differ. If the price is cash and they agree on the existence of intention for one, it is for the one intended, because what is established by their agreement is like established by sight, and their agreement is a proof against them. Payment is not looked at, and no consideration to it in this case, because he can buy for himself and for the principal. If he specifies by intention, it is specified. And he is a usurper if he pays from the principal's property when he intended it for himself. And if they differ on intention, payment decides by consensus, because its indication for specification is like his adding the purchase to it, because the apparent is that he does what is permissible lawfully or customary. And if they agree that intention was not present, then according to Muhammad [al-Shaybani], God knows? it is for the contracting party, because man's absolute acts are for himself, so it becomes like the one commanded to perform Hajj who is absolute and does not intend it for the one for whom he is performing. According to Abu Yusuf, payment decides, because the absolute is ambiguous, so it remains suspended. Whichever property he pays from, he specifies the ambiguous by it, so it becomes like the case of mutual denial. Unlike the one commanded for Hajj, for Hajj is an act of worship which is only performed with intention, so he was commanded to intend the Hajj for the one for whom he acts; if he does not, he is contrary. As for transactions, intention is not...
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute and expansion of a ruling. The commentator explains the detailed rules when an agent purchases with a specified price or an absolute price. The core principle: when the agent adds the contract to a specific price (e.g., 'I buy this with this thousand dirhams belonging to Zaid'), the purchase is for the owner of that price. This is based on custom and the presumption that one does not add another's property to his own contract dishonestly. Even if he intended otherwise, the custom and law override his hidden intention, and he must guarantee if he uses the principal's money for his own purchase. When the price is absolute (not tied to a specific fund), further rules apply based on whether the price is cash or credit, and whether the parties (principal and agent) agree or differ on intention. A key ikhtilaf (disagreement) between Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf arises when both agree that no intention was present: Muhammad says the purchase is for the agent (the contracting party) because absolute acts are for oneself, analogizing to Hajj where intention is required; Abu Yusuf says payment decides because the absolute contract is ambiguous and payment clarifies it. The commentator notes that the analogy to Hajj fails because Hajj is worship requiring intention, while transactions do not necessarily require intention for validity. The passage cuts off at the end, but the point is that in transactions, intention is not essential for the determination of ownership in the absence of a specified price.

Key Terms
ثمن معين (thaman mu'ayyan): A specific price, e.g., 'these 1000 dirhams' belonging to a particular person.
ثمن مطلق (thaman mutlaq): An absolute price, not tied to a specific fund, e.g., 'for 100 dirhams' without specifying whose money.
نقد (naqd): Payment in cash; the actual money handed over.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The principal, because the agent acted for him.
B) The agent, because he made the contract.
C) It depends on whose money was used for payment.
D) The goods are invalid because intention is necessary.

Key Takeaway
In agency purchases with an absolute price and no intention, the Hanafi school offers two valid positions: the agent defaults to ownership (Muhammad) or payment decides (Abu Yusuf), with later preference often given to Abu Yusuf for transactions.

The Agent as Contracting Party: Possession Requirement in Sarf and Salam
Ruling with proof involving agent's separation in sarf/salam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the contracting party (al-'aqid) in sarf and salam contracts and apply the rule that the agent's separation before possession invalidates the contract.

Core Idea
Only the agent's separation voids sarf/salam; the principal's separation does not — the agent is the contracting party.

In this passage, the author clarifies a crucial distinction in Hanafi sales law: in contracts of currency exchange (sarf) and forward sale (salam), possession (qabd) must be taken by the actual contracting party. The agent (wakil) acting on behalf of the principal is the 'aqid, while the principal is not directly involved in the contract. Thus, if the agent separates from the session before taking possession, the contract becomes void. However, if the principal separates, the contract remains valid because the principal is not the one contracting. This ruling also distinguishes between an agent and a mere messenger (rasul).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two contracts and the condition
The passage specifically addresses sarf (currency exchange) and salam (forward sale). The condition for validity is possession (qabd) before separation from the session.
2
Determine who is the contracting party (al-'aqid)
In these contracts, the agent (wakil) is considered the 'aqid, not the principal. The principal is not a party to the contract in terms of possession.
The text states: 'العاقد هو الوكيل' – the contracting party is the agent.
3
Apply the condition to the agent's separation
If the agent separates before taking possession, the required condition is not met, so the contract becomes void (batil).
This is because possession by the 'aqid is a condition.
4
Apply the condition to the principal's separation
If the principal separates before the agent takes possession, the contract remains valid because the principal is not the contracting party.
The principal's separation does not affect the condition.
5
Contrast with the messenger (rasul)
A messenger is not a contracting party; the sender is the 'aqid. Therefore, the messenger's possession is not valid for the contract.
The messenger's role ends with communication; possession must be by the 'aqid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: ويبطل...الخ؛ يعني يعتبرُ في السَّلَم والصرف مفارقة الوكيل لا الموكل، فيبطل عقدهما بمفارقة الوكيل صاحبه قبل القبض؛ لوجود الافتراق من غير قبض، ولا يبطل بمفارقة الموكل، إذ القبض للعاقد، وهو ليس بعاقد، وما قيل: من أنه إذا حضر الموكل مجلس العقدِ لا يعتبر مفارقة الوكيل ضعيف؛ لكون الوكيل أصلاً في الحقوق في البيع مطلقاً. كما في «البحر».والتفصيل: إنّ المستحق في الصرف والسلم قبضُ العاقد، والعاقد هو الوكيل، فيشترط قبضه، وإن كان لا يتعلق به الحقوق؛ كالصبي والعبد المحجور عليه؛ لأنّ قبضه وتسليمه صحيح، وإن لم يتوجه عليه المطالبة، ففي حكم صحة التقابض هو كوكيل تعلق بـه حقـوق العقد، فإذا قبض الوكيل ثم العقد؛ لوجود شرطه، وإن فارقه قبل القبض بطل شرطه، وإن فارقه الموكلُ قبل القبض لا يبطل؛ لأنه ليس بعاقد. بخلاف الرسول فيهما؛ لأنَّ الرسالة حصلت في العقد لا في القبض، وكــلام الرسول ينتقل إلى المرسل فيكون العاقد هو المرسل، فيكون قبض الرسول قبض غير العاقد فلا يجوز.وقال في «النهاية»: هذا إذا كان الموكَّلُ غائباً من مجلس العقد، وأما إذا كان حاضراً في مجلس العقدِ يصير كأنَّ الموكل صارف بنفسه، فلا تعتبر مفارقة الوكيل، وعزاه إلى خُوَاهَرْ زَادَه، وهذا مشكل، فإنَّ الوكيل أصيل في «باب البيع» حضر الموكل في مجلس العقد أو لم يحضر. كذا في «التبيين».
His statement: 'And sarf and salam are invalidated...' means that in salam and sarf, the separation of the agent is considered, not the principal. So their contract is invalidated by the agent's separation from his companion before possession, due to the existence of separation without possession, and it is not invalidated by the principal's separation, because possession is for the contracting party, and he (the principal) is not a contracting party. And what is said, that if the principal is present in the session of contract, the agent's separation is not considered, is weak; because the agent is the primary party in rights in sale absolutely, as in 'Al-Bahr'. The detail: the one entitled to possession in sarf and salam is the contracting party, and the contracting party is the agent, so his possession is required. Even if he (the agent) is not subject to rights, like a minor or a restricted slave, because his possession and delivery are valid, even if no claim is directed against him. Thus, in the ruling of the validity of mutual possession, he is like an agent to whom the rights of the contract are attached. So if the agent takes possession, then the contract is valid because its condition is fulfilled, and if he separates before possession, its condition is invalidated. But if the principal separates before possession, it is not invalidated because he is not a contracting party. Unlike the messenger in these two contracts, because the message is completed in the contract, not in possession, and the speech of the messenger transfers to the sender, so the contracting party is the sender, so the possession of the messenger is possession of a non-contracting party, so it is not permissible. And it is stated in 'Al-Nihayah': This is when the principal is absent from the session of contract. But if he is present in the session of contract, then it becomes as if the principal himself is the exchanger, so the agent's separation is not considered. And he attributed it to Khwahar Zadah. And this is problematic, because the agent is the primary party in the chapter of sale whether the principal is present in the session of contract or not. This is as in 'Al-Tabyeen'.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage addresses a key ruling in Hanafi fiqh regarding the contracts of sarf (currency exchange) and salam (forward sale). The matn states that these contracts are invalidated by the separation of the agent (wakil) from the session before possession, but not by the separation of the principal (muwakkil). The commentary clarifies that possession (qabd) must be taken by the contracting party, who is the agent. The principal, even if present, is not the contracting party in terms of possession. Thus, if the agent separates before taking possession, the contract becomes invalid because the required condition of possession before separation is not met. However, if the principal separates, it does not affect the contract. The commentary draws a contrast with a messenger (rasul), who is merely a conduit for the contract; the contracting party is the sender (al-mursil), so the messenger's possession is not valid for the contract. Finally, a minority view from Khwahar Zadah suggests that if the principal is present in the session, the agent's separation is not considered; but this is rejected because the agent remains the primary party in all sales, regardless of the principal's presence.

Key Terms
صرف (sarf): Contract of exchanging currencies or gold/silver for the same species or different species, with immediate possession required.
سلم (salam): Forward sale where the price is paid in advance for future delivery of a specified commodity.
وكيل (wakil): Agent who acts on behalf of the principal (muwakkil) in a contract.
رسول (rasul): Messenger who conveys an offer or acceptance but does not act as the contracting party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the separation of the agent before possession invalidate a sarf contract, while the principal's separation does not?
A) Because the agent is the one who takes possession as the contracting party
B) Because the principal is not present at the session
C) Because sarf requires physical exchange, which only the agent can do
D) Because the principal is responsible for possession under the contract

Key Takeaway
In sarf and salam, possession must be taken by the agent (the contracting party) before separation; the principal's separation does not affect validity.

Agency to Buy Meat: Agent Buys More Than Ordered
Intra-Hanafi dispute on agent's extra purchase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on an agent's purchase of extra meat for the same price and identify the practical consequence of the intra-madhhab dispute.

Core Idea
An agent's deviation from a specified quantity does not bind the principal if the extra commodity is perishable.

When an agent is authorized to buy a specific quantity of a commodity but buys more for the same price, does the principal benefit or suffer? This lesson explores an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn over whether the principal is bound by the extra purchase of meat. The key distinction hinges on the perishability of the commodity and the nature of the agency contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Read the scenario: agent told to buy one mann of meat for one dirham, buys two mann for one dirham from meat that sells at one mann per dirham. What is Abu Hanifa's ruling? What is the Sahibayn's ruling?
Abu Hanifa: principal owes half dirham for one mann. Sahibayn: principal owes one dirham for two mann.
2
Understand the Sahibayn's analogy
The Sahibayn analogize to an agent authorized to sell a slave for 1000 selling for 2000 (extra belongs to principal). They argue the agency covers the contract, not just delivery, and the extra is a benefit.
Asl: slave sale; far: meat purchase; illa: benefit to principal.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's response
Abu Hanifa distinguishes: the extra price (thaman) does not spoil, but extra meat (muthman) can spoil. Also, the agent deviated from a specified quantity, exceeding authority.
The analogy is invalid due to the perishability of meat.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: Under Abu Hanifa, principal gets one mann at half price; agent keeps extra mann but must pay the remaining half dirham. Under Sahibayn, principal gets both mann for one dirham.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.
