And whoever is authorized to buy one mann of meat for a dirham, then he buys two mann for a dirham from meat that sells for one mann per dirham, his principal is bound by one mann at half a dirham; this is according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he is bound by two mann for a dirham because the agency is in the contract, not in the delivery, and his speech transfers to the principal, so the agent's delivery becomes delivery of a non-contracting party and is invalid. As in Majma' al-Anhur. His statement: And whoever appoints... is a summary of the ruling. The statement: And whoever appoints... means whoever appoints someone to buy one mann of meat for one dirham, then the agent buys two mann of meat for a dirham from meat that sells for one mann per dirham, the principal is bound by one mann at half a dirham. [3] His statement: According to the two, he is bound...; all three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) said that, and in some copies of Mukhtasar al-Quduri it is recorded that Muhammad's statement aligns with Abu Hanifa's, and Muhammad did not mention the dispute in al-Mabsut at the end of the chapter of agency for sale and purchase, where he said: the principal is bound by ten (units) at half a dirham, and the rest belongs to the agent. [2] His statement: And for him (Abu Hanifa) he has: The principal ordered the agent to buy one mann, which is a specified quantity, and did not order him to buy more than that, so the purchase of the extra is executed on the agent's account due to deviation; and the purchase of one mann is on the principal because he performed what was ordered. Unlike what he cited as evidence, he said: the extra there is a substitute for his property, so it belongs to him. At that point, it is objected to him: The dirham is the property of the principal, so the extra is his property, so there is no difference between them. And it was answered: The extra there is a substitute for the price, not the priced, so the difference is apparent. The conclusion is that this is an analogy of the sold commodity to the price, and it is invalid due to a difference. The basis is that the extra 1000 does not spoil with long storage, unlike meat, and it can be spent on another immediate need, but that may be difficult for meat and it spoils. Thus they said.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute in the chapter of agency regarding an agent authorized to buy one mann of meat for one dirham. The agent instead buys two mann for one dirham, from meat that sells for one dirham per mann. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the principal is bound by only one mann at half a dirham, because the agent exceeded the specific quantity ordered. The extra mann is on the agent's account. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), the entire purchase (two mann for one dirham) binds the principal, because the agency is effective in the contract itself, not merely in the delivery. They analogize this to the case where an agent is told to sell a slave for 1000 and sells for 2000; the extra is for the principal. The commentary explains Abu Hanifa's reasoning: the principal's order was to buy a specified quantity (one mann), not to buy as much as possible. The extra is a deviation, so it is not binding on the principal. The Sahibayn argue that the principal's objective was to spend the dirham to obtain meat, and the agent's act of getting more meat is a benefit, so it binds the principal. The text then presents a rebuttal: the analogy to the sale case is flawed because extra price does not spoil over time, whereas extra meat can spoil. Therefore, Abu Hanifa's view is preferred. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is recorded as Abu Hanifa's view, as noted from al-Mabsut and al-‘Inayah.

Key Terms
رسالة (risalah): the agency relationship and the act of contracting; the authority delegated to the agent
مَنْ (mann): a unit of weight for meat (approximately two pounds)
ثمن (thaman): price; the money paid in a sale

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if an agent is told to buy one mann of meat for one dirham, but buys two mann for one dirham from meat that sells at one mann per dirham, what does the principal owe?
A) The principal owes one dirham for two mann.
B) The principal owes half a dirham for one mann.
C) The principal owes nothing; the agent keeps all the meat.
D) The principal owes one dirham for one mann.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an agent who buys more than the specified quantity of a perishable commodity does not bind the principal for the excess; only the ordered quantity at a proportionate price is binding.

Agent's Beneficial Violation: The Extra Meat Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on agent buying more meat than ordered

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Hanifa's ruling on extra meat bought by an agent and explain why he distinguishes it from the sale case.

Core Idea
A beneficial violation is not always valid for the principal—perishable goods change the ruling.

An agent instructed to buy one mann of meat for one dirham instead buys two mann for the same price. Is the extra meat for the principal or the agent? This lesson unpacks the intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad, focusing on the role of analogy and the nature of the subject matter.

Guided Path
1
State the facts and the dispute
Identify the principal's order (buy one mann for one dirham) and the agent's action (bought two mann for same price). Determine the hukm for the ordered quantity and the extra.
The ordered quantity is valid for the principal by consensus.
2
Examine Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa rules that the extra belongs to the agent because the agent violated the mandate. The principal only owns what he ordered.
The extra is a separate purchase by the agent on his own account.
3
Analyze Muhammad's opposing view
Muhammad holds that the entire purchase is for the principal because the agent acted beneficially, analogizing to the case of selling an item for more than the authorized price.
His dalil is qiyas: extra meat is like extra sale price, both benefits to principal.
4
Assess the Hanafi response and distinction
The commentary refutes the analogy: in the sale, the extra price is a substitute (badal) for the principal's item; here the extra meat is not a substitute. Also, meat is perishable, unlike money.
The principle: a violation that introduces risk (like spoilage) is not automatically for the principal.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The commentary favors Abu Hanifa's view, making it the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.
The tarjih authority is the author of 'Umda al-Ri'aya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[1] قوله : لأنَّ الموكل .. الخ ؛ تقريره : إنَّ الموكل أمر الوكيل في هذه الصورة بصرف الدرهم في اللحم، وظنَّ الموكَّلُ أن سعر اللحم مَنْ ، فإذا اشترى الوكيل بدرهم منوين فقد زاده خيراً، كما إذا وكله ببيع عبدِهِ بألف فباعه بألفين، فالحاصل أنَّ هذا خلاف إلى خير، فإنّ الأمر متناول، كما يساوي من منه درهماً بدرهم. Because the principal ordered him to spend the dirham on meat, so he spent and increased [it] with good, and for him (Abu Hanifa) is that he ordered him to buy a mann, not to buy an increase. And he only said: from what is sold for a dirham; so if he bought meat that is not sold for a dirham but for less, the purchase would be for the agent because the order ordered him to buy meat that equals a mann from it for a dirham, not for less. Al-Quduri: The statement of Muhammad is against the statement of Abu Hanifa, and Muhammad did not mention the disagreement in al-Mabsut at the end of the chapter of agency in sale and purchase, where he said: The principal is obligated for ten [units] for half a dirham, and the rest is for the agent. Likewise in al-Inayah. [1] His saying: 'Because the principal...' etc. Its explanation: The principal ordered the agent in this scenario to spend the dirham on meat, and the principal assumed that the price of meat was a mann, so when the agent bought two mann for a dirham, he increased it with good, just as if he authorized him to sell his slave for one thousand and he sold it for two thousand. So the result is that this is a violation that leads to good, so the command includes [the permissible] just as [in the case where] he sells [the slave] for a dirham. [2] His saying: 'And for him...' etc. Means for Abu Hanifa: The principal ordered the agent to buy a mann, which is a specified amount, and did not order him to buy more than it, so the purchase of the increase is executed for the agent due to violation, and the purchase of a mann is for the principal because he carried out the command. Unlike the example he cited, he said: The increase therein is a substitute for his ownership, so it is for him. Then it is objected: The dirham is the property of the principal, so the increase would be his property, so there is no difference between them. And it was answered: The increase there is a substitute from it, not a substitute for it, so the difference is apparent. And the conclusion: That is an analogy of the sold item to the price, and it is invalid because of a difference. And the basis of that: The extra thousand does not spoil with prolonged stay, unlike meat, and it is permissible to spend it on another immediate need, while that may be impossible for meat and it becomes spoiled. Thus they said.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding an agent who, instructed to buy a specific quantity of meat (one mann) for one dirham, instead buys two mann for the same price. The question is: does the extra quantity belong to the principal or to the agent? The passage begins by stating that according to Abu Hanifa, the purchase of the extra is for the agent (the purchase of the ordered mann is for the principal) because the agent violated the mandate by buying more than ordered. The agent is considered to have acted beyond his authority regarding the increase. The commentary explains that Muhammad holds that the principal benefits from the additional meat (a violation to good), and thus the purchase is valid for the principal, analogizing it to the case of an agent selling a slave for more than the authorized price. However, Abu Hanifa distinguishes that case: in the sale case, the extra is a substitute for the price, but here the extra is not a substitute; the principal only owns the ordered meat. The commentary then rejects the analogy of the meat case to the slave sale case, noting that money (the extra thousand) is not perishable, whereas meat is perishable and cannot be easily redirected to other needs. The overall tone supports Abu Hanifa's view that the agent takes the risk of the extra meat.

Key Terms
مَنْ (mann): A unit of weight, here used to specify the quantity of meat to be purchased.
وكالة (wakalah): Agency; the contract by which one person appoints another to act on his behalf.
خلاف إلى خير (khilaf ila khayr): A violation of the mandate that results in a benefit for the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It belongs to the principal because the agent acted beneficially.
B) It belongs to the agent because he exceeded the mandate.
C) It is invalid and must be returned.
D) It is divided equally between principal and agent.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a beneficial violation does not automatically transfer ownership to the principal; the nature of the subject matter and the concept of substitution determine the ruling.

Agent's Purchase of One Slave for More Than Half: The Hanafi Exception of Istihsan
Expansion of a ruling from Al-Hidayah on agency purchase with price specification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an agent buying one of two designated slaves for more than half the total price, and explain the istihsan exception that binds the principal.

Core Idea
An agent's overpayment for one slave binds the principal if the other is bought before dispute – the explicit purpose overrides the implied division.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya discusses a critical dispute in agency law: when a principal instructs an agent to buy two specific slaves for a fixed total price, and the agent buys one for more than half. The default ruling is that the principal is not bound, but Abu Hanifa introduces a significant exception through istihsan: if the agent buys the second slave with the remaining price before any dispute arises, the purchase binds the principal. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and contrasts it with the strict qiyas of the Shafi'i and Maliki schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
First, distinguish between cases where the principal mentions a total price and where he does not. This determines the agent's authority.
2
Analyze the default ruling for the price-specified case
When a total price is specified and the slaves are of equal value, buying one for more than half is a violation and does not bind the principal unless the second is bought before dispute.
3
Examine the istihsan exception
The exception holds that if the agent completes the purchase of both slaves for the total price before the principal objects, the principal is bound because the expressed purpose is achieved.
Key principle: the explicit statement (sarih) prevails over implication (dalalah).
4
Contrast with the opposing school's qiyas
Malik and al-Shafi'i follow strict analogy: the violation is established, so the purchase cannot bind the principal unless ratified.
5
Apply the Hanafi tarjih
The text confirms that Abu Hanifa's istihsan is the relied-upon position (madhhab al-Imam), giving it operative force.
This is an example of istihsan overriding qiyas in Hanafi usul.

فإن أمره بشراء عبدينِ عُيْنَيْنِ بلا ذِكْرِ ثَمَن ، فشرى أحدهما، أو بشرائهما بألف وقيمتهما سواء، فشرى أحدهما بنصفه، أو بأقل صح وبالأكثر لا ، إلا إذا شرى الآخر بباقي الثَّمن قبل الخصومة) : أي إذا أُمِرَ بشراء عبدين [1] قوله : أي إذا أمر بشراء ... الخ ؛ تقرير المسألة : إِنَّ الموكَّلَ إِذا وَكُل الوكيل بشراء عبدين معينين فلا يخلو : إما أن يذكر الثمن أو لا. فإن لم يذكر ثمنها فشرى المأمور للأمر أحد العبدين فصح هذا الشراء، ويقع عن الأمر، سواء اشتراه بقيمته أو بنقصان ؛ لأنَّ التوكيل مطلق غير مقيد بثمن مقدّر، وقد لا يتفق الجمع بينهما، فيجوز أن يشتري كلُّ واحدٍ منهما بقدر قيمته من الثمن أو أقل أو بزيادة يتغابن الناس في مثله، وليس له أن يشتري بما لا يتغابنُ الناس بمثله، وهو الغبن الفاحش ؛ لأنَّ التوكيل بالشراء بالمتعارف، والمتعارف فيما لا يتغابن الناس فيه ؛ فلهذا لا يجوز للوكيل أن يشتري بغبن فاحش بالإجماع. ولو ذكر ثمنهما بأن قال : اشتر لي عبدين بألف فقيمتهما سواء، فشرى المأمور للأمر أحدهما بنصف ألف أو بأقل من نصف الألف جاز الشراء، ووقع عن الآمر ؛ لأنه قابل الألف بهذين العبدين، فقيمتهما سواء، فينقسم بينهما نصفين دلالة، أمراً بشراء كلّ واحدٍ بخمسمئة ضرورة، فالشراء بخمسمئة موافقة، وبأقل منها مخالفة إلى خير، فيقع عن الآمر. وإن اشترى بأكثر من نصف الألف لا يقع عن الآمر، ولا يلزمه، بل يقع عن الوكيل قلت الزيادة أو كثرت ؛ لأنه مخالفة إلى شرّ إلا إذا اشترى العبد الآخر بباقي الثمن قبل الخصومة ؛ أي قبل أن يختصمَ الأمرُ المأمور ، وهذا استحسان، والقياس أن لا يلزم الآمر ؛ لثبوت المخالفة، وبه أخذ مالك والشافعي . فيكون ووجه الاستحسان : إنَّ شراء العبدِ الأوّل قائم ، وغرضه المصرح به وهو تحصيل العبدين بألف قد حصل ، وما ثبت الإنقسام إلا دلالة، والصريح فوق الدلالة إذا أمكن العمل به، فلا تعتبر الدلالة مع الصريح عند تعارضهما، وهذا مذهب الإمام.
If he ordered him to buy two specific slaves without mentioning a price, and he bought one of them, or [if he ordered] to buy them for a thousand and their value is equal, and he bought one of them for half of it or less, it is valid; with more, no, unless he buys the other with the remainder of the price before litigation. That is, when he is ordered to buy two slaves. [1] His saying: that is, when he is ordered to buy... etc. Explanation of the issue: When the principal authorizes the agent to buy two specific slaves, it is not free from [two cases]: either he mentions the price or not. If he did not mention their price, and the agent bought one of them, this purchase is valid and falls on the principal, whether he bought it at its value or at a discount, because the agency is absolute, not restricted to a specific price, and it may not be possible to combine both, so it is permissible for him to buy each of them for its value from the price or less or with an increase that people overlook (ghaban yasir), and he may not buy with what people do not overlook, which is gross fraud (ghaban fahish), because agency in purchase is according to custom, and custom is for what people do not overlook; therefore it is not permissible for the agent to buy with gross fraud by consensus. If he mentioned their price, saying: 'Buy me two slaves for a thousand' and their value is equal, and the agent bought one of them for five hundred or less than half of the thousand, the purchase is valid and falls on the principal, because he matched the thousand with these two slaves, their value equal, so the thousand divides between them in half by implication, necessarily ordering the purchase of each for five hundred. So purchase for five hundred is compliance, and for less than that is a violation to good, so it falls on the principal. If he bought for more than half of the thousand, it does not fall on the principal and does not bind him; rather it falls on the agent, whether the increase is little or much, because it is a violation to evil, except if he buys the other slave with the remainder of the price before litigation, i.e., before the principal disputes with the agent. This is istihsan; the qiyas is that it does not bind the principal because of the established violation, and that is what Malik and al-Shafi'i adopted. And the basis of istihsan: The purchase of the first slave stands, and his expressed purpose, which is to acquire the two slaves for a thousand, has been achieved. The division [of the price] is established only by implication, and the explicit [statement] is above the implication when it is possible to act upon it, so implication is not considered with the explicit when they conflict. And this is the madhhab of the Imam (Abu Hanifa).
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage from the sharh explains the ruling when an agent is appointed to buy two specific slaves. Two scenarios are presented: (1) No price is mentioned by the principal. In this case, the agent may buy one of the slaves at market value or even less, and the purchase is valid for the principal. However, the agent must avoid gross fraud (ghaban fahish), as that would be against customary practice. (2) The principal specifies a total price for both slaves (e.g., 1000) and the slaves are of equal value. Here, if the agent buys one slave for half the price (500) or less, it is valid for the principal, as the price is implicitly divided. But if he buys it for more than half, the purchase is not binding on the principal; it falls on the agent, unless the agent buys the second slave with the remainder of the price before any dispute arises. This exception is based on istihsan (juridical preference). The qiyas (analogy) would say it is not binding on the principal because of the violation, which is the view of Imam Malik and Imam al-Shafi'i. The reasoning for istihsan is that the expressed purpose of buying both slaves for the total price is achieved, and the division of the price is only by implication. Since the explicit statement (buying both for a thousand) can be acted upon, the implication is set aside. This is the madhhab of Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
غبن فاحش (ghaban fahish): Gross fraud or excessive overpayment that is not tolerated by custom.
استحسان (istihsan): Juridical preference; departing from strict qiyas for a stronger reason (like custom or necessity).
دلالة (dalalah): Implied meaning or indication, as opposed to explicit statement (sarih).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is required for the purchase of one slave for more than half the stated total price to bind the principal?
A) The agent must buy the slave for exactly half.
B) The agent must buy the second slave with the remainder of the price before any dispute.
C) The principal must explicitly approve the purchase after the fact.
D) The agent must return the extra amount to the principal.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses istihsan to prioritize the principal's expressed purpose over implied price division, binding the principal if the agent completes the purchase before dispute.

Agent Buying One of Two Specified Slaves: Rulings and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Expansive commentary on agency purchase of specified slaves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for an agent buying one of two specified slaves when the price is specified, and explain the difference between Imam Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions.

Core Idea
Buying one slave at more than half the total price is invalid unless the second is bought before dispute—unless the excess is customary.

This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a common scenario in agency: a principal instructs his agent to purchase two specific slaves for a lump sum. The ruling hinges on whether the agent's action complies with the principal's explicit goal or deviates from it. We will examine the default ruling, the underlying reasoning of istihsan, and the intra-Hanafi difference with the Sahibayn.

Guided Path
1
Classify the agency scenario
Determine whether the principal specified a total price and whether the slaves are of equal value. If no price is mentioned, the agent may buy one slave and it falls on the principal because the agency is absolute.
The rule changes when a price is specified.
2
Identify the implied per-slave price
When the total price is given and the values are equal, each slave is implied to cost half the total (implication/dalala). This sets the benchmark for compliance.
This implication is not explicit but necessary by context.
3
Evaluate the agent's action as compliance or deviation
Compare the actual price paid for the first slave to the implied half. Equal or less is compliance or good deviation (valid for principal). More than half is harmful deviation.
Amount of increase does not matter for Imam Abu Hanifa; any excess is harmful.
4
Apply the ruling for harmful deviation
If the agent buys one slave for more than half, the purchase does not bind the principal unless the agent buys the second slave with the remainder before any dispute. This is based on istihsan: the principal's explicit purpose (both slaves for total) is achieved.
Qiyas would not bind the principal; this is the view of Malik and Shafi'i.
5
Compare Imam Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that if the excess is within customary tolerance (ghabn yasir) and enough remains to buy the second, the purchase binds the principal. Their reasoning gives weight to custom ('urf) in absolute agency.
The relied-upon fatwa follows Imam Abu Hanifa, but the Sahibayn's view is also applied in practice.

Source Text

فإن أمره بشراء عبدينِ عُيْنَيْنِ بلا ذِكْرِ ثَمَن ، فشرى أحدهما، أو بشرائهما بألف وقيمتهما سواء، فشرى أحدهما بنصفه، أو بأقل صح وبالأكثر لا ، إلا إذا شرى الآخر بباقي الثَّمن قبل الخصومة (فإن أمره بشراء عبدينِ عُيْنَيْنِ بلا ذِكْرِ ثَمَن ، فشرى أحدهما، أو بشرائهما بألف وقيمتهما سواء، فشرى أحدهما بنصفه، أو بأقل صح وبالأكثر لا ، إلا إذا شرى الآخر بباقي الثَّمن قبل الخصومة) : أي إذا أُمِرَ بشراء عبدين فإذا ذكر ثمنهما بأن قال : اشتر لي عبدين بألف فقيمتهما سواء، فشرى المأمور للأمر أحدهما بنصف ألف أو بأقل من نصف الألف جاز الشراء، ووقع عن الآمر؛ لأنه قابل الألف بهذين العبدين، فقيمتهما سواء، فينقسم بينهما نصفين دلالة، أمراً بشراء كلّ واحدٍ بخمسمئة ضرورة، فالشراء بخمسمئة موافقة، وبأقل منها مخالفة إلى خير، فيقع عن الآمر. وإن اشترى بأكثر من نصف الألف لا يقع عن الآمر، ولا يلزمه، بل يقع عن الوكيل قلت الزيادة أو كثرت؛ لأنه مخالفة إلى شرّ إلا إذا اشترى العبد الآخر بباقي الثمن قبل الخصومة؛ أي قبل أن يختصمَ الأمرُ المأمور، وهذا استحسان، والقياس أن لا يلزم الآمر؛ لثبوت المخالفة، وبه أخذ مالك والشافعي . ووجه الاستحسان: إنَّ شراء العبدِ الأوّل قائم، وغرضه المصرح به وهو تحصيل العبدين بألف قد حصل، وما ثبت الإنقسام إلا دلالة، والصريح فوق الدلالة إذا أمكن العمل به، فلا تعتبر الدلالة مع الصريح عند تعارضهما، وهذا مذهب الإمام. فإن قال : اشتريته بألف وقال آمره بنصفه، فإن كان ألف الأمر صُدق معينين فإن لم يذكرِ الشَّمنَ فشرى أحدهما يَقَعُ عن الآمر؛ لأنَّ التَّوكيل مطلق، وقد لا يتَّفِقُ الجمع بينهما، وإن سمَّى ثمنهما بأن قال : اشتر لي عبدين بألف، وقيمتهما سواء، فشرى أحدهما بالنصف أو بأقل صح عن الآمر، وإن اشترى بأكثر من النصف لا يقع عن الآمر، بل يقع عن الوكيل، إلا إذا اشترى الآخر باقي الثَّمن قبل الخصومة؛ لأنَّ المقصود حصول العبدين بألف، وعندهما : إن اشترى أحدهما بأكثر من النصف مما يتغابنُ النَّاسُ فيه، وقد بقي من الثَّمن ما يشتري به الباقي يصح عن الأمر. فإن قال : اشتريته بألف وقال آمره بنصفه، فإن كان ألف الأمر صُدق وعندهما : إنَّ الوكيل إن اشترى أحد العبدين بأكثر من نصف الألف مما يتغابنُ النَّاسُ فيه، وقد بقي من الألف ما يشتري به العبد الباقي فجاز الشراء، ووقع عن الآمر؛ لأنَّ التوكيل حصل مطلقاً، لا تقدير ثمن كل واحد منهما بخمسمئة، والمطلقُ يحمل على المتعارف وهو الشراء بالقيمة أو بزيادة يسيرة قدر ما يتغابنُ النَّاسُ فيه، ولكن غرضُهُ تحصيل العبدين بالألف، فلا بُدَّ من أن يبقى من الألف قدر ما يشتري بمثله الباقي؛ ليحصل غرضه.
If he orders him to buy two specific slaves without mentioning a price, and he buys one of them, or (if he orders) to buy them for one thousand and their values are equal, and he buys one of them for half or less, it is valid; for more it is not, unless he buys the other with the remainder of the price before the dispute. (The commentary:) That is, if he is ordered to buy two slaves. If he mentions their price, saying: Buy for me two slaves for one thousand, and their values are equal, and the one ordered buys one of them for half a thousand or less than half a thousand, the purchase is permissible and falls upon the orderer; because he matched the thousand to these two slaves, so their values are equal, thus it splits between them by implication, commanding the purchase of each for five hundred by necessity. So buying for five hundred is compliance, and for less than it is a deviation to good, so it falls upon the orderer. If he buys for more than half a thousand, it does not fall upon the orderer, nor is it binding on him; rather it falls upon the agent, whether the increase is small or large; because it is a deviation to evil, unless he buys the other slave with the remainder of the price before the dispute; i.e. before the orderer disputes with the one ordered. This is istihsan; by qiyas it would not bind the orderer due to the proven deviation, and Malik and al-Shafi‘i took that. The face of istihsan: The purchase of the first slave is standing, and his explicit purpose—namely obtaining the two slaves for one thousand—has been achieved, and the splitting was only established by implication, and the explicit overrides the implication when it is possible to act upon it, so the implication is not considered with the explicit when they conflict. This is the madhhab of the Imam. If he says: I bought it for one thousand, and his principal says: for half of it, then if it is the thousand of the principal, he is believed. For specified (slaves) if he did not mention the price, and he buys one of them, it falls upon the principal; because the agency is absolute, and combining them may not be possible. If he specified their price, saying: Buy for me two slaves for one thousand, and their values are equal, and he buys one for half or less, it is valid for the principal; if he buys for more than half, it does not fall upon the principal, but upon the agent, unless he buys the other with the remainder of the price before the dispute; because the goal is obtaining the two slaves for one thousand. And according to them (the two disciples): if he buys one of them for more than half, of what people normally tolerate, and there remains enough of the price to buy the remaining one, it is valid for the principal. If he says: I bought it for one thousand, and his principal says: for half of it, then if it is the thousand of the principal, he is believed. And according to them: if the agent buys one of the two slaves for more than half of the thousand, of what people normally tolerate, and there remains enough from the thousand to buy the remaining slave, then the purchase is permissible and falls upon the principal; because the agency was absolute, with no estimation of the price of each as five hundred, and the absolute is interpreted according to custom—namely purchase at the value or with a small increase that people normally tolerate—but his goal is obtaining the two slaves for one thousand, so it is necessary that enough remains from the thousand to buy the other with its like, so that his goal is achieved.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a detailed legal analysis (expansive commentary) of a ruling concerning an agent appointed to purchase two specified slaves. It belongs to the chapter of agency (wakala) in Hanafi fiqh, specifically under the sub-chapter of agency for sale and purchase. The text presents the matn (core text) and then the sharh (commentary) explaining the ruling and the reasoning behind it.

The passage begins with the matn: If a principal orders his agent to buy two specific slaves without mentioning a price, and the agent buys only one of them, the purchase is valid and binding on the principal. However, if the principal specifies a total price (e.g., one thousand) and the slaves are of equal value, then buying one for exactly half or less is valid; buying for more than half is not valid for the principal unless he also buys the second slave with the remaining amount before any dispute arises.

The commentary then expands: When the price is specified as a lump sum for both slaves of equal value, the implication is that each slave costs half the total. Therefore, buying one for exactly half is considered compliance, and buying for less is a deviation that benefits the principal, so it is still valid. Buying for more is a harmful deviation, so it binds only the agent—unless he completes the purchase of the second slave with the remainder, fulfilling the principal's goal of acquiring both slaves for the specified total. The commentary notes that this ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference). The qiyas (analogy) would hold that the agent's deviation invalidates the purchase for the principal, and this qiyas is the position of Imam Malik and Imam al-Shafi'i.

The commentary then presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ from Imam Abu Hanifa. According to them, if the agent buys one slave for more than half but within the range of what people customarily tolerate (ghabn yasir), and there remains enough of the price to buy the other, then the purchase is valid for the principal. Their reasoning is that the agency was absolute (no specific price per slave was set), and custom allows a slight increase beyond the exact half. Since the principal's goal (both slaves for the total sum) is achieved, the purchase should be binding.

The passage also discusses a scenario where the agent claims he bought for one thousand but the principal says he authorized only half of that. The text rules that the principal's word is accepted regarding the amount authorized, because the principal is the owner of the money and his claim is more credible in the absence of clear evidence.

Overall, this passage illustrates the Hanafi method of reasoning, combining qiyas and istihsan, and shows how the madhhab's positions can differ among its founders. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in later Hanafi works tends to follow Imam Abu Hanifa's view, but the Sahibayn's opinion is also considered and applied in specific circumstances.

Key Terms
عبدين معينين (abdain mu'ayyinin): Two specific, identified slaves as objects of purchase, as opposed to unspecified or generic slaves.
غبن فاحش (ghabn fahish): Exorbitant overpricing that people would not normally tolerate in a transaction; exceeding the customary range of mutual concession (taghabun).
دلالة (dalala): Implication or inferred meaning, as opposed to the explicit wording (sarih). Here, the division of the total price into halves is by implication, not explicit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a principal orders his agent to buy two specific slaves for 1000 dirhams (equal value each), and the agent buys one for 600 dirhams, what is the ruling?
A) The purchase is valid for the principal, as the agent acted within customary tolerance.
B) The purchase is not valid for the principal unless the agent buys the second slave with the remaining 400 before any dispute.
C) The purchase is void and the agent must pay from his own pocket.
D) The principal may choose to accept or reject.

Key Takeaway
An agent's purchase of one of two specified slaves at more than half the total price is not binding on the principal unless the second is bought before dispute, but the Sahibayn allow a customary excess if the total goal remains achievable.

Dispute on Agent's Purchase at More Than Half the Total Price
Intra-Hanafi dispute on agent's purchase at more than half

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions on an agent's purchase at more than half the total price, and apply the rule of taghābun to determine validity.

Core Idea
An agent's purchase at over half the total is invalid for the principal unless the excess is customary and the remainder suffices.

When a principal gives an agent a lump sum to buy two items, and the agent buys one at more than half, a dispute arises: is the purchase valid for the principal? Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples disagree. This passage from al-Hidayah explains their proofs and the role of customary tolerance (taghābun). Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to analyze agency disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the disputed issue
The principal gives 1000 to buy two slaves of equal value. The agent buys one slave for more than half the total (e.g., 600). Is the purchase valid for the principal? Both sides agree on some conditions but differ on the threshold.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his evidence (dalil)
Abu Hanifa: unless the agent buys the second slave with the remaining price before any litigation, the purchase of the first is for the agent himself. His evidence: the principal's objective is to obtain both slaves for the total sum; spending more than half on one defeats that objective unless immediately remedied.
This is a strict protection of the principal's interest.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their evidence
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: if the excess is within customary tolerance (taghābun) and enough remains to buy the second, the purchase is valid for the principal. Their evidence: the agency was absolute, not restricted per item; custom allows slight variations; the principal's objective is achieved if both can be bought with the sum.
They rely on 'urf (custom) to determine acceptable variation.
4
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa, the agent must act quickly to buy the second slave or bear the cost himself. Under the Sahibayn, the agent has more leeway as long as the total remains within the budget and the excess is not excessive.
Later fatwa often adopts the Sahibayn's view.
5
Apply the rule to a new scenario
Given a principal who gives 500 to buy two items, and the agent buys one for 300 (more than half but within taghābun, with 200 left), analyze the ruling under both views.
Under Abu Hanifa, invalid unless the other is bought before dispute; under Sahibayn, valid.

فإن قال : اشتريته بألف وقال آمره بنصفه، فإن كان ألف الآمر صُدق معينين فإن لم يذكرِ الشَّمنَ فشرى أحدهما يَقَعُ عن الآمر ؛ لأنَّ التَّوكيل مطلق، وقد لا يتَّفِقُ الجمع بينهما ، وإن سمَّى ثمنهما بأن قال : اشتر لي عبدين بألف ، وقيمتهما سواء، فشرى أحدهما بالنصف أو بأقل صح عن الآمر، وإن اشترى بأكثر من النصف لا يقع عن الآمر ، بل يقع عن الوكيل، إلا إذا اشترى الآخر باقي الثَّمن قبل الخصومة ؛ لأنَّ المقصود حصول العبدين بألف ، وعندهما : إن اشترى أحدهما بأكثر من النصف مما يتغابنُ النَّاسُ فيه، وقد بقي من الثَّمن ما يشتري به الباقي يصح عن الأمر. فإن قال : اشتريته بألفور، وقال آمره بنصفه، فإن كان ألف الأمر صُدق وعندهما : إنَّ الوكيل إن اشترى أحد العبدين بأكثر من نصف الألف مما يتغابنُ النَّاسُ فيه، وقد بقي من الألف ما يشتري به العبد الباقي فجاز الشراء، ووقع عن الآمر ؛ لأنَّ التوكيل حصل مطلقاً، لا تقدير ثمن كل واحد منهما بخمسمئة، والمطلقُ يحمل على المتعارف وهو الشراء بالقيمة أو بزيادة يسيرة قدر ما يتغابنُ النَّاسُ فيه، ولكن غرضُهُ تحصيل العبدين بالألف، فلا بُدَّ من أن يبقى من الألف قدر ما يشتري بمثله الباقي ؛ ليحصل غرضه). If he said: I bought it for 1000, and his principal said for half of it, then if the principal gave the 1000, he is believed regarding the two specific [slaves]. If he did not mention the price and bought one of them, it falls for the principal because the agency is absolute, and it may not be possible to combine both. If he specified the price of both by saying: Buy for me two slaves for 1000, and their values are equal, and he bought one for half or less, it is valid for the principal. If he bought for more than half, it does not fall for the principal but falls for the agent, unless he buys the other with the remaining price before litigation, because the goal is to obtain both slaves for 1000. And according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): If he bought one for more than half by an amount in which people tolerate mutual concession, and the remaining price suffices to buy the other, the purchase is valid for the principal. If he said: I bought it for 1000, and his principal said for half of it, then if the principal gave the 1000, he is believed. And according to them: If the agent bought one of the two slaves for more than half of the 1000 by an amount in which people tolerate mutual concession, and there remains from the 1000 enough to buy the remaining slave, then the purchase is valid and falls for the principal, because the agency was absolute, not restricting the price of each to 500, and the absolute is interpreted according to custom, which is buying at value or with a slight increase that people tolerate in mutual concession. But his object is to obtain both slaves for 1000, so it is necessary that enough remains from the 1000 to buy the like of the remaining slave to achieve his object. 'And if his 1000 is not [given] and it equals half of it, the principal is believed, and if it equals it, they take an oath' means: he said: Buy for me a slave girl for 1000 and did not give him the 1000, and the agent said: I bought her for 1000, and the principal said: Rather for half of it. If her value is 500, the principal is believed, likewise if her value is more than 500 and less than 1000, due to clear contradiction, because the order was to buy a slave girl worth 1000 for 1000; and if her value is 1000, they take an oath because the agent and principal are in the position of seller and buyer; if they take an oath, the sale is rescinded between them, and the sold item remains with the agent. (1) His statement: 'slave girl'; he placed the issue in a slave girl following the author of al-Hidayah, and if he placed it in a male slave it would be better to agree with the masculine pronoun in the author's statement: I bought it for 1000. [2] His statement: 'the agent is believed'; because the agent is a trustee in it, and he claims release from the trust obligation, and the principal claims the right to reclaim 500, and the agent denies, so the statement is his because the statement belongs to the denier. (3) His statement: 'because he ordered him' etc.; meaning that the agent contradicted what the principal ordered him, because the principal ordered him to buy a slave girl worth 1000, and the agent does not have the right to buy with excessive loss, so he contradicted, thus he is a buyer for himself. [4] His statement: 'because the agent etc.'; meaning that the agent and principal in this chapter are treated as seller and buyer due to the legal exchange, as we established previously, and a dispute occurred over the price.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding disagreements between a principal (āmir/muwakkil) and an agent (wakīl) over the price paid in a purchase. The author presents the ruling of Imam Abu Hanifa and then the view of the two (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad).

The passage first addresses a scenario where the principal gives a specific amount (e.g., 1000) and the agent buys one of two slaves. According to Abu Hanifa, if the agent buys at half or less, it is valid for the principal; if he buys at more than half, the purchase is for the agent unless he buys the other slave with the remaining price before any dispute arises, because the principal's objective was to obtain both slaves for the total amount. The two (Ṣāḥibayn) hold that even if the agent buys one slave at more than half, as long as the excess is within the range of mutual concession (taghābun) and the remaining amount can buy the other slave, the purchase is valid for the principal.

The second part deals with a general dispute: the agent claims he bought for 1000, the principal says for 500. If the principal had given the agent the 1000, and the purchased item is actually worth 1000, the agent is believed (as a trustee). If the item is worth less, the principal is believed because the agent exceeded his authority by paying a price involving excessive loss (ghaban fāḥish). If the principal did not give the money and the item's value is 500 (exactly half) the principal is believed; if the value equals 1000, both take oaths and the sale is rescinded, with the item remaining with the agent.

The commentary explains the reasoning: the agent is a trustee (amīn) and claims to have fulfilled the trust, so his word is taken unless there is clear violation. The principal's order is interpreted according to custom—a slight increase is tolerated, but excessive loss makes the agent liable. The analogy is drawn to seller-buyer disputes where oaths are taken.

The marginal notes clarify that 'specific' restriction is incidental, and 'before litigation' is a condition to prevent abuse.

Key Terms
تغابن (taghābun): Mutual concession in price; a slight difference that people normally tolerate in transactions.
غبن فاحش (ghaban fāḥish): Excessive loss or overpayment beyond what people normally tolerate in similar transactions.
الوكيل والموكل (al-wakīl wa al-muwakkil): Agent and principal; the person authorized to act on behalf of another in a transaction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if an agent buys one of two slaves for more than half the total price but within the range of customary tolerance, and enough money remains to buy the other slave?
A) The purchase is invalid for the principal according to all Hanafi imams.
B) The purchase is valid for the principal according to Abu Hanifa, but invalid according to the Sahibayn.
C) The purchase is valid for the principal according to the Sahibayn, but invalid according to Abu Hanifa unless the agent buys the other before litigation.
D) The purchase is valid for the principal only if the agent buys the other slave before litigation, according to all imams.

Key Takeaway
In agency disputes over price, the key differentiator is whether the excess payment is within customary tolerance (valid for principal under Sahibayn) or requires immediate remediation (Abu Hanifa's stricter condition).

Agency Price Dispute: Two Views on Mutual Oath
Expansion on intra-Hanafi dispute about tahaluf in agency price conflict.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relied-upon Hanafi ruling that mutual oath (tahaluf) is required in an agency price dispute even after seller confirmation, and distinguish the opposing intra-school view that the agent's word with oath suffices.

Core Idea
Seller confirmation does not cancel the need for mutual oath between agent and principal – the more apparent view holds.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies a nuanced point in Hanafi agency law: what happens when an agent buys a specific item without a specified price, then disputes the price with the principal, and the seller sides with the agent? Does the seller's confirmation end the dispute, or is there still a need for mutual oath? The commentator navigates two conflicting Hanafi positions and settles on the more apparent view (al-azhar) requiring tahaluf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the parties
The principal orders the agent to buy a specific item (e.g., a slave) without naming a price. The agent buys it, then a dispute arises: the agent says the price was 1000, the principal says 500. The seller confirms the agent's claim.
The seller's confirmation is key – it creates a 'notional sale' but does not automatically settle the agent-principal dispute.
2
State the two competing Hanafi opinions
Opinion 1 (some Hanafis, cited in al-Dhakhirah and al-Kafi): No mutual oath needed – the agent's word with his oath is accepted because the seller's confirmation removes the contractual dispute and the agent is a trustee (amin). Opinion 2 (al-azhar, attributed to Abu Mansur al-Maturidi): Mutual oath is required between agent and principal, leading to rescission of the notional sale.
The second view is called 'al-azhar' (more apparent) even though the first view is also recorded.
3
Analyze the basis of each opinion
Opinion 1 relies on the principle that the agent is a trustee (amin) regarding the price if he received it; his word is accepted with oath when claiming disbursement. Opinion 2 argues that the dispute between agent and principal remains unresolved despite seller confirmation; the agent claims a certain expenditure and the principal denies it, so mutual oath is the proper judicial procedure.
The distinction is also affected by whether the agent had already received the price (cash) or not – but the passage clarifies that even without cash receipt, the same principle applies.
4
Explain the practical consequence (thamarah)
If mutual oath is performed, the notional sale is rescinded, and the agent becomes liable for the item (must pay the price to the principal). If only the agent's word with oath is accepted, the sale stands and the principal must pay the agent's claimed price.
This directly affects the financial outcome for both parties.
5
Determine the tarjih and mufta bihi
The commentator states that 'al-azhar' is mutual oath, attributed to Abu Mansur al-Maturidi and upheld by Qadi Khan and Abu Ja'far. Thus the relied-upon position is that mutual oath is required.
Later Hanafi manuals (like al-Durr al-Mukhtar) often follow this, though Ibn Abidin raised objections about the condition of oath.

Book of Agency, Chapter of Agency for Sale and Purchase. Similarly, in [the case of] a specific item for which no price was named, and he bought it and they differed about its price. And if the seller confirms the agent, in the more apparent [view] they take mutual oath. Know that what is meant by his saying: 'He is believed in all that he mentioned' is belief without oath. And similarly in a specific item for which no price was named, and he bought it and they differed about its price. And if the seller confirms the agent, in the more apparent [view] they take mutual oath. That is, if he ordered him to buy this slave for him, and did not name a price for him, and he bought it, and he said 'I bought it for a thousand', and the principal said 'Rather for half of that', they take mutual oath. And if the seller confirms the agent, the consequence is mutual oath. And if they take mutual oath, the notional sale that occurred between them is rescinded, and the sold item remains with the agent, so it becomes incumbent on the agent [to pay]. And it is objected here: there is a demand, which is that if the agent took possession of the price and then dispute occurred, contravention and trusteeship are considered; and if he did not take possession, contravention and exchange are considered. So what is the ruling in that? And it was answered: In the first, the trusteeship preceded the exchange, and precedence is a cause for preference, so it is considered therein, unlike the second. Thus in 'al-Nata'ij'. [1] His saying: belief without oath; thus it is mentioned in 'al-Dar', and the author of 'al-Durr al-Mukhtar' followed him, but they explicitly stated what contradicts it. (†) (4) He said in 'al-Ashbah': The agent's word is accepted with his oath in what he claims, except the agent for collecting a debt, if after the death of the principal he claims that he collected it during his lifetime and handed it over to him, then his word is not accepted except with evidence. As in 'al-Walwaljiyya'. End. (1) What the commentator mentioned, the author of 'al-Durar' followed him (2:288), and 'al-Idah' (116/a), and 'al-Durr al-Muntaqa' (2:234), and 'Majma' al-Anhur' (2:234), and others, but Ibn Abidin objected in 'Minhah al-Khaliq' (7:164) and mentioned a long discussion about the condition of oath, and the texts of reliable scholars in that. (2) 'Nata'ij al-Afkar' (8:64). (3) 'al-Durr al-Mukhtar' (4:406). (4) 'al-Ashbah' (p.297). Zubdat al-Nihayah li 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah. And he only said this because in the case of the seller confirming the agent, it has been said: no mutual oath, rather the word is for the agent with oath, because the dispute is removed by the seller's confirmation, so mutual oath does not proceed. But the more apparent [view] is that they take mutual oath, and this is the statement of Abu Mansur. And it is explicitly stated in 'al-Dhakhirah' that the word of the trustee is considered with oath. And he said in 'al-Kafi': If the price was cash, the word is for the agent with his oath, because the price was a trust in his hand, and he claimed to have discharged the trust in the manner he was ordered, so his word is accepted. End. And it is apparent that there is no difference in believing the agent due to his being a trustee from one place to another, so the explicit statement in one place suffices, so the commentator's statement is not complete. [1] His saying: 'because... etc.'; meaning because the dispute is removed by the seller's confirmation, since he is present, so the mutual confirmation of the seller and the agent is treated as a new contract at that moment. And if the contract was made, the slave becomes incumbent on the principal, so likewise here, unlike the first issue because the seller there was absent, so the dispute was considered. And Qadi Khan corrected it, following the faqih Abu Ja'far. [2] His saying: 'Abu Mansur': he is Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Mahmud al-Maturidi. Al-Maturid is a village in Samarkand. He is the leader of the jurists and the imam of the theologians. No eye saw his like in establishing beliefs, nor ear heard his equal in removing corruptions. Mercy from Allah the Exalted upon the earth. He studied under Ahmad al-Juzjani, who studied under Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani, who studied under Imam Muhammad. This was stated by 'Allamah 'Ali al-Qari and other great researchers. He authored numerous beneficial books, including 'Kitab al-Tawhid', 'Kitab Ta'wilat al-Qur'an', 'Kitab al-Maqalat', and others, and he refuted 'al-Usul al-Khamsah' of al-Bahili, and 'al-Radd 'ala al-Qaramitah' with a noble refutation, and 'Radd al-Imamah' of some Rafidis with a subtle refutation. Many great researchers studied under him, including Abu al-Qasim Ishaq ibn Muhammad al-Samarqandi, Abu Muhammad 'Abd al-Karim ibn Musa al-Bazdawi, 'Ali ibn Sa'id, and others.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling from al-Wiqayah regarding agency for the purchase of a specific item without specifying a price. The original ruling states that if the agent buys the item and then disputes the price with the principal, and the seller confirms the agent's claim, the more apparent view is that the agent and principal take mutual oaths (tahaluf). The commentator explains that this means they are both believed without oath only in the sense of being believed, but the final ruling is mutual oath. The scenario: the principal orders the agent to buy a particular slave without naming a price. The agent buys it and says the price was 1000, while the principal says it was 500. If the seller confirms the agent's statement, the consequence is that they must perform mutual oaths. If they do, the notional sale between them is rescinded, and the agent becomes responsible for the slave (i.e., he must pay the price himself). The passage then addresses an objection: if the agent had already taken possession of the price (i.e., the principal had given him cash to pay), then the issue involves both trusteeship (amanah) and exchange. The answer distinguishes: when the price was already in the agent's hand, the trusteeship aspect precedes, so the agent's word is taken with oath. When the price was not given, only exchange is considered. The commentator then notes a dispute: some say that when the seller confirms the agent, there is no mutual oath because the confirmation removes the dispute; rather, the agent's word with oath is accepted. However, the more apparent view (al-azhar) is that they do take mutual oaths, and this is the opinion of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi. The passage cites al-Dhakhirah and al-Kafi to support the general principle that a trustee (amin) is believed with his oath. Finally, a biographical note on Abu Mansur al-Maturidi is given, highlighting his stature as a Hanafi jurist and theologian.

Key Terms
الوكالة (al-wikalah): Agency; the appointment of another to act on one's behalf in a legal transaction.
المأمور (al-ma'mur): The agent who is ordered to perform a specific task.
التحالف (al-tahālu): Mutual oath; each party swears an oath to support their claim, leading to rescission of the contract.
الأظهر (al-azhar): The more apparent or most correct view according to the school.
الأمين (al-amīn): Trustee; one entrusted with property, whose word is accepted with oath regarding the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where an agent is sent to buy a specific slave without naming a price, and after purchase the agent says the price was 1000, the principal says 500, and the seller confirms the agent, what is the more apparent Hanafi ruling?
A) The agent is believed without oath because the seller confirms him.
B) The agent and principal take mutual oaths (tahālu).
C) The principal is believed without oath.
D) The sale is void automatically.

Key Takeaway
The more apparent Hanafi position is that mutual oath (tahaluf) is required even if the seller confirms the agent, because the dispute is between agent and principal, not agent and seller.

The Agent's Statement When Seller Confirms: Mutual Oath
Explaining the ruling on mutual oath when seller confirms agent's claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school's relied-upon position requires mutual oath (taḥāluf) between agent and principal when the seller confirms the agent's claim, and identify the practical consequence of the opposing view.

Core Idea
Seller's confirmation does not end the dispute; mutual oath is required because seller becomes a stranger.

This passage addresses a procedural dispute within the Hanafi school: when an agent claims to have paid the price and the seller confirms, does this end the dispute between agent and principal? Some say yes; the relied-upon view says no—mutual oath is needed. Let's explore the reasoning behind each position and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Recognise that some early Hanafi scholars held no mutual oath is needed (because seller's confirmation resolves the dispute), while the relied-upon view (al-aẓhar) states that mutual oath is required.
The text explicitly says 'لكن الأظهر أن يتحالفا' (but the more apparent is that they take mutual oath).
2
Analyse the argument against mutual oath
Understand the reasoning: the seller's confirmation removes the conflict, so the agent's statement should be accepted with oath alone. This treats the confirmation as akin to creating a new contract on the spot.
This view considers the seller present and confirming, thus no need for mutual oath.
3
Understand the reasoning for mutual oath
The seller, after receiving the price, becomes a stranger to both parties. His confirmation does not bind the principal, who was not a party to the sale. The principal may still contest the agent's discharge.
The agent is a trustee (amīn), but his statement alone does not preclude the principal from swearing.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If no mutual oath: agent's statement stands, principal bears loss. If mutual oath: principal may swear and avoid payment if truthful, or the agent may swear and be exonerated.
This difference affects who ultimately bears the financial risk.
5
Note the tarjih authority
The view requiring mutual oath is declared al-aẓhar (more apparent) and was corrected by Qadi Khan following al-Faqih Abu Ja'far. This is the mufta bihi position.
Thus, the later Hanafi tradition leans toward mutual oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما قال هذا : لأنَّ في صورة تصديق البائع المأمور قد قيل : لا تحالف ، بل القولُ للمأمور مع اليمين ؛ لأن الخلافَ يَرْتَفِعُ بتصديق البائع ، فلا يجري التحالف ، لكن الأظهر أن يتحالفا، وهذا قول أبي منصور" له وقد صرّح في «الذخيرة»: بأن قول الأمين معتبر مع اليمين. وقال في «الكافي»: ولو كان الثمن منقوداً فالقولُ للمأمور مع يمينه ؛ لأنَّ الثمن كان أمانة في يده، وقد ادعى الخروج عن عهدة الأمانة من الوجه الذي أمر به فكان القول له. انتهى. ومن الظاهر أنه لا فرق في تصديق الوكيل لأجل كونه أميناً بين موضع وموضع، فيكفي التصريح في موضع ، فلا يتم قول الشارح ). [١]قوله : لأن ... الخ ؛ يعني لأنَّ الخلاف يرتفع بتصديق البائع، إذ هو حاضر، فيجعل تصادقُ البائع والوكيل بمنزلة إنشاء العقد في الحال، ولو أنشأ العقد يلزم العبد للآمر، فكذا هذا، بخلاف المسألة الأولى ؛ لأنَّ البائع فيها غائب، فاعتبر الاختلاف، وصححه قاضي خان له تبعاً للفقيه أبي جعفر . لأنَّ البائع بعد استيفاء الثَّمَنِ أجنبي عنهما، وأيضاً هو أجنبي عن الموكل فلا يصدق عليه.
And he only said this because in the case of the seller confirming [the statement of] the agent, it has been said: there is no mutual oath, but rather the statement of the agent is taken with his oath, because the dispute is removed by the seller's confirmation, so mutual oath does not occur. But the more apparent [view] is that they take mutual oath, and this is the statement of Abu Mansur. And it has been explicitly stated in al-Dhakhira that the statement of the trustee is considered with his oath. And in al-Kafi it is said: If the price was cash, then the statement of the agent is taken with his oath, because the price was a trust in his hand, and he claimed to have been discharged from the trust in the manner he was commanded, so his statement is taken. End. And it is obvious that there is no difference in the confirmation of the agent due to his being a trustee between one place and another, so the explicit statement in one place is sufficient, so the statement of the commentator is not complete. [1] His saying: 'because...' etc.; means that the dispute is removed by the seller's confirmation, because he is present, so the mutual confirmation of the seller and the agent is treated like the creation of the contract immediately, and if he created the contract, it would be binding on the principal for the agent, and similarly here, unlike the first issue because the seller was absent, so the difference is considered. And Qadi Khan corrected it following al-Faqih Abu Ja'far. Because the seller, after receiving the price, is a stranger to both of them, and also he is a stranger to the principal, so his statement is not accepted.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether mutual oath (taḥāluf) is required between the agent (mā'mūr) and the principal (muwakkil) when the seller confirms the agent's claim of payment. Some early scholars held that no mutual oath is needed because the seller's confirmation ends the dispute. However, the relied-upon view (al-aẓhar), attributed to Abu Mansur al-Maturidi and corrected by Qadi Khan, is that mutual oath is necessary. The reason is that the seller, after receiving the price, becomes a stranger to both parties, so his confirmation does not bind the principal. The agent is an amīn (trustee), so his statement is taken with oath, but this does not prevent the principal from contesting. The practical consequence: if mutual oath is taken and the principal swears, he may avoid payment; if not, the agent's statement stands, and the principal bears the loss.

Key Terms
تحالف (taḥāluf): Mutual oath between two litigants; each swears to their claim, often in sale disputes when price is contested.
أمين (amīn): Trustee; one entrusted with property, whose statement regarding the trust is accepted with oath.
مأمور (mā'mūr): Agent; the person commanded (by the principal) to act, here to buy or sell.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where the seller confirms the agent's statement, what is the relied-upon Hanafi position regarding mutual oath (taḥāluf) between the agent and the principal?
A) No mutual oath because the seller's confirmation resolves the dispute.
B) Mutual oath is required, and it is the more apparent view.
C) The agent's statement is accepted without oath.
D) The principal's statement is taken without oath.

Key Takeaway
When the seller confirms the agent's claim, the relied-upon Hanafi view requires mutual oath between agent and principal because the seller is a stranger after payment.

Return of Defective Item to Agent: Defect Types and Proofs
Explaining ruling on returning defective goods to a selling agent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between defects that occur and those that do not, and apply the Hanafi rule that an agent's acknowledgment (iqrār) of a defect that can occur binds the agent personally, unlike evidence (bīnah) or refusal to take oath (nukūl) which allow return to the principal.

Core Idea
An agent's personal acknowledgment of a defect binds him; only external proof allows return to the principal.

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya details the nuanced Hanafi rules when a sold item is returned to an agent due to a defect. The key distinction is whether the defect is one that can occur after sale or not, and the type of proof presented. Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining who bears the loss and the agent's right to pass the return to the principal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defect category
Determine if the defect is one that does not normally occur (e.g., extra finger) or can occur (e.g., staining). This classification triggers different rules.
The text gives examples: extra finger vs. defects that appear over time.
2
Identify the method of return
Was the return to the agent made by evidence (bīnah), refusal to take oath (nukūl), or acknowledgment (iqrār)?
These are the three proofs considered.
3
Apply the rule based on defect and method
For non-occurring defects: any proof suffices for return to principal. For occurring defects: only bīnah or nukūl allow return to principal; iqrār binds the agent.
This is the core ruling.
4
Consider the need for proof
The judge may require proof if the date of sale is unclear or the defect is known only by specialists (women/doctors).
The text explains why proof is sometimes needed.
5
Check the court context
Distinguish between iqrār made voluntarily (iqāla, new sale) and under court order (compelled rescission). Only in the latter can the agent litigate the principal.
This affects the agent's recourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية ولو رد مبيع على وكيل بعيب يَحْدُثُ مثلُهُ أو لا يحدث مثله ببينة، أو نكول، أو إقرار رَدَّه على أمره إلا وكيل أقر بعيب يحدث مثله ، ولزمه ذلك ولا تهمة في البيع فيجوز؛ لأنَّ الأمر ببيع الكُلِّ يتضمن بيع النصف ؛ لأنه ربما لا يتيسر بيع الكل دفعة. (ولو رد مبيع على وكيل بعيب يَحْدُثُ مثلُهُ أو لا يحدث مثله ببينة، أو نكول، أو إقرار رده على أمره إلا وكيل أقرّ بعيب يحدث مثله ، ولزمه ذلك) : أي باع الوكيلُ بالبيع ، ثمَّ رُدَّ عليه بالعيب ؛ فإن كانَ العيب مما لا يحدث مثلُه ؛ كالإصبع الزائدة ، أو لا يحدث مثله في هذه المدَّةِ يردُّ على الأمر سواء كان الرد على الوكيل بالبينة أو بالنكول أو بالإقرار، وإن كان العيب مما يحدث مثلُه ؛ فإن كان الرد عليه بالبينة أو النُّكول ردَّه على الآمر ، وإن كان بالإقرار لا يردُّه على الأمر. وتأويل اشتراط البينةِ أو النكول أو الإقرار في العيب الذي لا يحدث مثله ، أنَّ القاضي ربما يعلمُ أنَّ هذا العيب لا يحدث مثلُهُ في مدة شهر، لكن يشتبه عليه تاريخ البيع فيحتاج إلى إحدى هذه الحجج، أو كانَ العيب لا يعرفُهُ إلا النِّسَاءُ أو الأطباء، وقولُ المرأةِ والطَّبيب حجَّةٌ في توجهِ الخصومة لا في الرد ؛ فيفتقر إلى هذه الحجج للرد، حتى لو عاين القاضي البيع والعيب ظاهراً لا يحتاج إلى شيء منها. يتقيد بيع الوكيل ويتقيد شراؤه ؛ لأنَّ التهمة متمكنة في الوكيل بالشراء، بأن يكون شراؤه لنفسه، فلما استكثر ثمنه ألحقه بالموكل بخلاف البيع ؛ ولهذا لو قال الوكيل بالشراء : اشتريت وقبضت، وهلك في يدي، فهات الثمن لم يقبل قوله، ولـو قـال الوكيل بالبيع : بعت وقبضتُ الثمن وهلك، كان القولُ قوله صرَّح به الشمني وغيره. [١] قوله : فإن كان...الخ ؛ تقريره : إِنَّ هذا العيب لا يخلو : إما أن لا يكون حادثاً كالأصبع الزائدة والسن الزائدة، أو يكون حادثاً لكنّه لا يحدث مثله في تلك المدة أو يحدث في مثلها. فإن كان غير حادث رده القاضي بغير حجَّةٍ من بينة أو نكول أو إقرار، وكذا إذا كان حادثاً، لكنّه لا يحدث مثله في مثل تلك المدة، ردّه القاضي بغير بينة ولا نكول والإقرار لعلمه بكونه عند البائع. وإنما شرط المصنف الله البينة أو النكول أو الإقرار ؛ لأنَّ الحال قد يشتبه على القاضي ؛ بأن لا يعرف تاريخ البيع، فاحتاج إلى هذه الحجة ليعلم أن تاريخ البيع منذ شهر، فيظهر عند القاضي أنَّ هذا العيب كان في يد البائع فيرد المبيع عليه، أو كان عيباً لا يعرفه إلا الأطباء والنساء. وقولهم : حجة في توجه الخصومة لا في الردّ ، حتى لو كان القاضي عاين البيع، وكان العيب ظاهراً لا يحتاج إلى شيء من هذه الحجج، وإن كان عيباً يحدث مثله فكذلك الحكم إن كان ببينة أو نكول ؛ لأنَّ البينة حجَّةٌ مطلقة، وكذا النكولُ حجَّةٌ في حقه فيرد عليه. ثم في هذه المواضع كلّها ردَّ القاضي على الوكيل يكون رداً على الموكل ، فإن رده المشتري على الوكيل بإقرار بقضاء القاضي لا يكون رداً على الموكل ؛ لأنَّ الإقرار حجَّةٌ قاصرة ، ة، فلا يتعدّى بخلاف ما إذا كان العيب مما لا يحدث مثله. ولكن للوكيل أن يخاصم موكله فيردّ عليه ببيئة أو نكول، وإن رده المشتري على الوكيل بإقراره برضاه من غير قضاء فليس له أن يرده على الموكل ؛ لأنه إقالة، وهي بيع جديد في حق ثالث، والموكل ثالثهما. بخلاف ما إذا رده على الوكيل بإقرار بقضاء القاضي ؛ لأنَّ الرد حصل بقضاء وكان مكرهاً، فانعدم التراضي وهو شرط في المعاوضات المالية، فجعل فسخاً في حق الكل، ولكن الفسخ مستند إلى دليل قاصر وهو الإقرار، فعملنا بهما فمن حيث الردّ فسخ ، وكان للوكيل أن يخاصم الموكل، ومن حيث أنه مستند إلى دليل قاصر لـزم الوكيل إلا أن يقيم حجَّةً على الموكل. وإن كان العيب غير حادث أو كان حادثاً إلا أنه يحدث مثله في تلك المدة، فردّه على الوكيل بإقـرار بـغـيـر قـضاء لزم الوكيل وليس له أن يخاصم الموكل في عامة روايات «المبسوط».
Summary of the final goal of 'Umda al-Ri'aya. And if a sold item is returned to an agent due to a defect that can occur like it or cannot occur like it, by evidence, or refusal to take oath, or acknowledgment, he returns it to his principal except an agent who acknowledged a defect that can occur like it, and that is binding upon him. And there is no suspicion in sale, so it is permissible; because the command to sell the whole includes selling half; since perhaps selling the whole at once is not feasible. (And if a sold item is returned to an agent due to a defect that can occur like it or cannot occur like it, by evidence, or refusal to take oath, or acknowledgment, he returns it to his principal except an agent who acknowledged a defect that can occur like it, and that is binding upon him): That is, the agent sold, then the item was returned to him due to the defect. If the defect is such that its like does not occur—like an extra finger—or its like does not occur in this period, he returns it to the principal whether the return to the agent was by evidence, refusal to take oath, or acknowledgment. If the defect is such that its like occurs, then if the return was by evidence or refusal to take oath, he returns it to the principal; if by acknowledgment, he does not return it to the principal. The interpretation of requiring evidence, refusal to take oath, or acknowledgment for a defect whose like does not occur is that the judge may know that this defect does not occur like it within a month, but the date of sale is unclear to him, so he needs one of these proofs. Or the defect may be known only by women or doctors, and the statement of the woman and doctor is a proof for initiating litigation, not for the return; so he needs these proofs for the return. Even if the judge personally witnessed the sale and the defect was apparent, he does not need any of them. The sale of the agent is restricted, and his purchase is restricted; because suspicion is strong in the agent for purchase, that he may be buying for himself, so when he increases the price, he attributes it to the principal, unlike sale. For this, if the agent for purchase says, 'I bought and took possession, and it perished in my hand, so bring the price,' his statement is not accepted. But if the agent for sale says, 'I sold and took the price, and it perished,' his statement is accepted, as clearly stated by al-Shamni and others. [1] His statement: 'If it is... etc.'; its explanation: This defect is either not occurring like an extra finger or extra tooth, or it is occurring but its like does not occur in that period or does occur in like it. If it is not occurring, the judge returns it without any proof of evidence, refusal to take oath, or acknowledgment. Similarly, if it is occurring but its like does not occur in such a period, the judge returns it without evidence or refusal to take oath, and [without] acknowledgment because he knows it was with the seller. The author only stipulated evidence, refusal to take oath, or acknowledgment because the situation may be unclear to the judge, such that he does not know the date of sale, so he needs this proof to know that the date of sale was a month ago, so it becomes clear to the judge that this defect was in the seller's hand, so he returns the sold item to him, or the defect is known only by doctors and women. Their statement: 'Proof for initiating litigation, not for the return'—even if the judge witnessed the sale and the defect was apparent, he does not need any of these proofs. If it is a defect whose like occurs, the ruling is the same if it is by evidence or refusal to take oath; because evidence is an absolute proof, and likewise refusal to take oath is a proof against him, so he returns it to him. Then in all these cases, the judge's return to the agent is a return to the principal. If the buyer returns it to the agent by acknowledgment through a judge's ruling, it is not a return to the principal; because acknowledgment is a limited proof, so it does not extend, unlike if the defect is such that its like does not occur. But the agent may litigate his principal and return it to him by evidence or refusal to take oath. If the buyer returns it to the agent by his acknowledgment with his consent without a court ruling, he cannot return it to the principal; because it is a rescission (iqāla), which is a new sale regarding a third party, and the principal is the third party to both. Unlike if he returned it to the agent by acknowledgment through a judge's ruling; because the return occurred by ruling and he was compelled, so mutual consent is absent—which is a condition in financial transactions—so it is considered a rescission regarding all. But the rescission is based on a limited evidence, i.e., acknowledgment, so we act on both: from the perspective of the return, it is a rescission, and the agent may litigate the principal; from the perspective that it is based on limited evidence, it binds the agent unless he provides proof against the principal. If the defect is not occurring or is occurring but its like occurs in that period, and he returns it to the agent by acknowledgment without a court ruling, it binds the agent and he cannot litigate the principal in the majority of narrations of al-Mabsut.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a detailed Hanafi ruling on the return of a defective item to an agent (wakīl bil-bay‘) and the extent to which the agent can pass that return to the principal (muwakkil). The passage opens by stating the general rule: if a sold item is returned to an agent due to a defect, the agent returns it to the principal—except when the agent himself acknowledged a defect that can occur (i.e., a new defect), in which case the return binds the agent personally. The author then explains the reasoning: the command to sell the whole includes selling half, implying that the agent's authority is broad. The commentary clarifies that the key distinction is whether the defect is one that does not normally occur (like an extra finger) or one that can occur over time. For a defect that does not occur, any mode of proof (bīnah, nukūl, or iqrār) allows the agent to return to the principal. For a defect that does occur, only bīnah or nukūl allow the agent to pass the return; iqrār binds the agent because his admission is considered a limited proof that does not extend to the principal. The passage then explains why proof is sometimes needed: the judge may not know the date of sale, or the defect may be knowable only by specialists (women, doctors), whose testimony is sufficient for litigation but not for the return itself. At the end, the passage distinguishes between an iqrār made with mutual consent (which is a new sale, iqāla) and one compelled by court order (which is a rescission, but still binds the agent due to limited evidence). The final note mentions that in the majority of narrations of al-Mabsut, if the defect can occur and the agent acknowledges it without court, he cannot litigate his principal.

Key Terms
رد (radd): Return of the sold item due to a defect; also the legal act of rescinding the sale because of a defect.
عيب (‘ayb): A defect in the sold item that gives the buyer the right to return it.
وكيل (wakīl): An agent appointed by the principal (muwakkil) to perform a specific act, here selling or buying.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence or proof, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females, used in court to establish a claim.
نكول (nukūl): Refusal of the defendant to take an oath when required, which is considered as an admission against him.
إقرار (iqrār): Acknowledgment or confession by the agent that the defect existed at the time of sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer returns a defective item to the agent with evidence (bīnah) and the defect is one that does not occur (e.g., an extra finger), what is the ruling regarding the agent's return to the principal?
A) The agent must return it to the principal.
B) The agent is bound personally and cannot return it to the principal.
C) The return is invalid regardless of proof.
D) The agent can choose whether to return it.

Key Takeaway
The binding nature of an agent's acknowledgment depends on the defect type and the presence of judicial compulsion; for defects that can occur, acknowledgment binds the agent, not the principal.

Exception of Litigation for Co-Agents
Ruling that one agent may litigate alone despite joint appointment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a single co-agent may litigate alone and distinguish between joint and successive appointments.

Core Idea
A co-agent may litigate alone because joint presence is impossible — necessity overrides consent.

This lesson unpacks a nuanced exception in Hanafi agency law: although co-agents appointed jointly cannot generally act alone, litigation is an exception because requiring both to appear simultaneously would cause hardship and confusion. The passage explains the underlying principle, contrasts with successive appointments and executors, and addresses a disagreement with Zufar and the 'three' (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and al-Shafi'i).

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
State that co-agents appointed jointly cannot act alone because the principal consented to their joint opinion.
This applies even if the price is fixed, because discretion remains.
2
Identify the litigation exception
Note that the passage carves out an exception: one co-agent may litigate alone.
The exception is based on impossibility of joint presence.
3
State the opposing view
Zufar (and the 'three') hold that even in litigation, both agents must act together because litigation requires opinion.
The opposing view emphasizes the principal's consent to both opinions.
4
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi response is rational: simultaneous presence is impossible because it leads to harshness and confusion. The opinion is formed jointly beforehand, then one executes it.
This is a dalil aqli (rational proof).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: In Hanafi practice, a single co-agent can represent the principal in court without the other present.
Zufar's view would require both to appear, which is impractical.
