Except in litigation. [This applies] only regarding what they were jointly appointed for; because the principal only consented to their joint opinion, not to the opinion of one of them, even if the price was fixed, because fixing it does not prevent the use of opinion regarding increase and decrease, and in choosing the seller and buyer and the like. This is when he appointed them with a single statement, such as saying: 'I appoint both of you to sell my slave,' and the like. But if his appointment of them was successive, then one of them may act alone, because the principal consented to each one's opinion individually at the time of his appointment, so it does not change thereafter, unlike the case of two executors: if he appoints each of them with a separate statement, yet one of them may not act alone according to the soundest view, because the obligatory nature of the will is [established] at death, and at death they become executors jointly. The ruling of agency is established by the appointment itself. If each of them acknowledges the contract, each one substitutes for the other in action. And if he appointed them with a single statement, one of them may not act alone, even if one of them is a free adult and the other a slave or a minor. This is explicitly stated in al-Munah and others. If one of them sells in the presence of the other, then if the other ratifies it, it is valid; otherwise not. And if he was absent and then ratified it, it is not valid according to Abu Hanifa. And if one of them dies or loses his mind, the other may not act alone, because the principal did not consent to his opinion alone. This is explicitly stated by al-Ayni, al-Zayla'i, and others. [Regarding] his statement: 'Except in litigation,' it means that the action of one of them in litigation is permissible; so one of them may litigate alone. Zufar said: It is not permissible because it requires opinion, and the principal did not consent to either one of them. And the three (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and al-Shafi'i?) held this view. Our evidence: Their simultaneous presence in litigation is impossible because it leads to harshness and confusion.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the ruling that two co-agents cannot act alone, with the exception of litigation. The author explains that when a principal appoints two agents with a single statement, neither may act independently because the principal consented to their joint opinion, even if the price is fixed. The reasoning is that fixing the price still leaves discretion regarding increase, decrease, choice of counterparty, etc. However, if the appointments were successive, each agent may act alone because the principal consented to each individually. An important distinction is made with executors (wasiyyayn): even if appointed separately, they cannot act alone in the soundest view because the will takes effect at death, making them executors jointly. The passage also notes that if one agent's action is ratified by the other in his presence, it is valid; but if the ratifier was absent, Abu Hanifa holds that ratification is ineffective. Death or insanity of one agent terminates the authority of the other. The commentator then addresses the specific exception of litigation, stating that one agent may litigate alone, contra Zufar who argues that litigation requires opinion and thus needs both. The Hanafi response is that simultaneous presence is impossible due to hardship and confusion, so it is permitted. The opinion of the 'three' (likely Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and al-Shafi'i) aligns with Zufar.

Key Terms
الخصومة (al-khusūmah): Litigation, including presenting the case in court and arguing it.
التعسف والتشويش (al-ta‘assuf wa al-tashwīsh): Hardship and confusion that would result if both agents had to be present together in litigation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because the principal only appointed one of them.
C) Because simultaneous presence is impossible and causes hardship.
D) Because the other agent is always absent.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, necessity permits a single co-agent to litigate alone despite joint appointment, but this exception is limited to court proceedings.

Actions a Single Co-Agent May Perform Alone
Listing actions where a single co-agent may act alone

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four actions a single co-agent may perform alone and explain why each does not require mutual agreement.

Core Idea
Return, pay, divorce, free — one agent suffices when no judgment needed.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies a practical point in agency law: when two agents are appointed together, sometimes one can act alone. The key is whether the action requires independent opinion (ra'y) or is purely mechanical. We will explore four such actions and the reasoning behind each.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four actions
List the actions the author permits for a single co-agent: returning a trust, paying a debt, divorce, and manumission.
All are mentioned in the first sentence.
2
Distinguish 'return' vs 'reclaim'
Returning (radd) a trust, usurped item, or loan is allowed alone because it is a simple delivery. Reclaiming (istirdad) requires both because it involves taking possession, which requires judgment.
The passage explicitly contrasts radd with istirdad.
3
Understand 'pure expression'
Divorce and manumission are accomplished by mere utterance (ta'bir mahd); they do not require deliberation or opinion, so one agent suffices.
The author says these are 'tabyīn mahḍ'.
4
Apply the co-agency rule
General rule: co-agents act jointly for matters requiring opinion. These four are exceptions because they are mechanical. If a co-agent acts alone in a judgment-based matter, the act is invalid and he may be liable.
The passage uses demanding debt (iqtida') as an example requiring both.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورد وديعة، وقضاء دين، وطلاق وعتق لم يعوضا ; فلأحدهما أن يردها وحده، فإنّه لا يحتاج فيه إلى الرأي، هكذا ردّ المغصوب والعارية وغيرهما ، وقول صاحب «المنح ) : ورد عين أولى لشموله ما ذكرناه، وقيَّد بالرد احترازاً عن الاسترداد ، فليس لأحدهما القبض بدون صاحبه ؛ لإمكان اجتماعهما، وللموكل فيه غرض صحيح ؛ لأنَّ حفظ اثنين ليس كحفظ واحد، فإذا قبضه أحدهما كله ؛ لأنه قبضه بغير إذن المالك. فإن قيل : ينبغي أن يضمن النصف ؛ لأنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ منهما مأمور بقبضه بقبض النصف. قلنا : ذاك مع إذن صاحبه، وفي حال الإنفراد فغير مأمور بقبض شيء منه. أفاده في «السراج الوهاج».

Returning a trust, paying a debt, and divorce and manumission that are not for compensation. So one of them may return it (the trust) alone, because it does not require opinion. Similarly, returning a usurped item, a loan, and others. The statement of the author of al-Munah: 'Returning an item is more appropriate because it includes what we mentioned, and he restricted it to returning to avoid reclaiming; so one of them may not take possession without the other, because their joint presence is possible, and the principal has a valid interest: because two people's preservation is not like one's preservation. So if one of them takes possession of the whole thing, he is liable because he took it without the owner's permission. If it is said: He should be liable for half, because each of them is ordered to take possession of half. We respond: That is when his partner consents; in the case of acting alone, he is not ordered to take possession of anything.' This is benefited from al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. [Regarding] his statement: 'Paying a debt,' so one of them may pay it because it does not require opinion. And demanding a debt is like reclaiming a trust. [Regarding] his statement: 'Divorce and manumission not for compensation,' so one of them may divorce his wife alone or manumit his slave alone, because they do not require opinion; rather, they are pure expressions, and the expression of two and one is the same.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage lists several actions that a single co-agent may perform alone because they do not require independent judgment (ra'y). The actions are: (1) Returning a trust (wadi'ah) to its owner; also returning usurped property, borrowed items, etc. The author notes that 'returning' is specified to exclude reclaiming (istirdad), which requires both agents because it involves taking possession. If one agent takes possession alone, he becomes liable. (2) Paying a debt: one agent may pay the principal's debt without the other because it is a straightforward act. (3) Demanding a debt (equated to reclaiming a trust) is different; it requires both. (4) Divorce and manumission that are not for compensation (i.e., not in exchange for payment) can be effected by one agent because they are pure legal expressions (ta'bir mahd) where the utterance suffices. The commentary emphasizes that for actions requiring opinion, both agents must act jointly; for mechanical actions, one suffices.

Key Terms
الرد (al-radd): Returning an item (trust, usurped, borrowed) to its owner; contrasts with istirdad (reclaiming).
تعبير محض (ta'bīr mahḍ): Pure expression; an act accomplished solely by utterance without need for deliberation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
One co-agent may alone perform actions that are purely mechanical, but not those requiring independent judgment.

Acts a Joint Agent May Perform Alone
Commentary on the authority of joint agents to act alone

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three acts a joint agent may perform alone and explain why they are exceptions based on the criterion of not requiring opinion (ra'y).

Core Idea
A joint agent may act alone when the act requires no opinion—only pure expression or administrative routine.

When two agents are appointed jointly, the general rule is that they must act together. However, Hanafi fiqh identifies exceptions where one agent may act independently. This lesson explores those exceptions and the underlying reason: the act does not require legal judgment (ra'y). Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying agency law in both classical and modern contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three excepted acts
From the passage, list the three actions a joint agent may perform alone: returning a deposit, paying a debt, and divorcing or manumitting without compensation.
These are not exhaustive; they illustrate the principle.
2
Understand the governing criterion
The passage states that these acts do not require 'ra'y' (opinion/judgment). Acts requiring deliberation or discretion must be done jointly.
Ra'y is a legal term meaning ijtihad or considered opinion.
3
Analyze why each act meets the criterion
Returning a deposit is a ministerial task; paying a debt is a known obligation; divorce/manumission without compensation is pure verbal expression (ta'bir mahd). None require weighing options or consulting.
The condition 'without compensation' is crucial for talaq and itq.
4
Contrast with acts requiring ra'y
The passage contrasts 'returning' with 'reclaiming' a deposit. Reclaiming (istirdad) requires judgment about timing and consequences, so it must be done jointly.
Similarly, demanding a debt (iqta') is like reclaiming and requires joint action.
5
Recognize the exception for acts without compensation
Divorce and manumission with compensation (e.g., khula, mukataba) require ra'y because the amount and terms must be negotiated; hence they are not included in the exception.
This is explicitly stated: 'without compensation' (lam yu'awada).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورد وديعة، وقضاء دين، وطلاق وعتق لم يعوضا. [قوله : ورد وديعة ؛ فلأحد الوكيلين أن يردها وحده، فإنّه لا يحتاج فيه إلى الرأي، هكذا ردّ المغصوب والعارية وغيرهما ، وقول صاحب «المنح» : ورد عين أولى لشموله ما ذكرناه، وقيَّد بالرد احترازاً عن الاسترداد ، فليس لأحدهما القبض بدون صاحبه ؛ لإمكان اجتماعهما، وللموكل فيه غرض صحيح ؛ لأنَّ حفظ اثنين ليس كحفظ واحد، فإذا قبضه أحدهما كله ؛ لأنه قبضه بغير إذن المالك. فإن قيل : ينبغي أن يضمن النصف ؛ لأنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ منهما مأمور بقبضه بقبض النصف. ضمن قلنا : ذاك مع إذن صاحبه، وفي حال الإنفراد فغير مأمور بقبض شيء منه. أفاده في «السراج الوهاج». قوله : وقضاء دين ؛ فلأحدهما أن يقضيه بعدم الاحتياج فيه إلى الرأي ، واقتضاء الدين مثل استرداد الوديعة. قوله : وطلاق وعتق لم يعوضا ؛ فلأحدهما أن يطلق وحده زوجته أو يعتق وحده عبده ؛ لأنه لا حاجة فيهما إلى الرأي ، بل هو تعبير محض، وعبارة المثنى والواحد سواء. لم يعوضا) أما في الخصومة ؛ فلأن الاجتماع فيها يفضي إلى الشغب، وفي الأمور الأخر لا يحتاج إلى الرأي. بخلاف المعين
Returning a deposit, paying a debt, and divorce and manumission without compensation. [He said: Regarding 'returning a deposit': one of the two agents may return it alone, for it does not require opinion. Likewise, returning a usurped item, a loaned item, and others. The author of al-Munah said: 'returning a specific item' is better as it includes what we mentioned. He restricted it to 'returning' to exclude reclaiming, for it is not permissible for one of them to take possession without his companion, because it is possible for them both to meet, and the principal has a valid interest, for the preservation by two is not like preservation by one. If one of them takes possession, he takes it all, because he took it without the owner's permission. If it is said: he should be liable for half, because each of them is commanded to take possession of half. We say: that is with the permission of his companion; in the case of solitude, he is not commanded to take possession of anything from it. This is stated in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. He said: 'paying a debt': one of them may pay it because it does not require opinion. And demanding a debt is like reclaiming a deposit. He said: 'and divorce and manumission without compensation': one of them may divorce his wife alone or free his slave alone, because there is no need for opinion in them, rather it is pure expression, and the expression of the dual and singular is the same. 'Without compensation' as for litigation, because meeting in it leads to disorder; in other matters, opinion is not needed. Unlike the specified case.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling that a joint agent (wakil ma'a akhar) may perform certain acts alone without the consent of the co-agent. The author explains three such acts: returning a deposit (radd al-wadi'a), paying a debt (qada' al-dayn), and pronouncing divorce or manumission without receiving compensation (talaq wa 'itq bila 'iwad). The underlying reason (illah) for allowing independent action is that these acts do not require the exercise of opinion (ra'y); they are purely expressive or administrative. The commentary clarifies that 'returning a deposit' includes returning any entrusted item (like a loaned or usurped item), and the restriction to 'returning' excludes 'reclaiming' (istirdad), which is not allowed because the co-agent's presence is feasible and the principal has a valid interest in having two people for preservation. If one agent reclaims a deposit, he is liable for the entire value because he acted without the owner's permission; he cannot claim he was commanded to take half. Similarly, paying a debt does not require judgment, so one agent may do it alone. For divorce and manumission without compensation, it is pure verbal expression, so one agent may act independently; the condition 'without compensation' is crucial because if compensation is involved, opinion is needed and both agents must act jointly.

Key Terms
الرأي (al-ra'y): Legal judgment or discretion requiring consultation or deliberation.
تعْبِير مَحْض (ta'bir mahd): Pure verbal expression, an act that does not require opinion or ijtihad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is it permissible for one joint agent to return a deposit without the other's presence?
A) Because returning a deposit is a binding obligation.
B) Because it does not require the exercise of opinion (ra'y).
C) Because the principal can always revoke the deposit.
D) Because the co-agent is considered absent.

Key Takeaway
The distinguishing factor for joint agency independence is whether the act requires legal judgment (ra'y); if not, one agent may act alone.

Sub-Agency by Joint Agent and Conditional Talaq
Rule on joint agent appointing sub-agent and conditional divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a joint agent may appoint a sub-agent and explain why joint action is required for talaq or manumission when the principal's wording implies joint delegation.

Core Idea
A joint agent cannot delegate or enforce talaq alone without explicit permission or joint instruction.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the Hanafi position on two related issues: when a joint agent can appoint a sub-agent, and when joint action is required for divorce or manumission. The key principle is that actions requiring personal judgment (ra'y) by the agents demand their collective participation unless the principal has explicitly permitted individual action.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default rule for sub-agency
A joint agent cannot appoint a sub-agent unless the principal gives permission or says 'act on your opinion' (i'mal bi-ra'yik). Understand that this is a condition for validity.
The rule protects the principal's interest: the sub-agent becomes an agent of the principal, not of the first agent.
2
Analyze the consequences of valid vs. invalid appointment
If appointed with permission, the sub-agent is not affected by the first agent's dismissal or death; both are dismissed only by the principal's death. Without permission, the sub-agent's contract is suspended (mawquf) until ratification by the principal.
3
Recognize wording that requires joint action in talaq
If the principal says 'divorce her if you both wish' or 'her affair is in your hands' (tafwid), or 'divorce her both together', the agents must act jointly. The passage stresses that one agent alone cannot effect a divorce.
The same applies to manumission when the slave is unspecified.
4
Understand the rationale: need for opinion (ra'y)
These actions require deliberation (e.g., which slave to free, how many divorces). Hence the law requires collective decision-making unless the principal has delegated individual authority.
The text cites al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: 'because it requires opinion'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا توكيل الوكيل إلا بإذن آمرِهِ أو بقوله له : اعمل برأيك، فإن وكل بإذنِهِ كان الثاني وكيل الموكل الأولِ لا الثَّاني، فلا ينعزل بعزله أو بموته، وينعزلان بموت الأول ، وإن وكل بلا إذنِهِ فعقدُ الثَّاني عندَ الأول، أو بغيبته وأجاز هو، أو كانَ قَدَّرَ الثَّمن سواء. أما إذا قال: طلقاها إن شئتما ، أو جعل أمرها بأيديهما، فحينئذ يكون تفويضاً أو تعليقاً، فيشترط فعلهما لوقوع الطلاق. وكذا لو قال: طلّقاها جميعاً، ليس لأحدهما أن يطلقها وحده ولا يقع عليهما طلاق واحد، ولو قال طلّقاها جميعا ثلاثاً، فطلقها أحدهما طلقة واحدة، والآخر طلقتين لا يقع ، والمراد بالطلاق طلاق واحدة معيّنة. وكذا المراد بالعتق عتق عبد معين، فإنّه لو وكلهما بطلاق واحدة بغير عينها أو عتق عبد بغير عينه لا ينفرد أحدهما. كما في «السراج الوهاج» ؛ لأنه مما يحتاج إلى الرأي
And a joint agent may not appoint a sub-agent except with the permission of his principal, or if the principal says to him: 'Act on your opinion.' If he appoints with permission, the second agent becomes the agent of the first principal, not of the second, so he is not dismissed by the dismissal or death of the second, but both are dismissed by the death of the first. If he appoints without permission, then the contract of the second is pending on the ratification of the first, or if the first is absent and he ratifies, or if the price was specified, it is valid. But if he says: 'Divorce her if you both wish,' or places her affair in their hands, then it is a delegation or a conditional matter, so their joint action is required for divorce to occur. Similarly, if he says: 'Divorce her both together,' it is not permissible for one of them to divorce her alone, nor does a single divorce occur from both. And if he says: 'Divorce her together three times,' and one divorces once and the other twice, it does not occur. The intended meaning of divorce is one specified divorce. Similarly, the intended meaning of manumission is the manumission of a specified slave. If he appoints them to divorce one unspecified divorce or to manumit an unspecified slave, one of them may not act alone, as stated in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj, because it requires opinion.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage explains the rules for a joint agent appointing a sub-agent (tawkeel al-wakeel) and special cases of talaq and itq. The general rule is that a joint agent cannot appoint a sub-agent without explicit permission from the principal, or unless the principal said 'act on your opinion.' If permission is given, the sub-agent becomes an agent of the principal, not of the first agent, so he is unaffected by the first agent's dismissal or death; both are dismissed only by the principal's death. Without permission, the sub-agent's contract is suspended until ratification by the principal. The passage then addresses talaq and itq when the principal's wording implies joint action. If the principal says 'divorce her if you both wish' or 'her affair is in your hands', both agents must act together for talaq to occur. Similarly, if he says 'divorce her both together', one agent alone cannot divorce, and if they attempt a combined divorce (one pronouncing one talaq, the other two), it is invalid because the intended talaq is a single specified divorce. For manumission, the slave must be specified; if unspecified, one agent cannot act alone. The common thread is that these actions require opinion (ra'y) because the agents must deliberate on the specifics (which spouse or slave, how many divorces), hence both must participate.

Key Terms
توكيل الوكيل (tawkeel al-wakeel): An agent appointing a sub-agent.
تفويض (tafwid): Delegation of authority to both agents jointly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if two joint agents are appointed for divorce and the principal says 'divorce her both together', but one agent divorces alone?
A) The divorce is valid because one agent can act alone.
B) The divorce is invalid and no divorce occurs.
C) The divorce is valid only if the other agent agrees afterward.
D) The divorce is valid but incomplete.

Key Takeaway
Joint agents must act together when the principal's wording implies joint delegation; unilateral actions are invalid.

Agent for Litigation and Power of Possession: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling that an agent for litigation has authority to take possession, with intra-Hanafi dispute and analogy to agent for demanding debt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on whether a litigation agent may take possession, explain the principle 'man malaka shay'an malaka itmamah', and apply the later fatwa based on custom.

Core Idea
An agent for litigation may take possession according to the three imams, but later fatwa denies it due to custom.

This passage addresses a key question in agency law: does an agent appointed for litigation automatically have the right to collect the claimed property? Within the Hanafi school, the three principal imams answer yes, based on a logical principle of completeness, while Zufar disagrees. The discussion also compares this to an agent for demanding a debt (taqadi), and reveals how later jurists overturned the earlier ruling in light of prevailing custom. Understanding this dispute clarifies how Hanafi fiqh balances textual principles with practical custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and disagreement
Note that the text first states that according to the three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad), the agent for litigation has the power of possession, contrary to Zufar.
2
Examine the Hanafi proof (the three)
The reasoning is the principle: 'Whoever owns a thing owns its completion' (man malaka shay'an malaka itmamah). Since litigation aims at obtaining property, its completion is taking possession.
This is a qiyas based on the logical link between litigation and possession.
3
Understand Zufar's opposing view
Zufar argues that the principal only consented to litigation, not to taking possession, which is a separate act requiring separate authorization.
Note that other madhhabs (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) agree with Zufar.
4
Analyse the analogy to agent for debt demand
The text extends the same ruling to an agent for demanding debt (taqadi). Originally, taqadi included taking possession, but custom changed its meaning to only demand.
This analogy shows consistency in the school's logic.
5
Apply the later fatwa and the role of custom
The text states 'wa yufata bi 'adami qabdihima al-an' (fatwa is now given against their possessing). Custom ('urf) overrides the linguistic meaning, and by extension the earlier ruling, because people now understand taqadi as mere demand.
This demonstrates the Hanafi principle that custom can override qiyas when it becomes prevalent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الوكالة بالخصومة والقبض للوكيل بالخصومةِ القبضُ عندَ الثَّلاثةِ ) : أي عند أبي حنيفة، وأبي يوسف، ومحمد ، خلافا لزفر ، كالوكيل بالتقاضي في ظاهر الرواية ويفتي بعدم قبضهما الآن، فإنَّ الوكيل بالتقاضي يملك القبض في ظاهر المذهب [١] قوله : باب الوكالة بالخصومة والقبض ؛ إنما أخّر بحث الوكالة بالخصومة عن بحث الوكالة بالبيع والشراء ؛ لأنَّ الخصومة تقع باعتبار ما يجب استيفاؤه من هو في ذمته، وذلك لا يكون في الأغلب إلا بمطالبة المبيع ردَّ الثمن، أو لأنَّ الخصومة مهجورة شرعاً ؛ فلذا أخر عمَّا ليس بمهجور شرعاً، والخصومة هي الدعوى الصحيحة، أو الجواب الصريح بنعم أو لا، وقد تقدم ذكره، فتذكر. [٢] قوله : عند الثلاثة ؛ وجه قولهم : إنَّ من ملك شيئاً ملك إتمامه، وإتمام الخصومة وانتهاؤها لا يكون إلا بالقبض. [٣] قوله : أي عند ... الخ ؛ أشار إلى أنَّ المراد بالثلاثةِ أئمتنا الثلاثة ، لا لا الشافعي ومالك وأحمد ، فإنّهم يوافقون قول زفر . [٤] قوله : خلافاً لزفر الله ؛ فإنّه يقول : إنَّ الوكيل بالخصومة لا يكون وكيلاً بالقبض ؛ لأنَّ الموكَّل إنما رضي بخصومته، والقبض غير الخصومة ولم يرض به الموكل. [٥] قوله : كالوكيل بالتقاضي ؛ أي كما أن للوكيل بالخصومة القبض، كذلك للوكيل بالتقاضي القبض، والمراد بالتقاضي هو طلب الدين من المديون، وهذا على أصل الرواية. ووجهه : إنَّ التقاضي في معنى القبض ، قال في منتهى الأرب»: تقاضي: وام بازخواستن دوام بازگرفتن يقال : تقاضاه الدين؛ أي قبضه. انتهى. وهكذا في «القاموس» (١) ، وغيره، إلا أنَّ العرف بخلافه، فإنّه يراد به المطالبة، والعرف راجح على الوضع ؛ لأنَّ الألفاظ إنّما وضعت لحاجة الناس، وهم لا يفهمون المعنى الذي وضعت به.
Chapter of Agency for Litigation and Possession. For the agent for litigation, there is possession according to the three (Imams): Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad, contrary to Zufar. Like the agent for demanding debt in the apparent narration, and fatwa is now given against their possessing. The agent for demanding debt possesses the power of possession in the apparent madhhab. [1] His statement: 'Chapter of Agency for Litigation and Possession'; he delayed the discussion of agency for litigation after the discussion of agency for sale and purchase because litigation occurs regarding what is owed by someone, and that is usually only by demanding the price of the sale, or because litigation is disliked (mahjur) legally; therefore he delayed it after what is not disliked legally. Litigation is the valid claim or the explicit answer with yes or no, and it has been mentioned before. [2] His statement: 'according to the three'; the rationale of their statement is that whoever owns something owns its completion, and the completion and conclusion of litigation is only by possession. [3] His statement: 'i.e., according to... etc.'; he indicated that by 'the three' are meant our three imams, not al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad, for they agree with Zufar's statement. [4] His statement: 'contrary to Zufar'; he says that the agent for litigation does not become an agent for possession because the principal only consented to his litigation, and possession is other than litigation and the principal did not consent to it. [5] His statement: 'like the agent for demanding debt'; i.e., just as the agent for litigation has possession, so does the agent for demanding debt, and what is meant by demanding debt (taqadi) is requesting the debt from the debtor. This is according to the original narration. Its rationale is that taqadi is in the meaning of qabd. He said in 'Muntaha al-Arab': Taqadi: seeking, demanding, and taking. It is said: taqadahu al-dayn, i.e., he took it. End. And similarly in al-Qamus etc., except that custom ('urf) is otherwise, for it means demand, and custom takes precedence over linguistic assignment because words are only placed for people's need, and they do not understand the meaning for which they were placed.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage discusses a dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the powers of an agent for litigation (wakil bi al-khusuma). The three leading imams—Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani—hold that the agent for litigation also has the authority to take possession (qabd) of what is claimed. Their reasoning is based on the principle: 'whoever owns a thing owns its completion' (man malaka shay'an malaka itmamah). Since litigation is aimed at obtaining what is due, and the full completion of that is taking possession, the agent must be allowed to do so. Zufar, another Hanafi scholar, disagrees, arguing that the principal only consented to the agent's role in litigation, not in taking possession, which is a separate act. The text also compares this to an agent for demanding debt (wakil bi al-taqadi). According to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya), such an agent also has the power of possession, because the term taqadi originally meant both demand and taking. However, the text notes that the fatwa is now against giving possession to either agent, because custom ('urf) has changed the meaning of taqadi to only mean demand, and custom overrides the original linguistic assignment. This shows how later Hanafi jurists modified rulings based on changing customs. The passage also clarifies that 'the three' refers to the Hanafi imams only, not the founders of other schools, who actually agree with Zufar's position.

Key Terms
وكيل بالخصومة (wakil bi al-khusuma): An agent appointed to represent someone in litigation (legal claim or defense).
تقاضي (taqadi): Originally meant demanding and taking a debt, but custom changed it to mean only demanding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later fatwa (mufta bihi), does an agent for litigation (wakil bi al-khusuma) have the right to take possession (qabd) of the claimed property?
A) Yes, because he owns the completion of litigation.
B) No, because custom changed the meaning of taqadi and the fatwa now denies it.
C) Yes, according to all Hanafi scholars.
D) No, because Zufar's view is stronger.

Key Takeaway
The later Hanafi fatwa denies possession to litigation and debt-demand agents, overriding the original qiyas with custom ('urf).

Agent for Litigation: Collection Authority Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on agent collection authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on whether an agent for litigation has collection authority, identify Zufar's objection, and apply the fatwa change based on custom and dishonesty.

Core Idea
An agent for litigation collects by default; custom and dishonesty now change the fatwa.

In this lesson, we examine an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the scope of authority of an agent appointed for litigation (wakil bi al-khusumah). The three Imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) held that such an agent automatically has the power to collect the debt, while Zufar disagreed. We will explore the rationale and how the fatwa later shifted due to custom and prevalent dishonesty.

Guided Path
1
State the positions
Identify the ruling according to the three Imams and according to Zufar. Note that Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali agree with Zufar.
2
Understand the proof of the three Imams
The three Imams reason that whoever owns something owns its completion. Collection is the completion of litigation. So the agent for litigation necessarily has collection authority.
This is a qiyas based on the principle that the power to do the main act includes the power to complete it.
3
Analyze Zufar's objection
Zufar argues that the principal only consented to litigation, not collection. Collection is a separate act requiring separate authorization.
Zufar applies strict interpretation of the agency contract's scope.
4
Evaluate the fatwa change and custom
The passage explains that custom (urf) now interprets the term 'taqadi' as mere demand, not collection. Additionally, due to widespread dishonesty among agents, the fatwa is that neither the agent for litigation nor the agent for demanding payment has collection authority. The fatwa may vary based on local custom among merchants.
This shows how custom and practical circumstances can override the default ruling in Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الوكالة بالخصومة والقبض
للوكيل بالخصومة القبض عندَ الثَّلاثةِ : كالوكيل بالتقاضي في ظاهر الرواية ويفتى بعدم قبضهما الآن
(للوكيل بالخصومةِ القبضُ عندَ الثَّلاثةِ ) : أي عند أبي حنيفة، وأبي يوسف، ومحمد ، خلافا لزفر ، كالوكيل بالتقاضي في ظاهر الرواية ويفتي بعدم قبضهما الآن، فإنَّ الوكيل بالتقاضي يملك القبض في ظاهر المذهب قوله : باب الوكالة بالخصومة والقبض ؛ إنما أخّر بحث الوكالة بالخصومة عن بحث الوكالة بالبيع والشراء ؛ لأنَّ الخصومة تقع باعتبار ما يجب استيفاؤه من هو في ذمته، وذلك لا يكون في الأغلب إلا بمطالبة المبيع ردَّ الثمن، أو لأنَّ الخصومة مهجورة شرعاً ؛ فلذا أخر عمَّا ليس بمهجور شرعاً، والخصومة هي الدعوى الصحيحة، أو الجواب الصريح بنعم أو لا، وقد تقدم ذكره، فتذكر. قوله : عند الثلاثة ؛ وجه قولهم : إنَّ من ملك شيئاً ملك إتمامه، وإتمام الخصومة وانتهاؤها لا يكون إلا بالقبض. قوله : أي عند ... الخ ؛ أشار إلى أنَّ المراد بالثلاثةِ أئمتنا الثلاثة ، لا لا الشافعي ومالك وأحمد ، فإنّهم يوافقون قول زفر . قوله : خلافاً لزفر الله ؛ فإنّه يقول : إنَّ الوكيل بالخصومة لا يكون وكيلاً بالقبض ؛ لأنَّ الموكَّل إنما رضي بخصومته، والقبض غير الخصومة ولم يرض به الموكل. قوله : كالوكيل بالتقاضي ؛ أي كما أن للوكيل بالخصومة القبض، كذلك للوكيل بالتقاضي القبض، والمراد بالتقاضي هو طلب الدين من المديون، وهذا على أصل الرواية. ووجهه : إنَّ التقاضي في معنى القبض ، قال في منتهى الأرب: تقاضي: وام بازخواستن دوام بازگرفتن يقال : تقاضاه الدين؛ أي قبضه. انتهى. وهكذا في «القاموس» ، وغيره، إلا أنَّ العرف بخلافه، فإنّه يراد به المطالبة، والعرف راجح على الوضع ؛ لأنَّ الألفاظ إنّما وضعت لحاجة الناس، وهم لا يفهمون المعنى الذي وضعت له الألفاظ، بل يفهمون المجاز ، فصار المجاز بمنزلة الحقيقة العرفية. لكنَّ الفتوى في هذا الزَّمان على أنَّ الوكيل بالخصومة والوكيل بالقاضي لا يملكان القبض ؛ لظهور الخيانة في الوكلاء. قوله : لظهور الخيانة في الوكلاء ؛ وقد يؤتمن على الخصومة من لا يؤتمن على المال، وفي «البحر» نقلاً عن «الفتاوى الصغرى»: التوكيل بالتقاضي يعتمد العرف إن كان في بلد كان العرفُ بين التجار أنَّ المتقاضي الذي يقبضُ الدين، كان التوكيل بالتقاضي توكيلاً بالقبض وإلا فلا ، ذكره عن الفضل. انتهى. وفي «الفتاوى السراجية»: الوكيلُ بالتقاضي وكيل بالقبض في ظاهر الرواية ، والفتوى على أنه ينظر إن كان التوكيلُ بذلك في بلد كان العرف بين التجار أنَّ المتقاضي هو الذي يقبضُ الدين كان توكيلاً بالقبض، وإلا فلا. انتهى.
Chapter of Agency in Litigation and Collection
For the agent in litigation, he has collection according to the three: like the agent in demanding payment in the apparent narration, and a fatwa is given now that neither has collection.
(For the agent in litigation, collection is affirmed according to the three): meaning according to Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad, contrary to Zufar, like the agent in demanding payment in the apparent narration, and a fatwa is given now that neither has collection, for the agent in demanding payment does not own collection in the apparent madhhab. He said: Chapter of agency in litigation and collection; indeed, he delayed the discussion of agency in litigation after the discussion of agency in sale and purchase because litigation occurs by virtue of what must be exacted from what is in one's liability, and that usually does not happen except with demanding the return of the price, or because litigation is legally abhorrent; therefore, he delayed it over what is not legally abhorrent, and litigation is the valid claim or the explicit answer of yes or no, and it has been mentioned previously, so recall. He said: According to the three; the proof of their statement: Whoever owns something owns its completion, and the completion and end of litigation cannot be except through collection. He said: Meaning according to ... etc.; he indicated that by 'the three' is meant our three imams, not al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad, for they agree with Zufar's statement. He said: Contrary to Zufar; for he says: The agent in litigation is not an agent for collection because the principal only consented to his litigation, and collection is not litigation, and the principal did not consent to it. He said: Like the agent in demanding payment; i.e., just as the agent in litigation has collection, so too the agent in demanding payment has collection, and by demanding payment is meant requesting the debt from the debtor, and this is according to the foundation of the narration. Its proof: Demanding payment is in the sense of collection. He said in Muntaha al-Arab: Taqadi: meaning to ask for and take back; it is said: taqadahu al-dayn, i.e., he collected it. End. And similarly in al-Qamus and others, except that custom is contrary to that, for by it is meant demanding, and custom is preponderant over linguistic usage because words are only established for people's need, and they do not understand the meaning for which the words were established, but they understand the metaphorical meaning, so the metaphor becomes like a customary reality. But the fatwa in this time is that the agent in litigation and the agent for litigation do not have collection because of the prevalence of dishonesty among agents. He said: Because of the prevalence of dishonesty among agents; and sometimes entrusted with litigation is one who is not entrusted with wealth. In al-Bahr, quoting from al-Fatawa al-Sughra: Agency in demanding payment depends on custom: if it was in a city where the custom among merchants was that the demander of payment is the one who collects the debt, then the agency in demanding payment is agency in collection; otherwise no. He mentioned it from al-Fadl. End. And in al-Fatawa al-Sirajiyyah: The agent in demanding payment is an agent for collection in the apparent narration, and the fatwa is that it is considered: if the agency for that in a city where the custom among merchants was that the demander of payment is the one who collects the debt, then it is agency in collection; otherwise no. End.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with intra-Hanafi dispute. It states that an agent appointed specifically for litigation (khisumah) is also authorized to collect the debt according to the three Hanafi imams: Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad. Their proof is that whoever owns something owns its completion, and completion of litigation is collection. Zufar disagreed, arguing that the principal only consented to litigation, not collection. The passage then compares this to an agent appointed for demanding payment (taqadi), noting that in Zahir al-Riwayah the agent for demanding payment also has collection power. However, the passage notes that custom (urf) now interprets taqadi as mere demand, not collection, and custom overrides linguistic origin. Therefore, the fatwa in this time is that neither the agent for litigation nor the agent for demanding payment has collection power due to widespread dishonesty among agents. Contemporary fatwa depends on local custom: if merchants in a city customarily treat the demander as the collector, then the agency includes collection; otherwise not.

Key Terms
الوكيل بالخصومة (al-wakil bi-al-khusumah): An agent appointed to handle litigation on behalf of the principal.
القبض (al-qabd): Taking possession of the debt or property from the debtor.
التقاضي (al-taqadi): Requesting payment of a debt; in this text, it is debated whether it includes collection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes the fatwa change regarding an agent for litigation's collection authority?
A) The fatwa now affirms collection authority because of the completion principle.
B) The fatwa now denies collection authority due to custom and dishonesty.
C) The fatwa now follows Zufar's view exclusively.
D) The fatwa now grants collection authority only if the agent is also a lawyer.

Key Takeaway
An agent for litigation has default collection authority per the three Imams, but custom and dishonesty override this in contemporary fatwa.

Agent for Collection and Litigation Authority
States ruling on agent for collection also having litigation authority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the agent's authority to litigate, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of the dispute.

Core Idea
An agent for collection may or may not have litigation authority depending on which Hanafi master you follow.

When a principal appoints an agent to collect a debt, does that appointment automatically include the right to sue the debtor? Imam Abu Hanifa said yes; his two foremost disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, said no. This lesson unpacks the reasoning on both sides and shows why the distinction matters in practice, especially when the debtor claims the debt has already been settled.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested ruling
State the issue precisely: Does an agent appointed to collect a debt also have the authority to litigate (i.e., sue the debtor)?
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, meaning all parties are within the same school.
2
State Imam's position and proof
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the agent does have litigation authority. His proof: the principal appointed him to collect the debt in order to possess a substitute for the debt; since the debt itself cannot be physically taken, the agent must be able to litigate to effect transfer of ownership.
Imam's argument is based on the necessity of litigation for effective collection.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the agent does NOT have litigation authority. Their proof: collection and litigation are distinct activities; not everyone capable of collection is capable of litigation, so the principal's consent to litigation cannot be presumed.
They also argue that a narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad supports their view.
4
Note the third narration
Hasan ibn Ziyad's narration agrees with the Sahibayn, reinforcing the view that the agent does not have litigation authority by default.
This is a nawadir transmission.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence appears when the debtor brings evidence that the principal had already collected the debt or released the debtor. If the agent had litigation authority, such evidence may be admissible to dismiss the suit; if not, the agent's authority to pursue the case may be questioned.
The exact ruling depends on whether the agent's authority is considered broad enough to handle such defenses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وللوكيل ... الخ ؛ يعني أنَّ الوكيل بقبض الـديـن يملك الخصومة قبل القبض عند الإمام، وأمّا عندهما لا يملك الخصومة، وهو قول الأئمة الثلاثة، ورواية الحسنه عن الإمام. وجه قولهما : إنَّ القبض غير الخصومة، فلا يكون الوكيل بالقبض وكيلاً بالخصومة، وأيضاً ليس كلُّ من يصلح للقبض يعرفُ الخصومة، ويهتدي إلى المحاكمة، فلا يحصلُ الرِّضاء من الموكل. ووجه قول الإمام : إِنَّ الموكَّلَ وكَّل بقبض الدين بتملك المقبوض بما في ذمَّةِ المديون ؛ لأنَّ قبض نفس الدين لا يتصوّر ؛ ولذا قلنا : إنَّ الديون تقضى بأمثالها ، فإنَّ المقبوض ملك المطلوب حقيقة، وبالقبض يتملك بدلاً عن الدين، فيكون وكيلاً في حق التمليك، وذلك لا يكون إلا بالخصومة. وثمرة الخلاف تظهرُ فيما إذا أقام الخصم البينة على استيفاء الموكل، أو إبرائه،
He said: 'Walilwakil... etc.'; meaning that the agent for collection of the debt has the right to litigate before collection according to Imam, but according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he does not have litigation authority, and this is the statement of the three imams (i.e., Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar?) and a narration of Hasan from Imam. The proof of their statement: Collection is not litigation, so the agent for collection is not an agent for litigation; also, not everyone fit for collection knows litigation and can conduct court proceedings, so the principal's consent is not achieved. The proof of Imam's statement: The principal appointed him for collection of the debt to possess the collected property in lieu of what is in the debtor's liability, because collection of the debt itself is inconceivable; hence we say that debts are settled by their equivalents, for the collected property is actually the property of the debtor, and by collection it becomes possessed as a substitute for the debt, so he becomes an agent in the right of ownership, and that cannot be except through litigation. The fruit of the dispute appears when the opponent brings proof that the principal has already collected or released the debtor, ...
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the reverse scenario: if someone is appointed as agent to collect a debt, does that include the authority to litigate? Imam Abu Hanifa says yes, because the agent cannot effectively collect without the power to sue if necessary. The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that collection and litigation are distinct activities, and not everyone capable of collection is capable of litigation, so the principal's consent to litigation cannot be presumed. A narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad agrees with the two. The practical consequence (thamarah) of this difference arises when the debtor claims the principal had already collected or released him—the ruling on the admissibility of that evidence may differ depending on whether the agent had litigation authority.

Key Terms
الوكيل بقبض الدين (al-wakil bi-qabd al-dayn): An agent appointed to collect a debt from the debtor.
ثمرة الخلاف (thamarat al-khilaf): The practical consequence of a legal disagreement; what actually changes in application.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), why does an agent for collection NOT have the authority to litigate?
A) Because the principal would never intend to give such authority.
B) Because collection and litigation are different skills; not everyone fit for collection can litigate, so consent is not presumed.
C) Because litigation is not mentioned in the Quran or Sunnah.
D) Because the agent must be a scholar to litigate.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the authority of a debt-collection agent to litigate is disputed: Imam Abu Hanifa includes it; the Sahibayn (and Hasan ibn Ziyad) exclude it, and the practical consequence appears when the debtor presents evidence of prior settlement.
